LITERARY NOTES.
Louis Becke’s new book, “A First Fleet
Family,” in which he collaborated with
Walter Jeffery, of Sydney Evening News,
should reach Australia this week, and is
being published simultaneously in Europe
and America.
* * * *
The jealous U.S. copyright-law requires
that a book must be locally printed and
issued before, or at the same time as, the
first edition is published elsewhere ; so tha
there can be no convenient transfer of copie
or stereo-plates. Ward, Lock, and Bowdei
incurred the fresh expenditure for “ Sevei
Little Australians,” but so far the book ha
failed to catch on with Uncle Sam’s sma]
people. As some amends, it has been trans
lated into Danish and Dutch.
* * * *
The title of Ethel Turner’s book of shor;
yarns, to reach Australia next month, ha
been changed to “The Little Duchess anc
Other Stories.” “The Little Duchess” i
the heartless young lady who married—
wasn’t it the shopwalker I —in The Bulletin
a while back. Mrs. Curlewis has lately be
come a leading contributor to The Windsor
and that magazine’s July number will con
tain one of her stories; another by “Ada
Cambridge ” (of Melbourne) ; and a third by
Guy Bootliby (whose recent “ Beautiful
White Devil,” by the way, is shocking stuff
in the way of literature, however it may suit
the public that buys).
* * * *
Cassell and Co. paid 8| per cent, for *95.
* * # *
The Bookman prints a portrait of Alice
xMeynell, author of some clever verse and o:’
' ‘ The Rhythm 1 of Life a book of in-
genious essays. She looks painfully thin
and a low-cut dress shows a sad pair of salt-
cellars. The literary woman, if typically
feminine, takes a big risk when she starves
her body of blood to feed her brain. Only
a,typical women like George Eliot or Harriet
Martineau—practically males, with no debt
of motherhood to pay—can safely dedv the
sex’s physiological law.
* * * *
Publisher : “ You may send a book—a good
book—to one ‘ leading ’ (so it alleges) Aus-
tralian newspaper, and wait vainly for
comment in its literary column. Then you
may send to enquire ??.??? 4 Oh, yes,’ says
the editor, ‘we got that book—a dashed fine
book, too ; but you didn’t send any adver-
tisement. Of course, you know you’ll have
to send along an advertisement. We won’t
ask for a big one—say half-a-sov., and we’ll
give you a real good notice. Ta-ta ! Don’t
forget the ad.’ ”
* * * *
Olive Schreiner is moved to publish in the
Fortnightly a series of articles on the Boers
and South Africa written years ago. “ The
Story of an African Farm” has nearly reached
its 100th thousand. Its vitality is remark-
able. Though 10-years-old, it is asked for
every day at the booksellers’.
* * * *
When Agent-General Reeves left Maori-
land he thought he had given his country
at least one good thing in a School Reader
edited and printed in the province. The
Women’s Christian Temperance Unions have
told his successor at the Education bureau
“that to put the book in its present form
into the hands of the young will not tend to
their advancement intellectually or morally.
The language in which much of the book is
written is slangy and vulgar, and could not
fail to corrupt the purity of the English
tongue throughout the community. The
sentiments expressed in not a few of its
pages are such as, to lead to cruelty of dis-
position and indifference to the sufferings of
others.” This will shock Mr. Reeves.
* * * *
Nuggets from Nordau :
A man cannot beget both thoughts and children.
Originality is the first night of commonness.
There are very few Josephs outside the Bible,
and a Mrs. Potiphar is a phenomenon.
Gallantry is an invention of prosperity and
leisure.

but the discussion grows too personal for

you : the ad mis. plea does not meander
through that sheet of advertising opinions....
Stud. : “ Jubilee ” is spelt “ jubile ” in most
Bibles (Lev., xxv, 8-17), but “jubile” isn’t
necessarily correcter. The dies, give it from
Lat. jubilaeus, from Heb. yobel, the blast of
a trumpet Zeta : “ Daantjie kom hier te
orig,” alleged Boer for “Duncan Gray cam’
here to woo,” does look rough ; but the
“roughness” is in your own eye. How do
you think the original looks to the Boer ?
And just take a common English word—say,
“ kitchen ” —detach your mind from its mean-
ing, and see what an uncouth set of conso-
nants it is.

A Vivisectionist Romance.
i Apparently H. G. Wells has definitely
chosen his literary niche, and it is neither
very large nor very lofty. “The Island of
I>r. Moreau 1 is his fourth book : two longer
stories—“ The Time Machine” and “The
\\ onderful Visit ’ —and a book of short ones
—“ The Stolen Bacillus ” —preceded it. All
have the same qualities in varying propor-
tions—a scientific basis, singularly fresh and
vivid imagination, considerable literai’y dex-
terity,. and a delicate satirical vein. But they
want strength, depth, fundamental brain-
power. It is the age’s lack. We are prat-
tlers, not speakers.
Too fast we live, too much are tried,
Too harass’d, to attain
Wordsworth’s sweet calm, or Goethe’s wide
And luminous view to gain.
And Mr. Wells is invalid into the bargain.
Is it possible to create anything permanent
in literature unless one is prepared to squan-
der upon it a rich treasure of vitality ?
“ The Island of Dr. Moreau ” easily out-
does “Frankenstein,” though that is saying
little. It is singular how weakly Mary
Shelley’s romance reads for a work whose
title every member of Parliament is at liberty
to misapply. The conception is strong, but
the execution falls far short of the require-
ments of a generation which sorrowfully puts
its foot in Addison’s style-holes. Without
doubt century-beginning critics were easily
pleased in the matter of fiction. What
notice would “Waverley” win from an
average modern reviewer? “A rather
wearisome romance disfigured by numerous
pedantic asides, and rarely brightened by
graphic touches of character. A plodding
style heightens the reader’s tedium.” Would
not that be something like it ? And how r
would the average modern reader view
another slow-moving Scott in comparison
with the sturdy Doyle or the brisk, stirring
Weyman ?
* * * *
“ The Island of Dr. Moreau ” is the labora-
tory of another Frankenstein, with the dif-
erence that this Frankenstein doesn’t pestle
flesh and hair in a vague, unexplained way ;
but proceeds quite scientifically to vivisect,
and graft, and cultivate, until he has pro-
duced a population of semi-human mon-
strosities. “ Strange as it may seem to the
unscientific reader,” says the author, “ there
can be no denying that, whatever amount of
credibility attaches to the detail of this story,
the manufacture of monsters—and perhaps
even of quasi- human monsters—is within
the possibilities of vivisection.” And the eerie
theme is decidedly well handled. The veri-
similitude, with the consequent repulsion,
are better managed than in “Dr. Jekyll ” or
“Great God Pan,” Wells’s method being
more the detail-method practised by Defoe
in the “Journal of the Plague Year,” or Poe
in “ The Case of M. Valdemar.” The book
is undoubtedly clever, and creepy, and a fine
imaginative feat.
* * * •
Edward Prendick, more or-less naturalist,
finds himself a passenger in a vessel coming
from Arica (South America) with a puma,
a llama, some rabbits and staghounds, a man
called Montgomery, and a nondescript M’ling
with pointed ears tipped with fine black fur
at the edges. Willy-nilly, the drunken cap-
tain sets him ashore with this ocean men-
agerie at the island of Dr. Moreau—a massive,
white-haired man with “the squarest and
most resolute face. ” Driven from England
by the “insular prejudice” against vivisec-
tion, Dr. Moreau has found a little place
where he can experiment without let or
hindrance. Montgomery is his assistant, the
puma and staghounds his material, M’ling a
sample of his workmanship—a beast made
man. Gradually Prendick realises this. The
puma is fettered at once to the enthusiast’s
operating-bench, and yells with pain.
Prendick’s nerves give way, and he rushes
from Moreau’s stockaded enclosure. He is
introduced by an Ape-Man to the home of
the Beast People, where he learns their Law
—a set of conventions implanted in them by
Moreau.
And then began the insanest oeremony. The
voice in the dark began intoning a mad litany,
line by line, and I and the rest to repeat it. As
they did so, they swayed from side to side, and
beat their hands upon their knees, and I followed
their example. I could have imagined I was
already dead and in another world. The dark
hub, these grotesque dim figures, just flecked here
and there by a glimmer of light, and all of them
swaying in unison and chanting :
“Not to go on all-fours ; that is the Law. Are
we nob Men ? ”
“Not to suck up drink ; that is the Law. Are
we not Men ? ”
“Not to eat flesh or fish ; that is the Law. Are
we not Men ?”
“ Not to claw the bark of trees ; that is the Law.
Are we not Men ? ”
“Wot to chase other men; that is the Law. Are
we not Men ? ”
And so from the prohibition of these acts of folly,
on to prohibition of what I thought then were the
maddest, most impossible, and most indecent
things one could well imagine. A kind of
rhythmic fervor fell on all of us ; we gabbled and"
swayed faster and faster, repeating this amazing
law. Superficially the contagion of these brute
men was upon me, but deep down within me
laughter and disgust struggled together. We
ran through a long list of prohibitions, and then
the chant swung round to a new formula :
“ His is the House of Pain,
His is the Hand that makes,
His is the Hand that wounds,
His is the Hand that heals.”
And so on for another long series, mostly quite in-
comprehensible gibberish to me, about Him, who-
ever he might be. I could have fancied it was a
dream, but never before have I heard chanting in
a dream.
* » » *
Suddenly Moreau appears. Prendick
takes to his heels and runs to the sea, still
under the impression that these weird crea-
tures are men, like himself, whom Moreau
has brutalised—not brutes whom he has
humanised. He is sought and soothed,
and Hr. Moreau explains.
These creatures you have seen are animals carven
and wrought into new shapes. To that —to th
study of the plasticity of living forms—my lif
has been devoted. I have studied for years, gain
ing in knowledge as I go. I see you look horrified
and yet I am telling you nothing new. It al
lay in the surface of practical anatomy years ago
but no one had the temerity to touch it. It’s no
simply the outward form of an animal I can
change. . . . It is a possible thing to
transplant tissue from one part of an animal to
another, or from one animal to another, to alter
its chemical reactions and methods of growth, to
modify the articulations of its limbs, and indeec
to change it in its most intimate structure?
“But,” said I, “these things—these animals
talk ! ”
He said that was so, and proceeded to point oul
that the possibilities of vivisection do not stop al
a mere physical metamorphosis. A pig may be
educated. The mental structure is even less
determinate than the" bodily. In our growing
science of hypnotism we find the promise of a
possibility of replacing old inherent instincts by
new suggestions, grafting upon or replacing the
inherited fixed ideas. Very much, indeed, of
what we call moral education is such an artificial
modification and perversion of instinct; pugnacity
is trained into courageous self-sacrifice, and sup-
pressed sexuality into religious emotion.
* * * *
And in this company Prendick passes
many weeks. But by degrees the brutal
instincts of the Beast-People resume their
sway. At last there is a rebellion, which is
overcome ; but the puma wrenches its fetters
out of the wall of the operating-room and
escapes. Moreau pursues it and kills it, blkt
himself is killed in the struggle. Mont-
gomery is also beaten to death. Prendick at
last escapes in a derelict boat, and is picked
up by a passing vessel. Then he gives what
may be called the moral of the book in a
passage which makes one turn back to see if
the whole is not an allegory whose satire is
well hidden. For he says his return to
civilisation was accompanied by something
like mental disease :
My trouble took the strangest form. I could
not persuade myself that the men and women I
met were not also another, still passably human,
Beast-People, animals half-wrought into the out-
ward image of human souls, and that they would
not presently begin to revert, to show first this
bestial mark and then that. . .
When I lived in London the horror was well-
nigh insupportable. I could not get away from
men ; their voices came through windows; locked
doors were flimsy safeguards. I would go out
into the streets to fight with my delusion, and
prowling women would mew after me ; furtive,
craving men glance jealously at me; weary, pale
workers go coughing by me| with tired eyes and
eager pace, like wounded deer dripping blood ;
old people, bent and dull, pass murmuring to them-
selves, and all unheeding a ragged tail of gibing
children. Then I would turn aside into some
chapel, and even there, such was my disturbance,
it seemed that the preacher gibbered Big Thinks
even as the Ape Man had done ; or into some
library, and there the intent faces over the books
seemed but patient creatures waiting for prey.
The Devil’s Case.
Ishmaelite Robert Buchanan’s new poem,
“ The Devil’s Case,” has caused some London
stir. The Freethinker gives extracts from
the work. Mr. Buchanan says at the outset:
Please remember, Gentle Reader,
Not to judge me line by line ;
Tbo’ I try to state it clearly,
Tis the Devil’s Case, not mine !
Mr. Buchanan’s Devil is a benevolent philan-
thropist, profoundly sympathetic with human
suffering, whom the poet, when in distress
and with youth’s illusions gone, meets on
Hampstead Heath on the night after a Bank
Holiday. The Devil is in clerical attire and
reads the pink edition of the Star in the
moonlight. The “Devil’s Case” is the re-
sult of the interview. The Devil calls
attention to all the sufferings, disasters and
horrors in the paper, and professes himself
to be anxious for their alleviation :
If there is a God, He blundered ;
Man is here to set Him right.
Mr. Buchanan deems himself an efficient
interviewer, and even Devil’s advocate, since
he holds something in common with Nickie
Ben :
J, tlie Interviewer hated
Cordially by cliques and critics;
Hailed at in a hundred journals
As a Scotchman lost and lorn ;
He the Interviewed, for ages
Outlaw’d hy the cliques of Heaven,
\V no for over and for ever
801 l the Log and praise the Lord 1
* * t
fetill we keep as our possession
One thing even the angels envy—
lower to stand erect, while cravens
801 l the Log and bend the knee;
Power to feel and strength to suffer,
Will to fight for Heaven only,
Zeal to speak the truth within us
While the slaves of Heaven are dumb.
Naturally, the Devil appoints Mr. Buchanan
his Laureate, to justify his ways to mortals
ihe Devil has some grit in him. He shows
the poet all the kingdoms and the religious
Jfaders of the past, and professes to have
been the inspirer of all that has tended to
the welfare of humanity. This gives a gooc
opportunity for comment upon the faiths o
Greece, of Buddha, Zoroaster, Mahomet
etc., and to show the triumphs of intelligence
and democracy ascribed to the Devil. The
hero says :
I’m the father of all Science-
Master builder, stock improver,
First authority on drainage,
Most renowned in all the arts.
While the Priests have built their Churohe.
lo a God who does not heed them,
I have fashion’d decent dwellings
Public hospitals, and baths.
“ Take no heed about to-morrow ”
Said the Man-God ; “do not labor,
Be content with endless praying,
And eternal laissez-faire.” '
But the Devil, being wiser,
Knows that he who fails to reckon
With the morrow will discover
That To -morrow is To-day!
One wonders, if this is Mr. Buchanan’s
Devil, what sort of Deity he possesses. We
seem to get a glimpse in the fine Litany
which serves as epilogue to the volume, from
which one extract :
Thou hast set these Rulers above us, to bind us
to blind our eyes ;
dhou hast sent these Priests to lure us with creeds
and dogmas and lies ;
Thou hast built Thy Church on the sands still
shifting and tremulous.
From Churches and Priests and Liars, good Lord
deliver us.
The volume is full of vigorous heresy. The
poet sometimes puts in a feeble word for
orthodoxy, but the answer is usually crush-
ing. Thus, to the argument that Evolution
is working out the will of the Almighty
Father, conies the reply :
Say, can any latter blessing
Cleanse the blood-stained Book of Being?
Can a remnant rendered happy
Wipe out centuries of sorrow ?
The Devil’s advice is, therefore :
“ Waste not thought on the Almighty ;
Seek with all thy soul’s endeavour
How to make thine earthly dwelling
Bright and fair, in God’s despite.”
Mr. Buchanan may disclaim the Devil’s
teaching, but the Devil has a good deal of
reason on his side. The review of the past
gives Mr. Buchanan as fine opportunities as
the subject of the “Wandering Jew.” At
times he takes advantage of them. Take,
for instance, this on Voltaire:—
Diabolically smiling,
Up to Prince and Priest he strutted,
Tapped his snuff-box, and politely
Cracked his jokes at the Madonna !
Naught of holy reputation
’Scaped the ribald rascal’s laughter ;
Far away as Rome the Churches
Echoed with his jests profane.
Then, behold, a transformation !
Suddenly he rose, transfigured ;
Periwig and snuff-box vanished,
And an Angel stood revealed.
In his hand my sword of Freedom
Flashing on the eyes of Europe—
While the hounds of persecution
Paused, and Calas kissed his feet!

T ■ O O K 1
BEFORE YOU
f. E A F?
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE WALLABY TRACK.
Oh, a weird, wild road is the Wallaby Track
That is known to the bushmen only,
Stretching away to the plains out back
And the big scrubs lorn and lonely ;
Dawn till dark they are passing there,
Over the hot sand thronging,
Shouldering burdens of Doubt and Despair,
Passion and Love and Longing.
There are pearls of dew on the Wallaby Track
For the maiden Day’s adorning,
And blush-clouds beating the night-shades back
In the van of the golden morning;
There are glories born of the sinking sun
In the splendid eve’s lap dying,
A glitter of stars lit one by one,
And a rustle of night-wings flying.
There are long bright days on the Wallaby Track,
With a blue vault arching over,
And long, long thoughts that are drifting back
To the waiting wife and lover;
There are horse-bells tinkling down the wind
With a thousand rippling changes,
And the boom of the team-bells intertwined
From the far-off mulga ranges.
There are stars of gold on the Wallaby Track,
And silver the moonbeams glisten ;
The great Bush sings to us, out and back,
And we lie in her arms and listen ; .
Our dull hearts quicken their rhythmic beat,
For a wild swan’s southward flying,
And gather old memories sadly sweet
From a wind-swept pine-bough’s sighing.
There are lone graves left on the Wallaby Track,
And the bush-grass bends above them—
They had no white hands to wave them back,
Perhaps—no hearts to love them !
But none the less will their sleep be sound
For the Hope and the Love denied them ;
They will hear no tramp on the thirsty ground,
Though our path runs close beside them.
N.S.W.
GLENROWAN.
THE DEMOCRAT.
Who will come list to this song of the heart-life of
the Democrat, crying friendship,
Crying for the subjugation of things contrary to
all inherent and unrestrained expression of
liberty ?
Come on then !
We will tread this rough new ground together,
We will make war on all that opposes the humane.
Have you computed the peril—the failure of
leaders, promises, hopes, expectations—any-
thing ?
Come on then !
The prizes are the smiles, hand-grips, rough
touches, and words of approval of a brave
circle of hearts, never failing, eternally being
replaced;
The wage is the work —incessant, incessant work
amid the dumb fear and failing of friends ;
The defeat is nothing, either, yet will we have
our own supreme defiances.
We will acknowledge the worth of Literatures and
Sciences, while they hold us not subservient,
Nor assume to be incognisant of our existence;
nor deny us the right of this rough, uninter-
rupted expression;
We will acknowledge the power of Fame and
Ambition (for we must not deny anything).
Are you agreed ?
Come on then ! We will do our work ;
We will not be concerned about what is called
victory or defeat.
Vic.
J.E.M.
A NATIONAL HYMN.
Hail, North Queensland ! May a blessing
Rest upon thee evermore !
Though thy debts are rather pressing,
And thy creditors galore :
Heed them not. The true salvation
Of a rapid coastal boat
And a mighty hopeful nation
Lies in keeping things afloat.
Sweat and Sorrow,
Bung and Borrow,
Are thy war-cries in the field.
“ May the Devil take to-morrow ! ”
Is the motto on thy shield.
Hail, North Queensland ! Mining duffers
Crown thy famine-stricken hills,
Where the pensive hatter suffers
From his unpaid tucker-bills.
Where the fossicker is toiling
For a bit of low-priced tin,
Just to keep the billy boiling
For himself, his dog, and gin.
Lonely campers
Cook their dampers
Where the crowd their revels held,
When the thunder of the stampers
Through the rocky gorges yelled.
Hail, North Queensland ! Stock-diseases,
Drought and dingoes fill thy plains
From the one-horse coastal leases
Out to where it never rains ;
Where the salted horse is tougher
Than the thickest ’gator-hides,
And the festive cattle-duffer
Takes romantic moonshine rides.
Stock-congested,
Tick-infested,
Dismal, hungry, and so forth.
God made earth and sky and rested ;
Then the Devil made the North !
Hail, North Queensland ! Hail the climate !
Wonderful beyond compare;
It would make the solemn Primate
Of the Church of England swear.
Months of roasting inflammation,
Varied by a mighty burst,
Drowning half the population,
While the balance die of thirst.
Clear and hazy,
Cold and mazy,
Under 32 at night;
And at noon there, drive you crazy—
Just one-twenty Fahrenheit !
Hail ; but, no ; it ought to spell:
H-E-L-L !
Q.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
•Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable. MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in lllach and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C. ; Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgate Circus ; Street & Co., Cornhill.

SATURDAY, JUNE 6, 1896.
A Municipal Problem.
In New South Wales, though the present
Municipalities Act has been in force for
many years, local government has made but
little progress. Out of the 306,000 sq. miles
which make up the extent of the province,
only about 2800 sq. miles are under any form
of municipal administration, and the area in-
creases very slowly indeed. No less than
1993 sq. miles of this incorporated area are
situated in the eastern division of the pro-
vince ; west of the mountains municipal
government is almost unknown, and over
this vast space of country almost every drain
and ditch and culvert, every rut and hole and
stump and knob in the roadway, every foot-
path and stray dog and Bathurst burr and
dead goat, are supposed to be under the
direct aegis and personal supervision of the
Government at Sydney. But while N.S.
Wales is thus in one respect very much in
want of a system of local government, in
another direction it is locally-governed alto-
gether too much. Within a circle the cir-
cumference of which is nowhere more than
seven miles from Sydney Post Office there
are no less than 40 municipalities; and
within 20 miles of Sydney there are at
least 46. Most of them are small—some of
them miserably small, and their revenues
range from various sums down to almost
nothing. Also, their debts range from vari-
ous sums up to almost anything. There are
many stages of prospective bankruptcy among
them, and many varieties of incapacity, and,
taking them all round, they are dismally un-
satisfactory, and about the best thing to be
said for them is that their circumstances
might be a little worse—not very much worse
in some cases, yet a little.
* * * *
Within the narrow radius above-mentioned
there are 40 mayors and as many full-
blown corporations. Almost every muni-
cipality has its town-hall—there are nearly
£500,000 worth of assorted town-hall in
Sydney and its immediate surroundings.
This little district is bossed by 396 aldermen,
and, in addition, almost every municipality
has its own council-clerk, inspector of nuis-
ances, valuer, rate-collector, engineer, and
other functionaries. There are 35 separate
municipal debts, all within gunshot range
of Sydney Post Office tower, and 27 different
bank-overdrafts, and for the year ended
February, 1895, there were, out of 38 muni-
cipalities whose accounts were published, 25
deficiencies, five cases where the accounts
were squared by hard borrowing, and eight
surpluses. Almost every municipality has
its own internal feud among the aldermen,
its own special row with the municipality
next door, its own trouble about the night-
cart and where the contents thereof are to
be deposited, and its own separate and indi-
vidual difficulty about making the accounts
look moderately coherent. Each munici-
pality has its own auditors, who include
teachers, auctioneers, shopkeepers, butchers,
and all manner of other people whose ac-
quaintance with accounts is strictly limited.
The public lighting is various : two munici-
palities are lighted with electricity, one with
gas and electricity, 33 with gas, and four are
not lighted at all. One has its own electric-
lighting plant, while another is electric-
lighted by contract. The financial arrange-
ments are also various, the loans bearing
interest at from 4 up to 8 per cent. ; nearly
half the total loans carry over 5 per cent.,
and nearly one-quarter over 6 per cent.
Altogether, things are about as mixed as
they can be in the many towns of Greater
Sydney.
* * * *
The obvious impossibility of having 40
different -water and drainage schemes led,
some years ago, to the establishment of the
Metropolitan Board of Water Supply and
Sewerage, which bosses the city and fifteen
suburbs ; those which lie outside its jurisdic-
tion mostly dispense with any scientific
sewerage system, and leave the dead cat in
the gutter to be removed by the act of God.
This was about the first beginning of any
attempt at consolidation. The Metropolitan
Fire Brigade was also established because it
was found impossible for each municipality
to maintain its own brigade—which, in some
cases, would have absorbed practically the
entire revenue. The latest trouble caused
by the disjointed state of things arises over
the electric-lighting problem. The experi-
ence of many British cities has shown that
municipal lighting can be made both cheaper
and more successful than lighting by private
enterprise. A strong desire is growing up in
Sydney to make electric-lighting a municipal
undertaking ; the N.S. Wales metropolis has
had long experience of the blessings of
private enterprise as typified by the incubus
of the Australian Gas Light Co., and it has
no desire to repeat that awful experiment
and set up a new Old Man of the Sea
in place of the one now existing. But,
as matters stand, whereas a private syndi-
cate might get powers to light all the 40
municipalities of Sydney, no municipality
can get power to do any lighting outside its
own boundaries. Therefore, whereas a
syndicate might do the whole work with one
plant, the municipalities would require 40
separate plants, and as many engineers and
staffs of workmen to cover the same ground.
Therefore, the syndicate has so many advan-
tages that there is serious risk of it getting
a hold at least on the smaller municipalities.
And for this, as well as many other reasons,
The Bulletin submits that the present
policy is a grotesque failure ; and that the
whole municipal system should be consoli-
dated under a County Council, which would
municipally govern all the city and its sur-
roundings from Manly to Burwood, or
possibly to Parramatta, and from George’s
River to Narrabeen or thereabouts.
* * * *
The advantages of such a consolidation are
many and obvious. It would do away with
39 out of the 40 mayors—or, if the boun-
daries of Greater Sydney were extended to
the wide limits suggested above, it would
enable 45 out of 46 mayors to be dispensed
with. It would also enable about 300 out
of the 396 aldermen to be left out, and any
one who has seriously considered 396 aider-
men will understand the advantages im-
plied by such a change. To make a
thoroughly capable alderman many qualifi-
cations are required, and the impossibility of
finding in a limited population 396 available
men who possess them has been sufficiently
demonstrated. There is no honour attached
to the billet in a small outlying municipality ;
there is no money except it be come by dis-
honestly ; the business consists mainly in a
constant wrangle about the night-cart and
like subjects, and the whole affair is mean
and commonplace and unsatisfactory. A
County Council which controlled the whole
metropolitan area would, on the other hand,
be a powerful institution with some sem-
blance of dignity about it. It would secure
better men, partly by reason of the fact that
50 or 100 capable men are easier to find than
396, partly because it would offer a sphere
of operations large enough to attract able
administrators, which the present retail dust-
cart business does not. The great majority
of men only serve their country for money or
for distinction, and neither is to be obtained
in the small halfpenny politics of the outside
municipality. The County Council of Sydney
would at least offer a certain amount of dis-
tinction—not of the very highest class, per-
haps, yet distinction none the less.
* * * *
Further, it could not fail, even under very
second-rate management, to be vastly more
economical than the state of things now pre-
vailing. Its securities would be almost, if not
quite, as good as those of the N.S.W.
Government, and it could easily raise money
locally at 3i per cent., or possibly even less,
whereas the present average is about 5 per
Cent. On this single item it eould make a
saving of £30,000 a year merely by renewing
and consolidating the present disastrous
loans as they fall in. Out of the £34,000 a
year which goes in salaries to 40 different
sets of officials, it could save, probably*
£lO,OOO by the mere centralisation of the
work. Instead of the 26 separate bank
overdrafts, most of them bearing interest at
7 and 8 per cent., it could obtain temporary
accommodation, if necessary, on the same
terms as an embarrassed Treasurer by the
issue of 4 per cent, bills. It might possibly
succeed in finding a financial genius to con-
trol the city treasury, whereas the impossi-
bility of finding 40 separate financial geniuses
is too obvious to require explanation. It
should be able to establish a clean and
moderately sane system of dealing with the
city refuse on the dessication principle, which
would be cheaper than the many disjointed
expedients now prevailing, whereas no such
thing seems possible under existing condi-
tions because the muicipalities won’t combine
to do the work, and it is impossible for each
one to pay for its own dessicator, and, in
any case, the presence of 40 dessicators in
one city would be a circumstance too awful
for expression. Further, the establishment
of a County Council is the only visible means
whereby a complete system of municipal
lighting can be arrived at. Under present
conditions it would require 40 separate
systems to light the metropolitan district,
and the spectacle of 40 mayors and 396
alderman trying to introduce 40 systems on a
7 per cent, overdraft is beyond description.
* * * *
Sydney, taking it in its widest sense, is a
huge, misshapen, ramshackle, prematurely-
old city of no particular shape or pattern,
laid out nohow in particular, with narrow,
crooked streets, and a general air of
disreputableness. It might be vastly
improved and practically reconstructed
as Birmingham was under the Chamber-
lain regime, and as Paris was in the
Haussmann era, and as old Glasgow is being
reconstructed now by the City Improvement
Trust—if there was any authority strong
enough to attempt the work. But repre-
sentative bodies in general move slowly, and
40 of them can hardly be said to move at all,
and out of the deliberations of 396 aldermen
no connected results can be expected. This
is another respect in which consolidation
might do much good. And the County
Council, in addition to absorbing the multi-
tudinous councils and aldermen and mayors,
might also—by dividing itself into depart-
ments and sub-committees—do the work of
the Water and Sewerage Board, the Fire
Brigades Board, the Transit Commissioners,
and the City Improvement Board—which
practically burst-up in 1895, after an exist-
ence of 16 years, and now survives only in
name, and thereby make these institutions
more economical and more easily manage-
able than they now are. Also, by the same
process of consolidation there would be only
one municipal body to grovel to the incoming
Governor and read him an address, whereas
now the grovels and the addresses are legion ;
and the periodical free fight in the Council
Chamber would at least be less frequent, in-
asmuch as there would be 39, or perhaps 45,
less Councils for fighting purposes ; and a
lot of dreary “ municipal intelligence ” would
vanish out of the papers ; and many other
desirable things would happen which need
not be herein specified.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Fall of Bent.
The South Melbourne election is an event that
will require a lot of hard explanation in many
quarters. The Trades Hall, which the “res-
pectable ” organs have so long represented as
broken-down and discredited and powerless and
alaughing-stockfor its friends and enemies alike,
nominated Tucker, and, on the advanced demo-
cratic ticket and as a general supporter of the
Govt., he beat the other five candidates in a
canter. The democratic vote was split by three
other aspirants, yet Owen, the Tory nominee,
only came in a bad third. Champion was a bad
fourth. Bent, who represented the Freetrade
interest as far as he dared, was a woeful fifth,
and got 49 votes against Tucker’s 573 ; if there
was any Freetrade party in S. Melb., it was too
muchashamed of the paunchy boomer to acknow-
ledge him. South Melb. election was the
first chance that a crowd of democratic electors
have had of telling one of the dreadful boomers
what they thought of him, and the result seems
to have surprised the boomer badly. Bent
has been treated with some semblance of
respect by his fellow-speculators at Brigh-
ton, and he probably imagined that
Victoria in general rather liked him
than otherwise, and when he discovered, in
his own dull, bear-like fashion, that he was
politically regarded as partly hog and partly
clown, he was quite dazed. In all his progress
through the electorate he hardly met one con-
stituent who didn’t call him some opprobrious
epithet at sight and say, “ Chuck ’im out on ’is
’ead.” Yet it seemed to him so utterly impos-
sible that any constituency could fail to appre-
ciate Bent that in the early stages of the
contest he apparently regarded it all as a horrid
dream. So he plodded on his heavy, ox-like
way through the campaign, and his broad, flat
feet went on awful pilgrimages up and down
the dingy streets where everybody guffawed at
his pretensions, till at last the polling-day came,
and he found that really and truly the people
didn’t want Bent at any price. No other man,
with Bent’s record, would have attempted to face
a democratic constituency—just now, at all
events. It was a piece of foolishness which
only the most dunderheaded variety of clod
could have been capable of, and the one clod in
ail Victoria who was that particular kind of
clod was Bent. Davies or Munro or almost
any other of the disastrous boomers would have
picked his time and his place carefully, and
obtruded himself on some district which the
financial antics of the boom hadn’t struck quite
so heavily; But the Brighton pachyderm de-
liberately threw his 2cwt. of aggressive beef at
the head of one of the most democratic elec-
torates in Victoria, at a time when a wave of
democratic fervor is passing over the province
and before the odor of sundry past events has
fairly begun to die away ; and the result is one
of the most satisfactory events which has hap-
pened in the Cabbage-Garden for a very long
time. s
“ The Bulletin ” On a Fence.
The N.S.W. Assembly has so far decided in
favor of the proposal to provide for the widow
and young children of Henry Parkes out of
the public revenue that the preliminary resolu-
tion was carried by 55 votes. An aggravated
minority, representing 22 votes, opposed the
measure, and, in the course of the debate the
two parties wrangled furiously over the old
man’s grave. As for the question at issue, it
did not, in a logical sense, get much further
advanced. The case is about equally strong on
either side, and about equally mean-looking
remarks can be made on it either way. Parkes
served the province in Parliament for nearly 40
years, and in office for about 15 years, therefore
New' South Wales should be grateful. It is
questionable if the province wouldn’t have been
quite as well, or perhaps a good deal better,
without his services, taken in a lump, therefore
it is open to argument whether New South
Wales should be grateful or not. Anyhow, the
province paid him for his services, therefore, if
the payment was adequate, there is nothing due
to him, and if it was inadequate, there is some-
thing due —it is difficult to say how much.
Parkes died very, very poor. Possibly he did
so because he was a nobly disinterested man,
who refused to avail himself of his opportunities
as a politician of a lower stamp might have done,
in which case his nobility of character deserves
recognition. Possibly he did so because
he was utterly thriftless, and prodigal, and
careless about his own and other people’s
money, or because he had overreached'himself
in grabbing at too much of the earth, and in
these latter cases there is no obligation. If
Parkes’s children should be supported by the
State because he served the State, then the
claims of every man who did service to the
State, even if he did it with a pick and shovel,
also call for recognition. Parkes’s claim
shouldn’t be recognised because the State
doesn’t recognise similar claims in the cases of
all its servants. Parkes’s claim should
be recognised because the claim of all
public servants should be recognised, and the
recognition must begin somewhere, and it may
as well begin with Parkes as with anybody
else. The people elected Parkes to be their
ruler and fetish and little tin god oftener and
more persistently than they did any other man ;
therefore, having declared him by their votes
to be a great and able and patriotic statesman,
they should at least staud by his memory. On
the other hand, the new political generation
which is springing up is not responsible for the
face that the old generation which is fast pass-
ing away chose to hold Parkes in reverence.
Parkes served in Parliament for perhaps 25
years under the old regime when members
were unpaid, and the country owes him for
these gratis services. On the other hand, he
declared over and over again that he didn’t
want payment, so the country doesn’t owe him
anything under this head. And if it does owe
him anything on this account, it also owes, on
the same account, to all the surviving poli-
ticians of the same era, and as it can’t pay
them all it shouldn’t pay anybody. On the
other hand, as it should pay them all, it should
begin by paying somebody, and that somebody
might as well be Parkes. Also, it might as
well be anybody else than Parkes. If N.S.
Wales allowed the young children of its best-
known politician to drift into the Benevolent
Asylum it would be, to all unthinking people,
and to many thinking people, a dreadful
advertisement of the State’s ingratitude. And,
on the other hand, if it provided for Parkes’s
family and left the families of other men,
who had served the State quite as well
for less money and less notoriety, to shift for
themselves, it would seem to some more deeply-
intelligent people an equally bad case of in-
gratitude. In any case, to provide for Parkes’s
family for purposes of mere advertisement is a
mean and slavish act; and not to provide for
them for purposes of advertisement in such an
extreme case would probably be a supremely
foolish one ; and to provide for them and pro-
fess that it isn’t for purposes of advertisement
would, in the case of most legislators, be a lie
pure and simple. And, under all these circum-
stances —should the N.S. W. Parliament provide
for Parkes’s family at the public expense, or
not?
Mildura is not ChafFey Bros. Ltd.
Mr. N. B. M‘Kay writes from Mildura :
Dear Bulletin,— lt is a mistake to imply that, because
there has been a complete collapse of the company of
Chaffey Bros. Ltd., the Mildura enterprise has proved a
failure. So far as the personal gain of the original pro-
moters was concerned, perhaps the scheme might be so
regarded. They put in all they had, played for a big
stake, and lost. Whatever the Mildura of the future may
be, it is hardly possible that George and W. B. Chaffey
will derive any benefit or advantage from it. So that, in
discussing the condition and prospects of this settlement,
the promoters and their company can be ignored alto-
gether. It is unjust to the thousands who have invested
and settled here, to represent the Mildura enterprise as a
failure. It is nothing of the kind. The productiveness of
the soil under irrigation, and the suitableness of the
climate for profitable fruit culture have been abundantly
demonstrated. There are holdings here now yielding re-
turns of £3O to £5O per acre, and there ought to be
hundreds more doing as well. There is nothing seriously
the matter with Mildura. All the troubles, or almost all,
are attributable to human greed, folly, ignorance, or
neglect, and human effort can set things right again, bet
the place and its people have a fair show. The settlers
have put their own money into this concern, they have
cost the State less per head than any other community m
Australia, and they have contributed more largely to the
revenue of the country than any other section of its in-
habitants. Why should a venture, which has for its basis
all the essential elements of success, be coupled and, m
fact, absolutely identified with the misfortunes of a badly-
financed company ? Having- been over eight years here,
I can speak with some knowledge of the matter, and
I emphatically assert that Mildura might have been,
and that it ought yet to become, all that its pro-


moters predicted it would be. And just one re-
mark in conclusion. It is all very well to blame
G. and W. B. Chaffky for entering upon a scheme
like this without having all the capital which, in the light
of dearly - bought experience, it is claimed that they
should have had. But had they possessed £250,000, or
anything like that sum, they would never have troubled
about investing in Australia. Their scheme was a self-
developing one, and the main reason that their company
went to pieces was that they were not permitted to work
it out in their own way. Four years ago the control of
affairs was taken out of their hands (and it has to be said
that good reasons were shown at the time), the revenues
of their company were cut off, and they were unable to
continue the work they had begun so well. And after
more than eight years’ relations with them—relations in-
volving many transactions—l have to state that I have
found them personally upright and liberal, and, in fact,
anything but the greedy adventurers that they have
frequently been represented to be. Now that they are
down, Tiie Bulletin will not, I think, grudge them this
acknowledgment.
The Rapiport - Baxter Fiasco.
The collapse of the “ Beach Scandals ” case in
Melb. Criminal Court leaves the public with a
choice of two suppositions. It must either be
that the Vic. Crown Law Department lacks
legal gumption, or that the case was intended
to fail. Ex-magistrates Rapiport and Baxter
were presented for trial on a charge of having
conspired with Elizabeth and James Boulter
“ to obstruct, prevent and defeat the due course
of justice.” Sundry other charges might have
been included in the indictment, but the Crown
confined itself to this one. E. and J. Boulter,
as the dullest person may perceive at a glance,
could not give strong evidence against their
alleged co-conspirators without placing them-
selves in the power of the law, nor had the
Crown, seemingly, given them any guarantee
of immunity from prosecution as an induce-
ment for them to tell their tales in the witness-
box. Rapiport’s counsel even raised the objec-
tion that Mr. and Mrs. Boulter couldn't give
evidence until they were disposed of, either by
being discharged or convicted, in conjunction
with the indicted parties. Chief Justice
Madden, before dealing seriously with the ob-
jection, ordered Elizabeth Boulter to be
sworn. His Honor then asked Mrs. B. if she
cared to risk the chance of prosecution by open-
ing her mouth, and she replied that she was
unwilling to speak on that understanding.
“ You have the right to hold your tongue if you
Jesire,” suggested the Judge to the witness.
So the witness took the hint to hold her tongue
and went silently away. Mr. Richard Boulter
followed his wife’s lead. His position having
been carefully explained to him by the learned
Judge, he also elected to leave the box with his
tongue in his cheek. Whereupon the Crown
counsel entered a nolle prosequi in a vague,
uncertain manner, and the friends and relations
of Rapiport and Baxter gave thanks to God,
with some of their heads resting upon the
Rappy-Baxter bosoms. Two courses are open
to the Vic. Crown Law department at time of
writing. It may brand itself as a rogue by
allowing the “Bench Scandals” case to drop,
or declare itself to have been an ass in the first
instance by instituting further proceedings
which will bring two alleged criminals to a
satisfactory trial.
The Fetich of a University Degree.
Dear Bulletin, —The principal sting of your
article, “ A Literary Fish,” is where, rhetori-
cally speaking, it should be, in the tail. It
challenges our Australian Universities to prove
that they have done anything for true culture—
genuine literature—science worthy of the name ;
and the challenge cannot be taken up. No
University can generate the creative intellect,
but any University grievously falls short of its
chief duty, its main raison d’etre, if it does not
supply that stimulative teaching which
awakens the creative order of mind to a sense
of its own power and possibilities. Taking
this affirmation —imperfect as to the whole
scope of University teaching, but complete, I
venture to think, so far as it goes—as a
definition of a criterion, how do our
Australian ’Varsities answer the test ? Do
they not fail to answer it at all ? In the
small body of Australian writings meriting
classification as literature, how paltry the
element contributed by University - trained
men ! In original science work, notwithstanding
our alumni have had the advantage of having
under their eyes an almost unworked field, the
notable results which have been obtained by
first-hand investigation can be enumerated in a
five-line paragraph. In law, something has
been achieved, no doubt ; but even here the
men who have gained admittance through the
legal portals without presenting the ’Varsity
ticket have done, man for man, better than the
cap-and-gown crowd. The reason for this is,
primarily, that our Universities have almost
wholly lacked the splendid type of teacher (the
only type to which reverence and obedi-
ence should attach) —the man who inspires.
Woolley and Badham, Wilson and Aldis
were of that type. Hearn approached
it ; Roby Fletcher our petty provincial
world knows not how great a soul was held
enthralled by denominational fetters in the
frail body of the first Chancellor of Adelaide
University—would have been of it had he begun
to lecture in his prime. Of the younger men,
Jenks was developing—he was some-tenths of
an ass was Jenks, but the rest was fine stuff —
Pitt Cobbett and Anderson are developing :
they have glimmerings of what is due from the
teacher to the hungry-to-be-taught. But these
few faithful —faithful to the ideals of the Great
Teacher—what are they among so many ? Our
’Varsity teaching has been of the perfunctory,
unstimulative order, and, as a result, our
’Varsity degrees have become mere fetiches—
masks of dull aspect veiling duller realities,
which impose alone on the credulous, the
ignorant, and the unthinking.— Pyella.
Svdney City Railway.
This paper has for the past fortnight been
scrutinising the Sydney City Railway subject
at odd intervals with odd corners of its mind,
and has entirely failed to come to any definite
conclusion. The problem seems about as com-
plicated as a discussion whether Parkes was
greater than Mt. Kosciusko, and all that can be
done at present is to sort out a few random
ideas which seem to emerge from the heap
rather more definitely than any others. (1)
Redfern railway-station must be enlarged or
shifted at once. (2) It is better to shift
it into the city than to enlarge it by taking
in the Benevolent Asylum, &c., ground across
the road. (3) Considering that the new arrange-
ments are more or less planned in perpetuity,
first cost—within reasonable limits isn’t of
much consequence. Build for to-day, if
you like, but build also for posterity.
So that, if a moderately good scheme
costs .£500,000, and a very good scheme costs,
say, ,£2,000,000, it would probably be better to
spend the odd <£1,500,000 and get the best
scheme obtainable. (4) The Railway Com-
missioners’ scheme puts the station at Park-st.,
which only lessens the Redfern inconvenience
to a limited degree. Many people coming to
George-st., or west of George-st., or north of
Park-st., will still seek another means of con-
veyance. (5) The Railway Commissioners,
generally speaking, are opposed to schemes in-
volving the expenditure of money, and in favor
of schemes which merely involve the costless
resumption of land—Hyde Park, for example.
The Railway Commissioners are like cricketers:
they have to “keep up their averages.” If
they saddle railway capital with an ex-
penditure on which they cannot pay in-
terest, their net-receipts average will go
down. This would grieve the Railway Com-
missioners. I)o they put departmental interest
before public interest ? They say “ No.” Is it
possible for them to deceive themselves uncon-
sciously ? (6) The King-street site would be
ten times better than the Park-street site, and
would apparently cost about twice as much.
Is it worth while choosing Park street? (7)
The railway must come farther sooner or later.
Is it worth while stopping at Park-street?
Says Governor Hampden :
The people who, in a wickedly self-indulgent way, give
a shilling here and there to beggars, commit an absolute
offence against society The man who gives
£1 to a charity is doing more good than if he gave £5O
in a casual way.
The last statement is pure speculation ; but,
even admitting that organised charity does
more general good than promiscuous, how many
unfortunate wretches have had reason to be
thankful for the “ self-indulgence ” of the
people who believe that the “ Bis dat ” maxim
is as true to-day as in the days of old Rome ?
The temptation to “ wickedness ” of this kind,
at all events, is seldom very violent in people
who talk like his Excellency. The man who,
from a sense of public duty, lacerates his own
feelings to any great extent by refusing to a
dead-beat the price of a meal, is—if he exists !
—a veritable hero. But, about the meanest
thing that w alks is the creature who saddles
upon a politico-economical principle his indiffer-
ence to the sufferings of others or his personal
aversion to “parting” uuder any circumstances.
An infinitely greater man than Lord Hampden
—Edmund Burke— wrote :
“ No obligation to benefit my species by degrees, or
upon system, can override my plain duty to feed the
hungry man, if I can, upon the spot. That is the “ sys-
tem ” which Nature has taught us, and Nature is always
right in the long run.
Vic. M.L.C. Embling, who has lately been
stumping his constituency, says that the Coun-
cil will of course “ ultimately bow to the will
of the people.” This is very kind of the Coun-
cil ; but, all the same, the assertion places that
dismal body on the horns of a very pretty
dilemma. The will of the people can be ex-
pressed in only two ways—by the decision of
the Assembly, or by that of the country at the
polling-booths. Now, if the Council “bows” to
the former, it thereby recognises the Assembly
as representative of the actual wishes of the
people at large ; and such an admission would
sweep away at one stroke all the Council’s pre-
tence of usefulness. If the 48 Rich Landlords
insist upon hearing the people’s decision at a
general election, then their backsliding is
still more vile; for they are then resorting
to the referendum, although most probably
their utter stupidity prevents them from per-
ceiving this. But, in point of fact, the Council
does not wash to bend its back (most M’s.L.C.
are too fat to stoop, anyway) in the very least
degree. Its policy is to delay everything use-
ful and desirable, until a disgusted or forgetful
people is ready to take half, or a-quarter, or
any fraction whatsoever of the loaf originally
demanded. And it is partly the people’s own
fault that this game comes off.
Melbourne Punch item
Russia has recently adopted a system that puts the big
nose of the usurious blood-sucker out of joint, and in this
matter we would do well to take points from the Musco-
vite. A State bank has been established at St. Peters-
burg, with branches in all the towns throughout the
country, where the people can raise loans at 4J per cent,
on all sorts of property, from dairy produce to real estate,
and so far as the experiment has gone it has been an im-
mense success.
Yeb when Maoriland and South Australia
started State banks the M'Kinley paper met
the proposition w ith a dreary sneer, and when
Turner announced his intention of creating a
similar institution in Victoria the same Fat
Man journal received him wdth a scoff and a
groan and a curse. The Tory paper keeps up a
reputation for Liberalism by approving of
democratic measures at the other end of the
world, and objecting to them when they get any
closer ; and by admiring the democrat who is
dead and can’t do anything more, while heaping
maledictions on the one w’ho is alive and active.
Therefore, the democrat wdio is alive is always
told that, if he were only like the one who is
dead, the Fat Man organ wouldn’t mind lending
him its support, but being the degraded object
he is it really can’t do so ; and very few people
notice that it called the dead democrat a de-
graded object also when he was alive, or that
it will begin to gently approve of the present
live one—when he is sufficiently dead. The
M‘Kinley organ’s enthusiasm about a State
bank at the other end of the globe is a touch-
ing hunk of sentiment.
Attorney-General Isaacs has not put „a
healthier complexion on the collapse of the
Rabibort-Baxter case by his attempt to shift
all the blame upon Chief Justice Madden.
Granting that the Judge was in a hurry to
advise (for his persistent cautions amounted to
advice) the chief witnesses to hold their tongues,
the fact remains that they had previously
declared themselves ignorant of their position
as regards the indictment, and it was the
Judge’s explanation which made them refuse to
give evidence against the prisoners. The
Boulters clearly had not been made easy in
their minds at the Crown Law Office ; they
were not encouraged to do their best for the
Crown case. Mr. Isaacs, in his explanations to
the Melbourne pressmen, gave away this impor-
tant feature of the scandal by saying—
A witness cannot refuse to go into the witness-box on
the ground that the evidence he will be asked for would
incriminate him, and that he intends to refuse to give
that evidence. What he has to do is to go into the box,
and after he is sworn he must wait till the objectionable
question is put, and then he can refuse to answer it.
Then the Judge hks to consider his refusal together with
any reasons he (the witness) may give for it, and decide
whether the objection ought to be sustained. If this
question had arisen I have no doubt the Crown-Prosecutor
would at once have satisfied the learned Judge that there
was no reason to fear any prosecution against the
Boulters.
How came it that the Boulters were not
assured of their safety weeks before they went
into court ? If the Crown counsel knew they
had no reason to fear a prosecution, why were
they allowed to assume otherwise, seeing that
the question re their willingness to testify was
only what might have been expected from the
Judge? By not “making it right” with the
Boulters beforehand the Crown Law Office
practically invited the disgusting fiasco which
Attorney-General Isaacs so loudly deplores.
And as for the A.-G.’s “ regret that the matter
was not concluded (mark the word !) in a more
definite manner for the sake of the community
generally,” the community would like to know
why he can’t prosecute Messrs. Rappy and
Baxter on one or more other charges. Surely
the matter is not bound to begin and end with
the abortioned Boulter case !
After long waiting, the Melb. Anti-Vaccina-
tionists have scored a win against the Jenner
Act. This Act provides that if, after receipt
of a notice from a deputy-registrar, any person
neglects to have his child vaccinated, he is to
be deemed guilty of an offence. In another
section provision is made for penalties for second
and subsequent offences. Judge Hodges has
decided that the Act makes no provision for
the sending out of notices by the deputy-
registrar a second time, and that thus, although
it had provided a penalty for a second offence,
it had not created a second offence. Which
practically means that the Anti-Vaccinationists
will get what they have demanded—the privi-
lege of paying a small fine for conscience’ sake,
instead of the ruinous fines necessarily imposed
for subsequent offences. Another pretty speci-
men of the coach-and-four trick. The Board
of Public Health is, of course, very angry, and
will try desperately to have the thing altered.
N.S.W. Speaker Abbott’s employment of the
word “frivolous,” in connection with Cope-
land’s amendment on Carruthers’ Homestead
Selections Bill in N.S.W. Assembly last
week, conclusively demonstrates the folly of
leaving sucli a word, without an exact defini-
tion, as a weapon in the armoury of the first
officer of Parliament. Copeland’s amendment
proposed to give 28 additional days’ occupancy
to the present holders of land intended to be
resumed under the provisions of the Bill. It
was the most important amendment proposed
during the discussion ; so important, indeed,
that Ministers saw the necessity of rousing
the House to resist it, with the result that only
Copeland and Perry stood firmly up for it.
Yet Speaker Abbott officially declared the
amendment to be “ frivolous,” and as “ frivo-
lous ” it accordingly figures on the official
record. Manifestly it is not the amend-
ment that was frivolous ; what Abbott’s
ruling means is that the support accorded it,
on one ground and another, was so numerically
small that there was no use on that occasion to
carry the matter further. But surely Parlia-
ment can hit on a word that will express that
particular state of things ; or, if it can’t, it can
surely find some word which will not commit it
to an admittedly false characterisation. “ Every
truth begins with a minority of one.” Every
truth, therefore, which happens to come
before Speaker Abbott during its earliest
stages, in the form of an unpopular motion
or amendment, must go on the records as
itself “ frivolous,” though Mr. Abbott per-
sonally believe it important, though the House
believe it important, though the community
believe it important. “ Frivolous ” it is, be-
cause of the inefficient vocabulary at the dis-
posal of the Parliamentary Chair.
Extracts from a report from the British consul
in Japan, lately published by the British
Foreign Office :
If the people of Australia seek cheapness without being-
particular as to durability, Japan can also soon supply
them with a hundred articles which they now either make
for themselves or import from England or Germany—
matches, boots, saddlery, harness, portmanteaus, hats,
&c., all of which Japan could furnish to them at less than
half the prices they would have to pay for European pro-
totypes. . . . Japan has at present absolutely no re-
quirements that Australia could supply which are not
already satisfactorily met by the much-nearer United
States ; and it is improbable that, unless tempted by lower
prices, Japanese buyers—who are strongly attracted to
the people of the United States by sentimental reasons,
and also by the further consideration that they are by far
Japan’s best customers for all the great staples of her
export trade —will ever seek in Australia articles which
they can obtain in less time and equally good from the
States. Among these are flour, leather, preserved pro-
visions, fruits, wine, biscuits, butter, lard, beef, &c. There
is but a limited field for the importation of Australian
stallions and brood mares, while of a wholesale import of
Australian horses for general use there is not the slightest
prospect. Almost the same may be said as to wool and
mutton.
Apparently something has again gone wrong
with Sydney Daily Telegraph’s theory that
boots aren’t made in Japan ; and Jack Want’s
assertion that there is only one boot-factory
in Chrysanthemum Land, and not much of
that, rests on no foundation. But perhaps
the cheerful Jack didn’t seriously investigate
the Jap. boot-question. Most likely he was—
ahem ! —otherwise employed.
With the exception of the Referendum, the
principles of the new platform of the Victorian
Labor-party and those of the Victorian Con-
vention of 1857 are much alike. It proves how
slowly the Victorian world has marched that
the Convention over which Wilson Gray pre-
sided presented the chief demands, even to elec-
toral equalifications and exclusion of the Chinese
—which a party of to-day puts forw'ard as a
new platform. Where details vary it is ques-
tionable if the new party has improved on
Gray. For instance, the Labor-party proposes
that “ registration shall be the sole qualification
for Parliamentary elections.” The Convention of
’57 resolved that there should be no registration
of voters whatever, but that each elector who
came to vote should take oath that he was duly
qualified, the argument in favor of the resolu
tion being that, as property and life are daily
disposed of by oath, the exercise ot the fran-
chise could be similarly governed. The new
platform provision for the enfranchisement of
seamen and other persons following migratory
occupations was more than equalled by the
Convention resolution permitting every person
to vote on merely making oath that he was two
months in the district.
New South Wales Education Minister Gar-
rard suggests the establishment of a State art
school, directly supervised by his department.
Already N.S. Wales subsidises art students by
a donation to the Art Society’s unsatisfactory
classes, and by endowment of classes at Sydney
Technical College. There is really no reason
why there should not be a national art school,
or a national school of music, and Mr. Garrard,
who knows nothing about either music or art,
can come to an unbiassed decision in the
matter. But the N.S. Wales Education
Minister who really desires to be famous must
democratise the University, and bring it under
direct departmental control as the keystone of
the province’s educational system.
To the numerous objections from all parts of
the world to the demonstrated inefficiency of
the Privy Council as a final Court of Appeal
from Courts dispensing all known systems of
jurisprudence, it has been repeatedly urged of
late by eminent authorities that there is no
longer any reason to complain, on account of
the fact that the membership of the P.C. and of
the House of Lords in its judicial capacity are
now practically the same. But hardly is this
effected than another change is made, by draft-
ing into the P.C. possibly incapable and super-
annuated colonial Judges possessed of the
requ site amount of impudence and social influ-
ence. The possibility of a Falkland Island
Judge having the balance of power in deter-
mining a Mercadool appeal, or a Kanaka Judge
from Queensland deciding the applicability of a
Maoriiand Undesirable Immigrants Act to
Solomon Islanders, or a YVindeyer on a murder
appeal, should urge Australians at once to
jockey the P.C. out of its seat, and substitute,
whether we get Federation or not, a wisely-
constituted Australian Federal Court of Appeal.
A startling judgment just given by the U.S.
Supreme Court (Circuit) on the question of the
burden of proof in criminal cases when the
plea of insanity is raised :
The law presumes that everyone charged with crime is
sane, and thus supplies, in the first instance, the required
proof of capacity to commit crime. It authorises the
jury to assume at the outset that the accused is criminally
responsible for his acts. But that is not a conclusive pre-
sumption which the law, upon grounds of public policy,
forbids to be overthrown or impaired by opposing proof.
It is a disputable, or, as it is often designated, a rebuttable
presumption. . . . Strictly speaking, the burden of
proof, as those words are understood in criminal law, is
never upon the accused to establish his innocence, or to
disprove the facts necessary to establish the crime for
which he is indicted. It is on the prosecution from the
beginning to the end of the trial, and applies to every
element necessary to constitute the crime. ... If
the whole evidence, including that supplied by the pre-
sumption of sanity, does not exclude beyond reasonable
doubt the hypothesis of insanity, of which some proof is
adduced, the accused is entitled to an acquittal of the
specific offence charged.
A writer in Westminster Review has
been urging the amendment of that part of the
law which gives judges and magistrates abso-
lute immunity from actions, even though their
judgments have been given from malicious or
corrupt motives. It doesn’t appear to be much
use urging such a proper reform in this judge-
ridden country, but surely something might be
done to provide unfortunate witnesses with an
action against barristers in the following cases :
1. Where the cross-examining barrister makes slander-
ous statements which are unnecessary to support the case
of his client.
2. Where he makes them without justification or excuse.
3. Where he makes them actuated by personal ill-will
or anger.
4. Where they are irrelevant to every question of fact
in issue before the Court.
Counsel’s privilege of slander in such cases as
these is based on the same principle of law as
the privilege of the corrupt or malicious judge
of the Westminster reviewer.
Were not the solicitors of Sydney a long-
suffering and humble crowd, they would never
have submitted so long to the indignities to
which they are subjected in the Supreme Court.
No accommodation worthy of the name is there
provided for them. Should a solicitor attend
to hear a case, woe betide him if he trespasses
on the seats inscribed “barristers,” and set
aside for those pets of the Bench—the “ mem-
bers of the bar.” He may be reproved from the
bench for his audacity (this has happened more
than once) ; at the least, he will be approached
by a tipstaff and invited to get out, or, at any-
rate, take one of the back seats, bearing the
legend, “ Legal profession.” Even there, how-
ever, he is not safe, for, should there be a large
influx of briefless juniors, he will be hunted
again to make room for them. Matters have
gone even further than this; lately a
solicitor, who was conducting his own case,
was naturally seated at the court-table ; on the
arrival of a bewigged and begowned youngster,
for whom there was no vacant chair, the pre-
siding Judge told him he must give up his seat
to the barristerial suckling—adding that solici-
tors must not sit at the table to the exclusion of
barristers, but must give way to them always.
One would imagine that, as a solicitor has
audience in the Supreme Court, he has as much
right to a seat at the table, when conducting his
case, as has a barrister. The Judges, however,
think otherwise. The barristers have rooms
provided for them at the Equity Court and
No. 2 Jury Court, but members of the “ lower
branch ” must not enter their sacred precincts.
When counsel is employed the solicitor,
in some of the courts, has to perch him-
self on a bench, with the table between
him and his counsel, and has to bawl his in-
structions across the table, thereby giving his
