Pinero’s “ Benefit of the Doubt,” which is
the latest new bill at Sydney Cri., shows itself
well up to its British reputation. It is a clever
story of how two homes were burst-up, not by
the great, crude paw of conspiracy and crime as
homes are burst-up in melodrama, but by the
small things which bring about trouble in real life.
There are two heroines. One marries a Scotch-
man who insists on taking her to his ancestral
castle under the shadow of Ben Thingumbob, and
keeps her there till she loathes the grouse,
and the heather, and the retainer with his
bagpipes, and her husband’s kilts, and can’t
take his knobby bare knees seriously any
longer. The other lady’s jealousy drives her hus-
band out of doors, and the man who is trying to
escape his w r ife’s language and the woman who is
avoiding her husband’s kilt and the nightmare of
his two knees get to sympathising with each other.
Then they get into the divorce-court, where the
Judge gives Mrs. Brough the benefit of the doubt
but warns her to walk a chalk-line in future.
And thereupon the broken-down co-respondent
appeals to her kilted husband for sympathy
and doesn’t get it, and flies in despair to the
house of the respondent, just as the plaintiff
has arrived there on all-fours to abase herself and
promise not to be a she-fiend any longer. And
there the co-respondent drowns her misery in too
much champagne on the respondent’s premises,
and asks him to fly with her, while the plaintiff
listens in the next room ; and the respondent, who
knows on one hand that the plaintiff is listening
and on the other that the co-respondent is getting
partly crazed and partly intoxicated, has a really
interesting time. The co-respondent, who is sup-
posed to be a pure and noble woman, grows un-
pleasantly realistic at this stage, and in any case a
woman who is partly tight and has dropped her
manners in a heap on the floor is more or less
Zola-esque at all times.
* * * *
Even after this outbreak, however, Pinero
contrives to make an artistic and happy ending of
the drama by means of a bishop and other ex-
traneous circumstances. Still, Mrs. Brough’s very
fine performance is marred somewhat by the re-
collection of the author’s moral hiccough in act
11., and it seems a pity that part of the hiccough
isn’t left out. Miss Beryl Faber scores another
big success as the plaintiff; and the dramatic scene
where these two ladies get together—both sober
and desolate and disconsolate —and the plaintiff
offers in effect to let the co-respondent stand on
her with both feet by way of reparation, and
the co-respondent fails to see how that will
improve the situation, is one of the best
things either of them has yet accomplished.
Cecil Ward, who is supposed to be the Caledonian
husband, is somewhat unconvincing because his
kilts don’t appear, and he has no accent and no
bagpipes and but little to do ; but Titheradge, as
the other husband, has a large, convincing char-
acter, and plays it admirably. Six other people,
including Brough, Boucicault, and Misses Watt-
Tanner and Kate Bishop, help more or less directly
to bring the drama about, or to keep it from
getting away when it is about, and fill in spaces
while the two heroines are getting ready to break
out in a fresh place ; and they all give complete
satisfaction. “The Benefit of the Doubt ” is dis-
tinctly a powerful play, and the co. plays it for all
there is in it. Also it contains a great moral of
some kind, though which moral the present writer
hasn’t yet decided, and the management describes
it on the bill as “a comedy.”
** h *
“The Derby Winner,” at Sydney Royal,
deserves special mention as an honorable howl in
four acts and thirteen scenes. As a drama it
is about up to the average, and as a pageant it is
vast and gorgeous and stupendous and makes the
spectator feel as if Solomon’s Temple had struck
him unexpectedly in the eye. It is a moving mass
of gas and glitter and scenery and horse and mili-
tary uniform, furniture, millinery, ancestral man-
sion, dazzle, paint, and rich, high-toned shriek.
As for the literary contents, they include the
villain, and the adventuress, and the distracted
wife, and the good girl, and the girl who is led
astray and starts talking about her honor because
she hasn’t any, and the funny man, and the other
accustomed props and circumstances. Also there
is a real billiard-table, and a moderately realistic
Derby at which the good man’s horse wins, and
the bad man’s horse is disgracefully left through a
complication of circumstances, and doesn’t even
get started. Consequently, the bad man is so
utterly and hopelessly broke that the author
doesn’t even think it necessary to explain what
becomes of him, but presumably he afterwards
makes a precarious living with a baked - ’tater
barrow. The adventuress is also left unfinished —
she laughs a wild laugh like that of a maniac
robbed of her clothing and departs out of the tale,
leaving her after-fate to the imagination ; alid
what becomes of the girl who lost her honor is not
mentioned ; but there is a general atmosphere of
happiness about things at the end, and presum-
ably all is well, Of the ladies who in ex-
pounding the narrative, the graceful Miss Frances
Ross deserves the first place, with Miss Vivienne a
very good second. Mrs. Holt is somewhere close
up, and Miss Elizabeth Watson brings up the rear
with a hard cackle and a tendency to whisky-and-
soda. Still this lady produces the biggest effect
she has yet achieved in Sydney when she kneels
down solemnly in the centre of the stage and
says “Damn!” It is a deep, soulful,
realistic curse the curse of a woman in
an evening wrapper, with her hair down-a
lost woman swearing on a ruined man’s Turkey
carpet and refusing to be comforted. The fact
that this part with the large swear in it is the first
in which Miss Watson hasn’t been quite such a
failure as she was in the other ones suggests the
painful reflection that perhaps she ought to swear
oftener. Baker plays the hero in his usual finished
style. Cosgrove is distinctly better than usual,
and Bland Holt, Norman, Charles Brown, and
Miss Flora Anstead are all highly satisfactory.
The fact that the girl who has lost her honor is
wooden and mechanical is hardly noticeable among
the overwhelming scenery and stupendous mass of
horse. A small, pallid girl trying to explain about
her honor in front of all these effects is bound to
be unconvincing, anyhow.
* * * *
“ The Wages of Sin ” is to be staged next
Saturday “ by arrangement with W. J. Holloway,
Esq.,” at Her Majesty’s, which is one more
evidence of the amazing tendency things have to
happen by arrangement with Plain Bill Hol-
loway. Meanwhile, the realistic locomotive in
“ Across the Continent ’’—almost the only stage
locomotive this paper has seen which looks fit to
run for its life—will continue until Friday to bring
the United States troops on the scene just in time
to prevent Manager Woods losing his scalp, and
the accustomed spectacle of blood and carnage,
hair, smoke, battle and dead-meat will continue
as usual.
* * * *
There is nothing specially new to say this
week on the subject of “Trilby,” save that The
Bulletin man thinks he has discovered a corn on
the heroine’s dainty extremity, and if his suspi-
cion proves well-founded he intends to make
trouble. A Trilby with a corn isn’t what the
public bargained for. anyhow. At the same time,
things might be worse. For instance, this paper
knows of two actresses now in Australia who
would do the Trilby business well enough but for
the fact that they have eleven corns between them.
How they are divided isn’t known with certainty,
for each alleges that she has one corn and that the
remaining ten belong to the other woman.
* * * *
Rev. Charles Clark was announced to start
his series of lectures too late for notice in this
issue, so the eloquent Rev. is reluctantly held over
till next week. By the time this gets into
print Mr. Clark—if nothing has intervened—
will be letting his magnificent voice loose in
Sydney Centenary Hall. The first lecture, by the
way—the one fixed for Tuesday night—contains a
lot about the liberties of Hingland, and the
advantages of living under Queen Victoria, and
the future Federation of the Empire ; and as this
paper doesn’t care to hear a man who is capable of
better things wasting himself in a building which
is fitted for a better purpose on the weary question
of the Silken Bonds, it is just as well pleased
that the said lecture arrived too late for notice.
* * * *
Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus continues to
flourish, and so does Rickards’ Tivoli show, and
various other things. Charles Godfrey shows no
falling off, neither does Mahomet, the Talking
Horse, and the report that money continues to
come in freely at both establishments grows so
monotonous that this paper hereby swears off all
further mention of it as long as it lives.
* * * *
Further inspection of “Silver King,” at
Melbourne Royal, strengthens The Bulletin’s
faith in that gold-mounted show. It even im-
proves on acquaintance. Plimmer’s Spider has
become a somewhat louder and more emphatic
criminal than at first, when he under-acted the
character in accordance with Garner and Sass
traditions. In fact, Plimmer began by restricting
himself to an imitation of Sass, a quiet, gentle-
manly creature whose bloodless method didn’t
deserve to be reproduced. Now that the young
man suits his accent to the word, and his
gesture to the deed—which the other Spider
didn’t —he may consider himself a complete success.
Scot Inglis developes his natural fitness for the
name-part, and Miss Harrie Ireland begins to
show a warm and tender fellow-feeling for the
character of Nellie Denver, more especially in the
cottage scene, when the wolf howls at the door
and Bodger, the baker, is her only hope. Also
Elton, Sweeney, and the company in general, are
going strong. The drunken passenger, who de-
mands an escort to a third-class smoking carriage,
deserves honorable mention for his neat study in
alcoholic effect, but praise for a small-part actor
is unfortunately apt to inflate the object and lead
him into extravagance. It is likely that “Silver
King ” will travel the remainder of Westmacott’s
course, and pass the post with a good many
pounds in hand.
* * * *
At the Princess’s they are doing satisfac-
tory business with “In Town.” The show, as
now treated, is less showy than the previous dis-
play ; nevertheless, the opera co. puts in several
claims to favorable comparison with the more
glamorous crowd of yore. Strange to say, Palotta,
the most-worshipped and overrated among the
bygone goddesses, is the least missed of them all.
Miss Muret seems about equal to Palotta in every-
thing, except age, and the fault of one pretty
woman in being a year or so younger than another
is readily forgiven by this benevolent journal.
Besides, Miss M. couldn’t avoid the defect any
more than Miss Nash could wipe out the peculiar
conditions of Fred Kaye’s walk. The present
call-boy is lacking in splay feet, but otherwise she
behaves humorously, and goes almost as far as the
original Shrimp. One consecutive performance of
“ A Gaiety Girl” concludes the musical comedy
season on Friday night'.
** # *
On Saturday the Princess’s will fall again
under the spell of Mrs. Potter and Kyrle Bellew,
whose five years’ absence has probably endeared
them to Melbourne. Anyway the city is already
disposed to rejoice over the return of these in-
teresting wanderers, and their first appearance in
“As You Like It” has been anticipated by a
furious rush for seats. Mrs. Potter in doublet
and hose, especially hose, is the question of the
hour.
** # *
Dan. Barry has prudently withdrawn him-
self and 00. from the Alexandra for a time, in
order that he may come back with a certain air of
freshness in the not too distant future. Daniel’s
latest tenancy of the theatre lasted six months,
during which period he attacked twenty dramas,
more or less, and kept the “ghost” moving.
Therefore, he can glance back at a long succession
of Alexandra managers who failed miserably, and
congratulate himself, like the Pharisee in the
Worrd.
** * *
At Melbourne Opera House nothing special
has been happening beyond the indisposition of
Alice Leamar. The pit and upper circles seem to
take offence at Alice’s state of health, and when
somebody, armed with a medical certif., an-
nounces that the volatile damsel can’t appear,
the multitude shouts Yah ! Booh ! or words
to the same effect. John Coleman was to
retire from the bill on Wednesday so as
to leave more room for the renowned Frantz
Family. These high-toned acrobats landed in
Melbourne early last week, and will make their
Australian debut this (Thursday) evening. The
nicety of their work is such that the voyage out
from Hingland unfitted them for immediate
action, so, in the meantime, they have been oiling
their joints and recovering their lost perfection.
At Poverty Point .
Kyrle Bellew started life at sea, but was pretty well dis-
gusted by knocking about the India waters. In Melb. he
lectured for a panorama of the Franco-German war at
the Waxworks, and afterwards as reporter for the Evening
Herald used to write police-court reports in this strain :
“ Mr. Brown and Mrs. Brown don’t get on well together.
The other night Mrs. B. got tight and he commenced to

Peart (Mercutio): “ ’Tis not so deep as a well, nor so
wide as a church-door, but ’tis enough ; ’twill serve. Ask
for me to-morrow and you shall find me a grave man.”
Frank Cates is dying of consumption in Melb. A private
collection amongst his many 7 friends has superseded the
public “ benefit,” which is so often nothing of the kind.
Wallace Brownlow is really quitting the Firm’s opera
co. this week, at the end of the Melbourne season. More-
over, it is almost certain that W.B. will sail for America
shortly, but the ladies mustn’t abandon all hope until he
gets aboard the lugger. Nothing is certain of a young
man who returns to the stage after he has bought a pick-
axe to go gold-mining with.
Melb. : “ Brownlow is wise. He will only prospect for
nuggets on the stage. He may have once given it up,
and come back, but then he was thinner and 30, a differ-
ent affair to being fatter and on the verge of 40. The
theatre nowadays is emphatically a case of making hay
while the sun shines, like being a Carbine or a cricketer.
The professional life of a Brownlow, at his zenith, apex,
and apogee, is only about ten years, and he cannot slice
them out where he pleases.”
A pen-picture of a prima-donna : —A tripping wisp of
Jewess, short skirted, thin ankled, a palpable deposit of
powder on the face, a very angry mouth, bright black
eyes, gilt hair, a blue costume splashed with scarlet, and
a scarlet cock’s feather stuck in a jaunty hat. And this
is the swift five-feet six of self-confident girldom at which
the boys turn round to look and make remarks.
During their recent visit to the United States Dan and
Tom Fitzgerald found themselves in a railway carriage
opposite to a solemn-looking American (why are Ameri-
cans so solemn-looking ?) who, observing their strange way
of speaking the American tongue, said, “Guess y 7 ou’re
English?” “Oh, no; nothing of the sort. We’re Aus-
tralians.” “ 11. landall, y’are ? ” From a suspicious
demeanor the solemn one changed to cordiality, and the
brothers soon found that an Australian is a persona grata
to Americans, while the Britisher simply 7 isn’t. During
the journey the solemn man cited, with damnable itera-
tion, the various wonders of his Great Country 7, and, in a
contest of patriotism, Tom and Dan found it necessary to
raise the Blue Mountains 14,000 feet, run a spiral railway
up to the summit of Mt. Victoria (17,460 ft.), endow them
with everlasting snow and the largest glacier in the world ;
widen the Murray River at Border Town and span it with
a bridge eight miles long ; increase the speed of all Aus-
tralian trains by 20 miles an hour, abolish the steam-trams
in Sydney, deepen the Mount Gambier lake, increase the
population of Australia by 20 millions, establish an admir-
able system of water conservation and irrigation through-
out the country, discover a great inland lake larger than
Lake Superior, deepen all the coal-mines and raise the
output of gold, praise the sheep, finish the North Shore
bridge, exterminate the rabbits, devise a full and workable
system of local Government for N.S. Wales, magnify the
Jenolan caves, supply the Australias with an army and
a fleet, create a central Government sitting at Boo-
ligal, and elect an Australian President—all within a 24
hours’ journey.
Photographer Falk is not impressed by the commercial
morality of many visiting “stars” who have brought
their patronage to the studio. A long experience of pro-
fessional sitters is embittered by many recollections of
unpaid bills and petty meanness. The stage and concert
platform are not training grounds for high principle, as
far as orders for photos, are concerned. Falk makes a
few honorable exceptions. Maud Hobson was somewhat
remarkable among the Gaiety Girlers for a sense of fair
dealing. Mrs. Potter and Bellew respected themselves
and their contracts, and Sarah Bernhardt did the generous
thing, as usual. Falk’s paid £lOO for the sole right of
“taking” the famous French actress in Australia, and
she, being pleased with the work, subsequently “ gave as
good as she took ” in the form of a huge order for por-
traits, which she could very well have dispensed with no
doubt.
Lawrence Irving, says The Bulletin’s London corre-
spondent, is beginning to tread in his famous father’s
footsteps, and is making good headway in his chosen pro-
fession. He, like the old man, has mannerisms without
number, and by-and-bye he may show that he inherits
genius. He is now playing Svengali in one of Tree’s
travelling co’s, and has made a decidedly good impression,
although he has not the advantage of the inspiration
which might be derived from the presence of his fianciein
the name-part.

Alfred Moul, now manager of London Alhambra, erst-
while musical critic of Melb. Age, has received from the
French President the decoration of Offieier de T Instruc-
tion Publique, as a reward for his untiring efforts on
behalf of the Soci<st6 des Auteurs, Compositeurs, et
Editeurs de Musique de France —in other words, for his
zeal in bringing to book those who, without authority,
annexed the works of French authors and composers. ’
Lovely woman seems responsible for the literally down-
ward tendency of dramatic art. Some 29 years ago
actresses drew audiences by the exercise of their brains
then came the decolletb or undraped and heaving-bosom
period, which was quietly followed by the leg-show-- -the
descent of art from the head to the limbs being very
rapid. The climax is reached by “ Trilby ’ —the drama of
the bared feet. Apparently the dramatic thermometer
has now reached freezing-point.
Clever Jennie Reiffarth, the Madame Vinard of “Trilby,”
was at first an operatic star. Her “ first appearance ” was
in “ II Trovatore,” in which she played Leonora at almost
a moment’s notice on the engaged prima donna throwing
up the role.

Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis was first announced, in error, as
the Lady Carlisle of the Melb. Trinity Collegians’ “ Straf-
ford,” and it proved to be Mrs. L. L. Lewis. Natheless,
one thinks of Mrs. G.B.W. as a struggling woman, in the
prime of life, cruelly shelved as an actress. Many Aus-
tralians went to see her in London, a couple of years ago,
as Mrs. Oakly, in “ The Jealous Wife,” and will testify she
was the unlucky best of her line.
Mrs. L. L. Lewis once more eyed the floats with the
Melb. Trinity Amateurs in “ Strafford.” Had she not left
the stage 30 years ago it is evident she would have
bloomed into achievements much beyond those of her
then tender age for a leading lady.
Pagliacci: “One with a faint recollection of theatrical
f cites in London, long out of fashion, could not help con-
trasting the fresh, beautiful, and ladylike actresses of
this Melb. one with the memory of raddled, impudent,
painted hussies, more corresponding to the low type of
circus-rider or vulgar cheap saloon barmaid of to-day.”
Memories of the Melb. Opera House tragedy of 15 years
ago, were revived last week by the arrest of one Robert
Greer for housebreaking, at Warrnambool (Vic.). This
young Bob Greer proved to be a son of the Greer who
shot his wife and her French lover—they were sitting
in a private box at the time—previous to settling himself
with another bullet.
Jennie Reiffarth and Maggie Moore, who hadn’t seen
one another for years, met again in a stage-box at Melb.
Princess’s the other night before the “Trilby” season
ended. Jennie recognised Maggie in the box—probably
she knew 7 where to look for her old friend—and threw off
a melodramatic exclamation as she walked down stage.
At the conclusion of the act she broke the theatre-rule
by rushing into Maggie’s presence, and exchanging hugs
with her in a dark corner of the den, until the call-boy
summoned the impetuous lady back to business.
Miss Geraldine Olliffe, says The Bulletin’s London cor-
pondent, came well through the ordeal of her appearance
as leading lady in Henry Arthur Jones’s new play, “The
Rogues’ Comedy,” at the Garrick. It is not a great work,
but it is interesting enough, though it is mightily reminis-
cent of more than one French play.
“ Charley’s Aunt ” is even now clearing £2OO per week
in London, and has broken the “ Our Boys ” record for
long-staying.
Wallace Kennedy, “ the South Australian tenor,” con-
buted songs of Schubert and Mendelssohn at a Steinway
concert, as London D.T. says, “in a manner studiously
modest and void of offence.”
Old Harry Stanley died the other week in Newcastle
(N.S.W.) hospital, and few people seem to have heard of
his passing hence. Yet the veteran deserved more
obituary notice than the average theatrical identity.
Stanley was a pioneer showman in India and Africa,
especially India, where he had a juvenile opera co.
running, under the management of Tom Liddiard (Fanny
L’.s brother), at the time of his death. For years he pros-
pered so exceedingly that, although the Oriental Bank
failure knocked him to the tune of £40,000, he must have
left a good deal of property in sundry places. Harry’s
business ability was not hampered by his social disposi-
tion. Almost to the last he had a keen eye for contem-
plating the wine when it is red.

The Wild-Cat Column .
Melbourne Age makes a financial suggestion
which is worth a lot of serious thought. The Vic. Govern-
ment has about £2,000,f!0i) of 4 per cent, loans falling due
next year, and £1,600,000 falling due in’99. South Aus-
tralia has £500,000 of 4) per cent, bonds falling due in
’9B, and £1,400,000 of 4 per cent, loans; due between now
and ’99. At present money is so cheap that it is about 100
to 1 it will never be cheaper in Australia within the
memory of this generation, and it is at least 10 to 1 it
will become much dearer before these loans fall due.
Therefore, it might be a great idea to offer now a local
loan at par and bearing, say, 3J per cent, interest, to meet
these obligations when they arrive, and sell the stock
gradually at the Treasury as the money came in. It
would only come in gradually, and, as the Government
could probably put it on pretty safe deposit at 2) per cent,
till it is wanted, the loss of interest in the interval would
be very little. And against this loss there is the saving
in the expenses of flotation, and the fact that the loan
which would now probably realise £lOO might later on,
in a tighter market, fetch only £95 or £94. If there ever
was a chance to raise a big loan locally at very low in-
terest and thus transfer some of this country’s English
obligations to the local market that chance is here now,
and it is a pity to let it pass in the shadowy hope that an
equally good chance may be here a year or two later. It
is only two years since N.S.W. and Queensland had to
borrow in London at 4J per cent, and Victoria at nearly
4)6, and in two years more these rates may be prevailing
again for all any one really knows to the contrary.
Apropos The Bulletin’s contention that loans can be
raised as cheaply in Australia as in London : In 1873-76
N.S. Wales raised a 4 per cent, loan in Sydney, and at the
same period it borrowed at 4) per cent, in London. In
1892-93 it raised a local loan at 4 per cent., and in 1893 it
borrowed in London at 4) per cent. Victoria raised 4 per
cent, loans in Melbourne in 1872, ’B2, ’B7, ’B9 and ’94. In
1874 it borrowed in London at £4l3s. ; in 1876 at £4 6s. lOd.;
in 1879 at £4 12s. lOd. ; in ’B3 at £4 3s. ; and in ’93 at £4
9s. 6d. So far as experience goes, except when very large
amounts are wanted, Australia is not only about as cheap
as England now but it has been so very often before.
It is a curious circumstance, by the way, that N.S. Wales
began by borrowing in the local market and made a heavy
loss by shifting its business to England. Between 1849
and 1854 the Government floated in Sydney four loans at
£4 18s. 9d. per cent., and every one of them sold at a
heavy premium. Next it floated two at £4 3s. Bd. per
cent, and sold them also at a premium. Then in 1855 it
shifted its borrowing business to London, paid 5 per cent,
interest, and sold its securities at a heavy discount in
addition. It kept on paying this 5 per cent. - for 17 years.
Which is a very solemn thing to look back upon over the
wastes of the intervening period. If N.S.W. hadn’t wanted
to borrow quite the whole globe in one act—if, say, half
the globe would have served its purpose—it could pro-
bably have dispensed with the London money-lender
from the beginning until now.

Vague cablegrams keep arriving to the effect
that Robert Harper is getting on well in his negotiations
with the British depositors of the Commercial Bank of
Australia, and that the arrangements for a reduction of
interest and an extension of time are nearly completed.
Meanwhile, the exact nature of the proposals is still kept
dark, and how much more time and how much less in-
terest the Commercial requires remain a mystery.

Dear Bulletin, — As one of the pioneers in 1889
of the share-export trade from Melbourne to London, I
venture to correct your opinion that the Melb. market
cannot finance the present boom. Every scripper for
whom I export shares to London puts his proceeds into
local shares. In other words, when trams, Hills, or Lyells
are sold in London, Bands or North Long Tunnels, or
some other trustworthy, reliable, old call-maker, are bought
in Melbourne. Our mining revival in Victoria is thus
owing to British capital owned and employed by Vic-
torians. May Igo further and assert that the share-ex-
port trade is bigger than the butter-export business, and
employs more people in mining and its allied industries
than are employed in farming. Here follows a word in
favor of the broking cult. Lots of people have been able,
by selling shares in London, to get money to pay 1893
calls here. Yet no State has ever given a broker a bonus,
or if it has I’ve been on ’Change when the Treasury
officer called with the cheque. Don’t, please, hurl that
oumbrous assegai “ Foreign Capital ” at me. Aus-
tralia is till eternity pawned to Foreign Capitalists,
and what does it matter if the “ F.C.” garner a few wild-
cat mines along with some of our best securities ? Some
wild-cats can become domesticated, the Great Boulder to
wit—and some tame ones go very wild, witness Bayley’s
Reward. John Bull always pays too much for his foreign
shares because he applies a British percentage test to a
foreign value. Bother the butter and boot bonuses !
They exist because politicians exist, and I’ll make one to
start a holocaust of members of Parliament, who, by
over-mortgaging Australia, have left us a mere equity of
redemption in the best and brightest land (pardon me,
Colonel Bell) on God’s footstool. —Yours, G.D.M.
* * * *
It transpired in the big Tasmanian Equity
suit that defendant John Henry owed the Bank
of V.D.L. £31,000 when that institution went bung.
The River Don Trading Co., practically another
of Henry’s concerns, had about £31,700 on the
V.D.L. slate. The bank agreed to take £12,000 in
cash in liquidation of John Henry’s £31,000, but the full
amount was not forthcoming, so the now-disputed shares
were pledged as collateral security for the balance.
According to the reported evidence the Don Trading Co’s
estate has panned out something less than a color to the
dish. The true inwardness of the Private Enterprise
banking failures will probably never be fully realised until
sunrise on Judgment Day.
* * * *
The last half-yearly statement of Melbourne’s
big butchering firm, T. K. Bennett & Woolcock Ltd., pro-
vides some interesting figures. In the half-year’s trade it
handled 2301 cattle, and 68,802 sheep, besides pigs and
calves. Takings for the half, £72,349. Since the forma-
tion of the co. during boom-time, it has returned to share-
holders the whole amount of subscribed capital less 2d.
a share.
* » * *
David Syme, of the Age, is gradually yielding
to the apparently inevitable Linotype, but the prospect of
increasing the ranks of the unemployed stays his hand.
At present he is chiefly engaged in furthering his scheme
of embarking in tobacco-growing on a big scale at Lily-
dale, and in this he sees a way of introducing the Lino-
type at the Aoe office. “ Comps.” and others who will
have to make way for the machine will be drafted on to
the tobacco-farm. It appears to be the only way out of
the difficulty, and will be an object-lesson for the pro-
prietors of the remaining big Australian newspapers. By
the way, Bondurant, the imported Vic. tobacco-expert,
states, as an established fact, that wherever the citrus
thrives the soil is eminently fitted for tobacco-growing.
* * * *
Potatoes are selling in Tasmania at 375. 6d. per
ton, and cauliflowers in Melb. at Is. 2d. a doz. Go on the
land !
Melb. stock-salesmen, in view of past experi-
ences, have now a sort of trade-protection society amongst
themselves. No buyer is allowed longer than a week’s
credit. If he does not pay to time word is passed round
and the defaulter’s name is chalked up on a black-board
at each seller’s stand. The system has already wiped out
several (financially) weak carcase-men.
* * * *
The shilling restaurants in Melbourne are almost
things of the past, the threepenny-course system hav-
mg practically wiped out the higher charge. Nearly
every city pub. now goes in for catering on the 3d. racket
(glass of beer another 3d.), and a poor trade is done in
any house where 500 meals are not served daily. Four
pubs, each cater for 700 to 1500 people daily at 3d. a head,
and are making money at the price.
* * * *
The question of the amalgamation of the two
rival Manly ferry co’s will be finally settled this week.
Statement of accounts shows that the now-comparatively-
resourceless Co-operative Co. has little choice in the
matter, it having lost £1219 during the half-year. Not
possessing the fighting-power of the other co., it has
either to walk into the old co.’s mouth or be put in with a
fork.

The bank-rate throughout Australia is 3 per
cent. Up to lately, it was 3J4 in Sydney and Brisbane,
while Melb. controlled all the rest at 3. Now the clog of
money is expected to reduce it in Melb. to 21.
* * * *
Northern inland towns hate Townsville. This
is why. After 25 per cent, duty had gone to the hated
Southern Govt., 22 tons of machinery paid £33 or 30s. a
ton to pass from the boat to Townsville station. Carriage,
£5 ; 10s. a ton lighterage ; 4s. a ton wharfage ; 2s. a ton
harbor dues, &c., &c., &c., ad infinitum.

The new fuel-economiser shortly to be adopted
on Vic. railways, is said by department experts to solve
also the spark-danger problem. The patent should be
worth something if, as is claimed, the locomotives emit
neither sparks nor smoke and burn 20 per cent, less fuel.

B.H. Proprietary monthly div. swallows up
£48,000, which no other mine in the world is touching by
long chalks.
About 150 processes for cheaply treating sul-
phides are yearly submitted to the Proprietary. Most
come from silver cranks in various parts of the world.
At present, the Proprietary, having gathered the experi-
ence of several Hill co’s with their various concentrating
plants, is thinking out a scheme whereby it can rake a
profit off very low-grade sulphides.
* * « *
B.H. Proprietary, which hitherto has not
separated its gold and silver, exporting the metals as dor6
bullion, has now made the necessary additions to the Port
Pirie plant for the separation. Another loss to the metal-
brokers on t’other side of the world, to whom the co. in
the old days, especially before Patton came, was a mag-
nificent gold-mine.
* * * *
B.H. Proprietary is getting out plans for a big
concentrating mill; and the Central (Ashcroft’s Process
Co.) is having a plant built in S.A.
* * * *
Broken Hill Water Supply Ltd. held a special
meeting in Melb. and decided to open a London office and
register; chairman Drysdale frankly owned up to the
object (or, at any rate, the looked-for result)--the enhanc-
ing of the value of shares. There was a mild protest that
to incur an expenditure which could not yield revenue
was not what investors would regard as economy ; but
the proposal was carried hands down. Meantime, thirsty
Broken Hill pays ss. per thousand gallons for the water
(besides 20s. per annum for rent of each meter, supplied
by the co. in every case), and curses the day when it took
this brazen-faced monopoly to its arms.
* * * *
Block 14 balance-sheet, issued last week, showed
a profit for the half-year (to March 31) of £16,629 15s. sd.
Of this £lO,OOO was distributed in two divs., and the
balance (profit and loss account) was increased from
£BOB2 9s. to £13,535 10s. 2d. The co. estimated its liquid
assets on March 31 at £43,526, including £22,507 ore and
concentrates on hand, and £13,709 estimated surpluses.
Liabilities at same date, £25,668.
Block 14’s operations are now running into big
figures. For the half-year : Ore raised, 30,307 tons; ore
purchased at the mine, 19,078 tons. Ore concentrated
(included, of course, in the foregoing), 42,973 tons sul-
phides and 2912 tons second grade carbonates. At the
smelters (Pt. Adelaide) : 5790 tons sulphide concentrates,
641 tons carbonate concentrates, 3162 tons carbonate ore
from the mine, 3401 tons slimes (zinc concentrates) —2301
tons purchased ore (more than three-fourths sulphides)
and other small lines, in all 15,515 tons, yielding 4765 tons
bullion, which contained 369,3130 z. silver. This propor-
tion of sulphide ore and concentrates could not be main-
tained but for the preliminary roasting which Mr. Green-
way introduced. This last half-year the average quantity
concentrated was 1765 tons weekly; previous half, 1291
tons. Previous half-year’s smelting was 11,352 tons.
Further large additions to roasting and smelting plant
are being made.
Block 14 directors are gradually writing-off the
first-cost of the mine smelters, now out qf use : £1176
14s. 3d. comes off this half. This plant, which, a few years
ago, was valued at £lo,ooo—it was set down at £6OOO even
in Mr. Patton’s time—now appears in the balance-sheet as
an asset worth £2OO.
Probably the most important item in the Block
14 report is general-manager Greenway’s reference to
zinc recovery. All along, before he was connected with this
co., Mr. Greenway combated the prevailing opinion that
the zinc market is so unstable as not to be worth troubling
about; consequently he has delved deep into the subject.
Result: “We can produce, from certain classes of sul-
phide ore, zinc concentrates of a character which the
European zinc-smelters are able to purchase on terms that
afford us a small profit, after paying all expenses of treat-
ment and shipment. We have shipped and received the
returns for about 250 tons of these concentrates ; further
small shipments are in transit, and our ordinary concen-
trating operations now embrace their production at the
rate of 50 to 100 tons weekly. Laboratory experiments
have demonstrated that the treatment of these concen-
trates may be advantageously carried out further before
shipment, and we are steadily following up investigations
in this direction, which promise to enable us to produce a
zinc product having a value that will add to the company’s
profits.”
Satisfactory trials of the British concentrating
plant has been made. The original intention to run it on
carbonates (of which there has been satisfactory develop-
ments lately) has been abandoned, in consequence of the
new arrangement made with the Burnham syndicate.
Carbonates are, however, being extensively broken. The
arrangement with the syndicate begins on August 1.
Adelaide shareholders in the South are credited
with being responsible for the movement for the sale of
that mine. Price mentioned about a quarter of a million.
The Ashcroft people would buy; the South adjoins the
Central, now held by that co. The big Melb. broking
holders are reported to be opposed to the sale. A quarter
of a million is a high price ; but the South is a big pro-
perty—the biggest lead property on Broken Hill, exclud-
ing the Proprietary. m t ,
Value of the lode at the 615 ft. level in Block 10.
First workings: l6oz. silver, 241 per cent, lead, 26) per
cent, zinc ; second : 15oz. silver, 20 per cent, lead, 28 per
cent, zinc; third : 17oz. silver, 21 per cent, lead, 27 per
cent. zinc. A hundred feet above this the average is
about loz. silver, and 2 per cent, lead higher. Captain

trating mills, leading to frequent delays.

concentrating mills during the third week in May.

trates, bulking-69'2 per cent, lead, 9oz. silver, 5'4 per
cent. zinc. Block 14 : 2369 tons crude, producing 781
tons concentrates and slimes, “ of the usual fair silver and
lead assay.” Junction : 410 tons crudes, producing 100
tons first quality concentrates (62 per cent, lead, 290 z.
silver, and 9'7 per cent, zinc), 70 tons seconds, and 40 tons
slimes (heavy in zinc). South : 1379 tons crudes (30£ per
cent, lead, soz. silver, and 18 - 8 per cent, zinc), producing
506 tons concentrates (68 T per cent, lead, 7’3oz. silver,
and 7’B per cent. zinc).
• * * *
Junction North crowd whoop that their mine
is making £lOOO a week profit. If so, divs. are within
early call.
* * • •
North, after getting what was supposed to be
the Junction lode at 500 ft. deep, is driving West, still
looking for it, passing on the way through quartzite with
patches of sulphide in it. Heaven only knows why it
need be so, but it is reported that “ there is without
doubt a strong body of ore at this point which, when cut
into, should prove of considerable width.”
* * * *
The lode in the Junction North is lending itself
admirably to market operations ; intrusions occur and
the lode “ makes” again in the most convenient way. Not
that the management lends itself to the process; but
a few Broken Hill speculators govern the market
in this stock, and, being on the spot, they take care that
all these variations, common to the Broken Hill lode, are
known in the cities. The ore broken for shipment assays
38oz. silver and 38 per cent, lead—an improvement upon
what was mined at first.
The Junction is easing off its crude ore ship-
ments. For the third week in May 393 tons was sent to
Dry Creek and 191 tons to Pt. Pirie ; and of second-grade
ore 134 tons to Block 14. The product from the mill (210
tons from 410 tons ore) consisted of 100 tons giving 290 z.
silver, 62 per cent lead, and 9'7 per cent, zinc; 70 tons
seconds and 40 tons slimes—altogether an excellent result.

A move in Pinnacles presently.

Cobar, probably the best N.S.W. goldfield, had
the smallest average per ton for ’95, viz., lOJ^dwt.

The Bulletin hears that a cwt. of stone (picked
out of 80 tons) from the old Pride of the Brook claim,
Stewart’s Brook, N.S.W., gave 102 oz. A hundred tons at
grass is expected to go an ounce per ton independent of
the cwt. of specimens.
* * * *
Mr. Roos, a prominent Inverell storekeeper, has
satisfactorily tested the English market for N.S.W. dia-
monds. Included in a 700-carat packet sent to London
through the 8.N.5.W., the other day, was one most flaw-
less stone of nearly three carats, a white octagon of mar-
vellous symmetry, which will lose little in cutting.
« * * *
From Hannan’s;—•“ I counted the number of
applications for exemption posted up on the board outside
the court-house to-day, and found they numbered fifty.
When one considers how this district is now split up,
there being warden’s offices at White Feather, Broad
Arrow and Kurnalpi, the number is significant. It pans
out that, excepting a few like the Boulder, all the other
shows about Hannan’s are being shepherded, and were
never intended, when taken up, for the purpose of win-
ning gold from the earth, but from poor, confiding Jay
Bull.”
* * * *
A damning fact about Hannan’s now is the
absence of English buyers. Few shows are going off, and
prospectors now only ask hundreds for what in Oct. last
they wanted thousands.
* * * *
Wired from Coolgardie: “It was thought the
Bank of England mine had demonstrated its extreme
richness; but in sinking below the 60ft. level, gold was
struck rendering previous displays insignificant. Two feet
of lode consists of about one-third gold, which occurs in
coarse particles in the stone, which is rich in fine gold
alone. Nothing equal to the bottom of this shaft has been
seen on the field. The lode covers the whole bottom of
the shaft, while 2ft. of it are wonderfully rich.” Just
think of it. One-third of a two-foot lode gold— i.e., there
is Bin. of solid gold in the lode. Things can’t go on like
this. The time must come when they’ll strike something
better than even solid gold. They will probably yet hit a
slatey formation which will be found to consist of Bank of
England £lOOO-notes.

Wired that at the Golden Run mine, Cool-
gardie, there is at grass a heap of 170 tons of quartz which
will average 40oz. per ton. Forty times 170 is 6800, and
6800 oz. at £4 means £27,200. Good.
* * » *
It is estimated that 70 per cent, of the capital of
the Westralian mines floated in London has gone in pro-
motion expenses. In other words, £140,000 is spent in
proving to the public that a mine is worth £200,000.
* * * »
The big Eastern dailies are beginning to fore-
shadow the time when English co’s working Coolgardie
and Murchison mines will be in the throes of liquidation.
It would be easy to prove that J. Bull was induced to put
up his money into these wild-cats by the Australian news-
papers which now regret his amazing credulity. As the
sensational but lying wires appeared in these papers they
were carefully scissored out and sent to London, where
they were reprinted in prospectuses, many of which were
made up almost entirely of clippings from the big Sydney
and Melb. papers, the “greatness” and truthfulness of
which papers were emphasised by the promoters. No
wonder some Westralian journalists have become rich
enough to retire from the Press ! The Eastern dailies
have innocently played their part in the W.A. boom
without participating in the boodle.
* • * *
London West Australian Review estimates
W.A. divs. paid out of “ mining profits ” in ’95 at £130,600,
and divs. from “financial and exploration companies” at
£512,616. £130,000 from the mines and £512,000 from
romancing about the mines ! Total mining divs. in ’94
and ’95, £199,000, of which two mines contributed
£178,000 (Bayley’s, £130,000; Great Boulder, £48,000) ;
total divs. from first-robber syndicates, £568,000 —
in other words, for every £1 of dividend extracted from
W.A. in gold, more than £3 are got by market-booming
and market-rigging.
• * * *
The opinion of an old and experienced Austral-
asian digger who knows Westralia well, expressed to a
Bulletin man, is that W.A. will never go bung for the
practical miner, who will always find a decent return for
honest and systematic work. The field for prospecting is
a tremendous one, and even in the best-known country,
where the richest gold runs in small veins in conjunction
with alpiost barren larger bodies, small parties who can
handle tools, and who know their way about underground,
will step in after big Hinglish companies have gone pop,
and fatten on the remains.
* * * *
Coolgardie should have been named Salina.
Salt everywhere—salt in the earth, salt in the water, and
many of the mines are much salted.
W.A. wire hummers should not grow so enthusi-
astic over first crushings, for is it not written that the
first shall be last ?
Wild-cats will surely destroy the M.I.C.E.
business now flourishing in W.A.
* * * *
Kalgoorlie : “ Splendid specimens found at the
Kurnalpi Consols mine. The stone is white quartz.”
“Too much white quartz” is the cruel, cold-blooded
truth about almost the whole country.
* • * *
Melbourne Australasian tells of a Maldon
(Vic.) man who went away to Coolgardie to seek his for-
tune. He didn’t find any reef out there that was good
enough to pay expenses, but the man who bought the
house he had occupied at Maldon found a profitable lode
in the back garden. The irony of it.
* * * *
Mr. De Rose, one of the pioneers of the Boulder
group of mines at Hannan’s, had a big “ send-off ” by
the Kalgoorlie Club. “The opinion was generally ex-
pressed that to the Brookman brothers and De Rose the
present great prosperity of the field was due. They stuck
to the Boulder group of mines when all the world cried
they were no good, until they had made their own for-
tunes and the fortunes of thousands of others.” The
Bulletin wants to say something on behalf of the world.
At that particular time, as far as any man could see, these
mines were no particular good. The writer happened to
be on them, and saw that they were spending many thou-
sands of pounds, and had no ore in sight that would pay
to crush. Many months after the Great Boulder was
placed in London the shares went down to 55., while men
who saw the mine would not give that modest amount for
a dozen of them. Suddenly the Boulder struck a rich
formation where it never expected to find one, and has
since become a noble mine. That is the simple truth,
which lots of people who know all about it are carefully
trying to conceal. Nothingsucceeds like luck. There are
hundreds of men in the same districts who have done far
more heroic work than the Boulder crowd, but, having
had less luck, they are never heard of.
* « « «
Peculiar that the S.A. Govt, refuses to publish
geologist Brown’s special three-months’ sniffing report on
the W.A. fields? Brown is a trifle too cautious for
Adelaide boomers, which perhaps explains.
* « * *
Long Tunnel, Walhalla (Vic.) is in the curious
position of having to prospect its old workings to pick up
the run of stone yielding so well to the L.T. Extended.
Theory is that in ’76 this make of stone was missed in the
eager development of the main shute, and should it exist,
the Long Tunnel will have a clear 1000 ft. of new country
to work —which would mean many more years’ life to this
“dandy” div.-payer.
* * * *
Last Chance United, Ballarat, past half milled
8640 tons for 26160 z. 16dwt.—rather a nasty drop on 17,879
tons for 36470 z. 3dwt. for the previous period. Divs. also
pinched from £3500 to £lOOO. Given a couple of dwt. im-
provement in the large body of stone met with in the
north drive, the Last Chance may be expected to do better
this term.
* * » *
The disappointing United Brothers, Glen Wills
(Vic.), at last seems in a fair way to pay occasional divs.
Past half’s milling, 3833 tons for 25530 z. 9dwt., and also
119 tons concentrates to net £l4 16s. per ton. Profit,
£2572 9s. Bd., used to wipe out an 8 p.c. loan from an ex-
chairman, so the co. starts the new term with a clean
sheet. Pretty well all the free-milling stone is exhausted,
and the larger quantity of refractory ore put through has
heavily increased concentrating ex’s on the previous half;
while to mine the stuff has cost considerably more—in-
creases that are not encouraging to buyers of these shares,
inflated to £2, but which have lately eased to under
20s. A late 360-ton crushing has given 3270 z., 250 z.
from plates, and 15 tons concentrates.
* • * *
Sir Henry Loch, Ballarat (Vic.), 311 oz. 15dwt.
from 568 tons, speaks well of an early div.-cut. Stone
chiefly from the 1400 and 1509 ft. levels, and averages 1
to 2ft. wide. Tributors monopolise the No. 2 shaft work-
ings. Co. had enough cash for a div. last week, but pre-
ferred to create a reserve with it. Milling dirt in sight
estimated to go £1 in divs., and there is still a large area
to expose. The Loch’s luck has given a fillip to the old
Band, which any day now may cut the former’s run of
stone, and send its shares up to extravagant prices.
* * * *
To date, North Prentice, Rutherglen (Vic.),
owes its backers £59,000, but the prospects look good
enough for divs. before the year closes. Gutter over
400 ft. wide, and opened up 1000 ft. without meeting the
eastern reef. When properly drained, there is a big area
ready for pannelling, promising good things. Gold
won past half, £1398.
» * » *
If Victoria keeps up her present rate of progress
throughout the year, the gold-yield for ’96 will he 100,000
oz. ahead of that for ’95. The increase for the first quarter
of ’96 was almost as large as the whole yield of Westralia
for the same period.
* * * *
Companies are being floated daily in Launceston
on the strength of good reports from the W. Coast, and
the shares are rushed by the public. Melbourne money
is coming over freely ; but for this Tas. would never
stand the strain. The Hercules mine has now a
large litter. Some of her pups : North Hercules, South
Hercules, East Hercules, West Hercules, Central Her-
cules, King Hercules, Queen Hercules, New Hercules,
Hercules Consols, Hercules Extended, Barlen Hercules,
and Hercules Number One.
* * * *
Anything bearing the name of Hercules on its
prospectus, even if “ Extended” unto seventy times seven,
and as far removed from the Hercules as anybody can
remember, is strong enough just now to agitate the Tas.
share-market. Later on some of ’em will smell strong
enough to kill at 16,000 miles range.

* * * *
Tas. gold-stocks, generally, are down; silver
ditto up. When the little silver-boom is dead and buried
gold shares will arise from the tomb and enjoy a flutter.
By the way, when Lefroy field booms Beaconsfield slumps,
and vice-versa.
* » * *
The Tasmania, Beaconsfield, is calling tenders
for timber for the new shaft to be commenced shortly. A
rumor was afloat that the mine is to be lighted by elec-
tricity. At present she consumes about 1000 candles per
24 hours, which means, roughly, a yearly light-bill ol
£lOOO.
* * * *
The water from the Tasmania mine pulls the
nails out of a pair of boots in something less than no time.
A drip on to an iron rail in one of the drives cut it clean
through in a few months. The greatest flow comes from
the West face in the low level—72oft. Recently the
pumps stopped work for a small repair. In three hours
the water rose four stopes high. Taking into considera-
tion the mighty rushing river she has to cope with, the
Tasmania is a long way the most wonderful mine in Aus-
tralasia.

• • * *
A Bulletin correspondent : —Since the opening
of Fermoy (N.Q.)field £26,000 worth of opal has been sold
locally, besides parcels taken away. Lately opals have
been found at greater depths, some very large finds be-
tween 20ft. and 40ft. —in some cases going through two
bottoms. The other day some of the most brilliant stone
yet seen was unearthed at 25ft., and at 28ft. was found a
solid piece of opal weighing 21b. and containing streaks of
excellent gem. About 250 men are on the field and have
in hand perhaps £4OOO worth of good opal.
* * * *
The boss was going through the store-book.
“ Seems to me those chaps you started sinking on that
leader are using a lot of fuse,” said he to his shift-boss.
“ See here—Monday 5 coils, Tuesday 7 coils, Wednesday
10 coils, Friday 13 coils—why, they must be firing all day !
We’d better go over and have a look to-morrow.” Next
day the shift-boss reports : “ Those chaps came to me,
said they couldn’t sink no further—said the fuse wasn’t
long enough. I went over; found they used a coil to
each hole and lit the hole, and scooted. The shaft was
deeper than the fuse, so they couldn’t sink no more, they
said. I showed the idiots how to light their holes.”
“ We’d better go over now,” said the boss. When they
got there they found the two “sinkers” on their stomachs,
with candles tied to long whip-sticks, trying to light their
holes; one had succeeded, t’other hadn’t. They were
just in time to pull t’other back from the edge.

SOCIETY
COOLALTA
The Rev. Lush, of Auckland, M.L. (The
Bulletin isn’t personally acquainted with Rev.
Lush, but it takes off its hat to him and meta-
phorically drops threepence in the plate of his far-
off tabernacle) :
The first thing needed to check the growing immorality
of the town is the re-organisation or suppression of all
Bands of Hope, Christian Endeavorers, and every society
which takes young people out at night. The religious
bodies are seriously to blame for keeping nurseries for
prostitutes, as many of these societies have, in cases, been.
That is a section of the naked and unadorned
truth, though it is quite unusual to hear it from a
parson. A year or two ago, by the way, another
Maorilander wrote to this paper on the same sub-
ject. “ Please send me some indecent photo-
graphs,” he said ; “I am tired of the girl who was
led astray while coming home from choir-practice.”
The photos, were not sent, and The Bulletin has
never quite decided what the writer was driving
at, but, in a general way, he and Parson Lush
seem to be of the same mind. And the world in
general is also getting dead-tired of the girl who
“fell” on her way home from the choir or the
Band of Hope or the Christian Endeavor.

* » * *
North Melbourne police-court is a sort of
haven of refuge and solatium for all sorts of sex
cases, from the maintenance-cabbage to the affilia-
tion-cauliflower. Various reasons have been given
for this partiality, but the cynic is apt to see,
from an incident in the Prahran court last week,
the true causa-causans. After hearing evidence in
an affiliation case, the Justices made an order for
Bs. per week and allowed £1 Is. costs. The solicitor
immediately arose and informed the Court that in
every case of this sort the N. Melb. Court allowed
,£3 3s. The plea had sufficient weight to induce
the Prahran beaks to split the difference. The
Bulletin now wants to know whether the North
Melb. Court is chosen by the complainant or by
her solicitor.
* * * *
W. J. Nott writes to Adelaide Register
suggesting a re-naming of Australian provinces.
New South Wales he wants shortened to “Wales”;
Queensland is to apply to the South only, and
N.Q. is to be called “Beatrice.” Westralia is
sub-divided into “ Arthur ” and “ Alberta ” ; and
S.A. into “Albert” and “Alfred.” Mr. Nott is
evidently entitled to crawl on all-fours with the
other British gentleman who, when Duke Clarence
visited Australia, begged a glass of dirty water
from the chambermaid who tended his bedroom,
and drank it! Of the two, The Bulletin rather
prefers the other British gentleman. His crawl
stopped on the premises.
* * * *
George Ashton has an article in the Studio
on the Victorian Art Academy, comparing Melb.
art “ bosses ” with those of Sydney, much to the
disadvantage of the former. George’s brother
Julian (a practising artist, by the way) is one of
the art-bosses of Sydney, and, being the only real
technicalist among the N.S.W. National Gallery
trustees, is Sydney’s most powerful art-patron —
and herein lies a substantial artists’ grievance.
Parkes’s picture, painted by Mr. Julian Ashton,
hangs in N.S.W. National Gallery. Dibbs’s pic-
ture, by Percy Spence, also hangs there—it was
placed there while Dibbs himself was in
power as Premier. Hunt’s portrait of old Jack
Robertson, tendered to the Gallery as a gift, was
denied admission on the alleged ground that it was
artistically unworthy. But who passed judgment
on its artistic worth, and on that of Julian Ash-
ton’s Parkes —also on Julian Ashton’s unattractive
“Prospector”? Perhaps the painter did not sit
on the tribunal himself, but he was of the tribunal
and had a seat at the back of the tribunal. No
court should hear the case of one of its own
members. “Old Jack’s” picture, refused admis-
sion to the Gallery, has now found a resting-place
in N.S.W. Parliament Houses. You see Old Jack
is dead. But if Old Jack were alive and in office
as Colonial Secretary, and his friends sought to
hang Hunt’s picture of him in the Gallery, how
long, do you think, would the trustees resist ?
*** . *
There are no nudities in the winter exhi-
bition of the Victorian Artists’ Society, opened
this week. Vic. painters can’t afford to pay
models, and handsome women who will sit for the
love of art are few. Hence the plurality of gloomy
“Evening” landscapes in the show.
*.* * *
The married woman possessed of separate
property and a little dishonesty has hitherto
eluded the meshes of “attachment of the person,”
fraud-summonses, company calls’proceedings, and
other privileges of the gaolable male. But Vic.
J u . st i C e Hodges has found in some musty law
books a lasso which will catch the indebted female
who wishes to fly to Westralia. It is now a kind
of cousin-german to the ne exeat colonia, but
formerly, as the capias, was an ordinary way of
starting legal proceedings, as Fielding and his
creations could testify. It was practically abolished
by the statutes for the abolition of imprisonment
for debt. Whether the Judge’s law will hold
water is another matter.
Mrs. BROWN-POTTER, as Charlotte Corday.
Photo, by Falk, N.Y.
In Judge Windeyer’s absence, N.S.W.
Divorce Court appears to give fuller scope to the
ordinary practising barristers; and in connection
with that fact the question has been started : Can
a R.C. barrister plead for a petitioner in a suit for
divorce? Of course, the question is one of con-
science and ecclesiastical discipline, not of law.

Prof. Harper, 8.D., the dour pietist who
runs Melb. Presbyterian Ladies’ College (whence
sprang Melba) the other Saturday enjoyed the
felicity of seeing one of his old pupils carry off the
local Lady Cyclists’ Road Race in pronounced
bloomers. Which fact will no doubt be proudly
chronicled in the next prospectus of the college.
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley’s de-
cision in the H. B. Muglisto fair affords
scope for a delicate hair-s pitting argu-
ment. The C.J. held that although he
could disbar Mr. Mugliston as a N.S.W.
barrister and admit him to the roll of
solicitors he had not the power to disbar
him as an English barrister, Mr. Muglis-
ton having been called to the English
bar before being called in N.S.W. The
fact that an Irish barrister had been in
similar circumstances admitted to the
N.S.W. solicitors’ roll a short time ago
the Chief Justice set down to an oversight
on the part of the Court. But the argu-
ment which his Honor will soon be asked
to consider is, that under the Act the
Court may, and must, if he presses, disbar
Mugliston as a N.S.W. barrister and
admit him as a N.S.W. disbarred bar-
rister to the N.S.W. solicitors’ roll. There
may be reasons why a barrister should not
take the rank of a competent solicitor by
mere transfer, but these are apart from
the intention of the Act under which the
Court works.
* * *
“The experts in the Dean case
were wrong,” said Premier Reid the other
day. The experts were right— right on the
evidence, right on the probabilities, right
on everything but the issue. Dr. Sydney
Jones and Dr. Manning reasoned logi-
cally, and were wrong. Lawyer Rogers
guessed illogically, and was right. In the
Dean case, two and two did not make four
—they made seven and a-half. But all
the same, N.S. Wales is not going to sub-
stitute trial by instinct for trial by reason ;
it still intends to declare that two and
two make four and no more or less.
The Dean case “experts” pulled up the line
which wobbled and got no fish ; the non-“ ex-
pert P u |] U P, || ne 'J 110 * 1 <hdn t wobble,
and found the flathead. Cause why: the lines
were tangled. But every fisherman m the country
still goes for the jigging line just the same,

One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
(ieorge-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*

difficulty just now is how to get his money in. In
the recovery of debts, especially in circumstances
of difficulty or delicacy, Holds worth & Co., 101
Swanston-street, Melbourne, meet with singular
success.*

Bulletin fable : One day a great and rich
official was walking alone in the wilderness, com-
muning with nature and allowing his thoughts to
dwell upon charity and kindred subjects, when he
came upon a poor fellow who lay upon the ground
famishing for water and food. The sufferer told
his story with much difficulty. He was a bush-
man, had long been without sustenance, and was
like to die if help were not offered him. “My
poor fellow ! ” said the great official. “ I object to
spontaneous charity. The man who puts his hand
in his pocket to assist unfortunate beggars offends
against society by helping to keep alive an impro-
vident and useless class. But lam not a hard or
an ungenerous man—l will recommend your case
to the consideration of the Charity Organisation
Society immediately upon my return to town.”
Thereupon the great man went his way, followed
by the blessings of the bushman.

A May truism— “After the day comes the
knight.”
* * * *
At the recent Lytton (Q.) encampment an
elaborately-millinered officer pulled his charger
backwards on top of himself, when saluting, and
had to be carried off the field. To a stranger from
’Ome it was a new sensation to see a charge of
cavalry followed by some half-dozen orderlies,
with all their work set to reap the harvest of hats
lost en route,
» * * *
The Victorian Government, after all, has
determined not to abolish the honorary magis-
trate system, but it has evolved a plan which
heaps contempt upon selfpaid - beakdom, and
goes far to flatten out bench-packing. It has
appointed a few additional honoraries, but a noti-
fication has been issued that in future a Jaypee
can officially attend only at the court nearest to
his residence, and that he has no official locus
elsewhere. Consequently, if the nearest beak
can’t be “ squared ” there is nothing to be done.
• • * •
A case decided in England in 1852, and
known as R. v. Gerber, is interesting reading in
view of the bungle made by the Vic. Chief Justice
and Crown law officials over the Rapiport business.
In this case A, B, C, and D were indicted together
and pleaded. Before they were given in charge to
the jury, the Court allowed D to be removed from
the dock and examined as a witness against his
associates. The Judge said: “D is not on his
trial, and, therefore, no objection that his evidence
is inadmissible can prevail. . . That the witness
under the present circumstances is admissible has
been settled for more than a century.” So it
would appear that, despite the protests of counsel,
the Boulters’ evidence was perfectly admissible in
the Rapiport case, since they were not even
“ charged ” at the trial.
* * * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph, leader-writing
on “ Moral Poisoners,” defines the indecent in art
to be an exhibition “ representing scenes which,
if made up by the living bodies therein pourtrayed,
would call for prosecution.” Is this so? If so,
why are not the trustees and managers of public
gardens, museums, and galleries prosecuted and
sent to serve with the lately-convicted picture-
seller Green? What about “The Boxers”?
What about the “Venus de Medici”? What
about a thousand and one canvases known to
everybody? If living bodies were to pose in
gardens and galleries after the manner of these
statues and pictures they would admittedly render
themselves liable to prosecution. Hence there is a
difference between what living bodies may do and
what statues and pictures may be made to do, and
the definition which places both on one plane is
itself a sort of “moral poison.”
* * * *
The tick-trouble bids fair to dwarf the
rabbit-plague into insignificance. From the
Archer to the Bloomfield (N.Q.) are now only
remnants of herds which once numbered thou-
sands. A thousand bullocks were put into a pad-
dock on the Lower Burdekin, and in a few weeks
40'» survivors were marched off to be boiled down.
If driven the cattle die ; if left in the infected
paddocks they die also. The sweat-glands seem
to be completely destroyed, and the cattle are
often afflicted with panting like a dog. Horses
have been affected of late, and even human beings
are not exempt from the attacks of the latest of
Australia’s blessings.
In a case heard in Brisbane Supreme Court
the other day, in which the trustee had died with-
out leaving an heir, it was explained that deceased
had met his death by the explosion of a cask of
rum on which he was sitting. Old Jamaica could
never hold a candle to the real Mackay.

Two recent Brisbane police-court cases.
Blank, indecently assaulting his own daughter,
nine months’ imprisonment. Dash, a youth,
stealing £B, nothing else known against him, 12
months’ imprisonment. The old contrast!

By the tick plague in Queensland nearly
all the dairy cattle have been killed around Towns-
ville, and the people of that town have to use pre-
served and goats’ milk.

Lord Brassey has developed into a bathetic
blarneyer since his arrival in Victoria. Whenever
he is called upon to address an audience he does
A QUEEN’S-BIRTHDAY INCIDENT.
(Her “ Ladyship” teas receiving the congratulations of a domestic.)
“ Oh, mum ! I’m so glad you’re a real lady now.”
“Well, Maria, I hope I have always been a lady.”
“ Well, yes, mum; but nobody could ever call you it before.”
so with his mouth full of butter, so to speak. The
other day he remarked that—
Since he had had the honor of residing in Victoria he
had seen that which made him feel a great admiration for
the high ideals of life, the thirst for knowledge, the love
of the beautiful, the taste for culture and the arts, and
the enjoyment of authors of the best kind, which he saw
widely displayed in Melbourne.
And not one of his audience guffawed, probably
not one grinned quietly, at this outrageous irony !
High ideals of life ? Guvmentouse and the Austral
Salong. Thirst for knowledge ? Yes, concerning
the horse that will win the Cup. Love of the
beautiful ? Quite so—in the way of legs. Taste
for culture and the arts? Ask the artists struggling
to make a living in Melbourne. Enjoyment of
authors of the best kind? Book-Arcade Cole
alone sold 7000 copies of “ Trilby ” in a few
weeks. He has not sold as many copies of
Thackeray, Balzac, and George Meredith put
ANY DAY IN THE CITY.—“LINE IN TEERUPTED !”
together in a year. Baron Brassey’s butter of
flattery tastes of vitriol now and then.

A Home Reading Union is one of the
swell institutions of Melbourne. Some people can
never do anything on their own account. They
can’t even read a book now and then without
banding themselves together into a society for the
purpose, with president, vice-president, secretary,
and so on. Some of these days the Mutual Nose-
Blowing Union will be one of the most fashionable
clubs connected with Melbourne sassiety.

double rooms, and all on the top floor.
They are mostly artists. Why is this
thus ? ’’—Wanderer.

Answers to Correspondents .
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.
