Sydney, June 2, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda—
According to their own unsupported statement,
the “ Very Nicest ” people gathered together at
Sydney Centennial Park last Thursday afternoon
for the first meet of the Hampden Riding, Driv-
ing, and Cycling Club. That the “altogether”
was farcical goes without saying. When the
Nicest people let it go abroad in the penny-papers
that they will perform in a certain place at such-
and-such an hour, naturally there is a huge and
critical crowd assembled to greet them ; and if
they really objected to the aforesaid crowd, or had
brains enough to see that they were really inviting
it to roll up, they would make their preliminary
arrangements in a haunted chapel at midnight,
and not in the Town Hall before the reporters.
To an unprejudiced observer the maiden meet
of the Hampden Club looked like anything but
the “ nicest way of bringing the nicest people
together it rather suggested a cock-fight whither
my lord Tomnoddy hies in the company of Bill
Sikes. All shades of the community, from His
Ex. downwards, were represented, and it was
amusing to see how gingerly the officials, laid on to
see fair play, interfered with the populace (plainly
“out for the afternoon”) who treated the sacred
pavilion as if it were a Punch-and-Judy show, and
criticised the Hampden Clubites as if they were
waxworks. I don’t think our glorious Constitoo-
shun ever had a more brilliant day out than on
Thursday, when grimy little guttersnipes barred
the way everywhere, and big officials looked on
helplessly and wished to Heaven, no doubt, that
the Nicest people—immortal phrase !—wouldn’t
put them in so difficult a position.
So far as the club was concerned (but in that
variegated orowd the club was like a needle in a
bundle of hay) the occasion was dressy. Lady
Hampden (who drove) certainly looked like an
elderly governess in a black hat and glasses, but
the Acting-Govemoress was a stately monument
of velvet and fur, and the committee obviously
had its best bonnet on. I was pleased to see that
more women rode than biked. The Vice-Regal
damsels and two of the A.V.R. ditto (who are the
chief chums of the Guvmentouse girls) were on
bikes which they rode discreetly, all wearing dark-
blue serge coats and skirts and white sailor hats,
and accompanied by Capt. Egerton, A.D.C., in a
lizard-striped suit and grey felt hat. By the way,
both Capt. Egerton and Rupert Carington biked
in long trousers, buckled at the ankle, though the
club secretary’s legs presented a variegated appear-
ance, what with golf stockings, knee-breeches,
and so forth; the sec., however, is a plump and
pleasing person who need not hesitate when it
comes to base revelations. But evidently the
Govmentouse male biking-suit has to have a pair
of everyday trousers buckled at the ankle. Calves
and all that sort of thing are doosid bad form at
Govt. House, dontcherknow, as anybody might
guess if he summoned up a mental vision of
what a certain prominent young official would
look like scudding under bare poles.
Some of the lady-cyclists (“devils in trousers”
is their East End name) were pretty well turned
out, but most of ’em went wrong about the trim-
mings. The really-fashionable cyclist is as neat
and natty as a horsewoman, and a bow of ribbon
or a flower is as much out of place on a wheel as on
a saddle. One or two wore rather vulgar hats—
vulgar because out of place—and one with a blue
bow at the back of her neok looked as if she
wanted to be a poodle. Skirts were universal. A
wild rumor that one pair of bloomers had reached
the park was true enough, but the wearer gyrated
on the edge of the crowd and fled like a lost soul
at the approach of man. I thought the club
equestriennes were all bright instances of how
taking women look on horseback provided they sit
straight and don’t worry. One society woman on
a wide-awake moke with a bang tail sported a
snuff-colored habit of soul-stirring cut, and was a
noticeable figure because most of the riders
sported black habits—worn in one instance, that of
a plump society girl, to minimise the German-
sausage effect. There were not many horsemen
present; the hardest riders were away at a meet-
ing of the Sydney Hunt Club held at Canterbury
the same afternoon.
On dit that the roll of Hampden Club members
is not nearly as long as it was expected to be, and
that the Nicest people hung back from subscribing
because the committee has arranged matters so
that it can do pretty well what it pleases,
and no questions asked or answered ; but if any-
thing goes wrong with the works, members are
expected to put their hands in their pockets and
square things. Some of the nicest people don’t
oonsider that the nicest way of spending their
money, so they decide to merely look on until the
rules alter. But the Hampden Club, with its
open rendezvous in a people’s park, is utterly
doomed as a really swagger institution. Unless
the club purchases some of the wild and woolly
land surrounding the park, and fences it in 6ft.
high, and meets therein and drinks its tea and
makes unexpected descents into the park from
this vantage, the Hampden Club meets will always
be the cheap circus the first one was, which not
even a hollow square of School of Industry children
(impressively drawn up on Thursday to give an
air of God-bless-the-Squire to the assemblage)
could reduce to even the suggestion of a forelock-
pulling contest.
Which reminds me that the description of an
entertainment given by the Factory Girls’ Club,
in Sydney lately, reads exactly like an English
forehead-knuckling and bob-curtsey matoh. Surely
it must have been one of the “ very nicest ” of
functions, what with curates, judges, old ladies,
the National Anthem, and so on. And. it’s
amusing to note the proprietary airs of the “ nicest
people ” when having their names handed down to
posterity in the social columns of the penny papers
—they always employ sounding territorial designa-
tions in conjunction with their names. The
Blanks (of Blenheim), the Borndrunks (of Men-
tone) —and so on. But it never sounds really
baronial. In Australia such things only suggest
a sort of glorified edition of “ no connection with
over the way,” or the horror of Smith, the large
fat publican, lest he should ever be mistaken for
Smith, the poor little greengrocer.
On dit regarding the awful lack of “ punctuality,
the politeness of kings,” noticeable about Gov.
Hampden—who has laid up more committee-men
with influenza through keeping’em waiting in their
pumps on top of cold marble steps for weary
hours than any other Gov. on the continent —that
the reason of this serious inability on his Ex.’s part
to keep railway-time is said to be want of organisa-
tion at Govt. House. The incompetency of the
officials (who only want a bun and a pair of
goloshes to be a farcical comedy) led to a fearful
mix-up at the levee the other day—a number of
military big-wigs with cards of entree arrived
much before the time specified, but only just
escaped being too late, and at the Queen’s Birth-
day dinner I hear that the most aboriginal arrange-
ments had been made for allotting the seats at
table. And anyone who knows how sensitive the
nicest people are in matters of precedence will
appreciate the horror of the thing. This happy-
go-lucky style of arranging the most official and
formal gatherings —trusting to chance, as it were,
to shake the big potatoes to the top —looks as if
the diplomatic Wallington, at present out of har-
ness, were seriously wanted at his old post.
The Steamship Companies’ official ball .takes
place at the Paddington Town Hall on the 10th
July. Mr. E. A. Desjardins is hon. sec. to what
is always one of the liveliest invitation-dances of
the year. But the very next dance is the French
ball on June 15, which is working up to be a great
success.
The only moral I observed in. “ The Benefit of
the Doubt,” at Sydney Criterion, was that the
woman who drinks fizz on an empty stomach when
a plate of biscuits stares her in the face deserves
all that happens to her subsequently. It isn’t
kind of Pinero to set all the married people squirm-
ing reminiscently over a realistic picture of a
madly-jealous wife nagging and worrying and
casting out damaging insinuations as to the
genuineness of the complexion of hubby’s
Platonic lady friend, and when the husband shakes
her in the last act you heave a sigh of relief—even
though it is the charming Beryl Faber who is
thus being agitated, in a cloudy costume of scarlet
and greyish-green like an amateur attempt at a
Turner sunset.
The platonic lady (Mrs. Brough) wanted shaking
too, I thought, and so did her cast-iron Dutch
husband (Cecil Ward), and the platonic Titheradge
besides —but, if somebody shook Pinero, perhaps
he wouldn’t inflict such shocking family-jars on
people under the guise of original comedies. Mr.
Brough and Dot Boucicault, as Sir Peter and the
addle-pated egotist, were oases in the sandy desert
of jaw-jaw, and Kate Bishop made every braced-
up fat woman in the circle heave sympathetically
when she announced her intention of retiring to
take off her corsets. The “ Benefit of the Doubt ”
was smartly costumed. Mrs. Brough looked very
handsome in a smoked-ivory color silk, with an
irregular black check, made with immense sleeves,
and fancifully-faked with melon-pink silk covered
with fine white lace —black silk braces and sash-
ends lined with pink ; piquant pink toque and a
green velvet cape, embroidered with jet. It was a
very elaborate turn-out, but, at the same time, I
scarcely think a woman of the world would meet a
charge of alleged friskiness with such an air of
coquettish-challenge about her attire. Virtue in
the witness-box should run to snuff - color and
square-toes if it really wants to convince the jury.
Some parts of the “ Benefit of the Doubt ” re-
minded me of a recent painful Australian suicide
—especially the part where “Eraser, of Locheen,”
stalks moodily aside like a modern Hamlet when
his feather-brained wife only wanted slapping.
The unhappy lady, according to the sad story in
the newspapers, was a weak, warm-blooded —per-
haps mad—woman married to a painfully-respect-
able husband. And when one day she suddenly
and oddly confessed to him that she had veered
from the path of virtue he immediately took his
portmanteau and fled for a judicial separation
without making the most idle inquiries into the
facts of the case. From a safe and formal dis-
tance he wrote her a cold communication to the
effect that the Lord might forgive her if she
prayed hard enough—the inference being that he
(the husband) never could ; and the poor young
wife killed herself and ended the miserable busi-
ness. Nobody can deny that the husband did
what was entirely proper and respectable—and yet
mightn’t it be worth while for some of these severe
moralists to forgive a woman once or twice ?
Voluntary confession is generally a sign of future
love, honor and obedience —but how many men
are there who wouldn’t grab their valise and flee
from the penitent just the same as that husband
did?
The popular G. L. Goodman, whose immaculate
shirt-front is almost part of the entablature of
Sydney Lyceum, is rather “ spiked ” at the grossly-
exaggerated reports that have gone the rounds of
the papers re the huge inheritance he is down for
owing to the murder of his uncle, Mr. John Levy,
a wealthy London merchant. But as Goodie him-
self doesn’t know how much he inherits, it is
obvious that the amounts published are fairy-tales.
As heir-at-law Mr. Goodman inherits the real
estate, and the rest is divided between six people ;
but whether his legal share will be five, four, or
three figures is beyond his knowledge. Since the
publication of these huge fanciful amounts it has
been perpetual Hospital Saturday with “ Goodie,”
who has been besieged with people trying to divert
some of the cash into their own pockets —Poverty
Pointers, especially, almost arriving with shovels to
remove what they consider their share of the spoil.
Also, every woman whom Goodie ever advised to
mind the step expects a parure of diamonds for
her whack.
Admiral Bowden Smith, who was popular in
Australia, and certainly did his best to give the
Sydney girls a chance with the officers of his
fleet by piling Pelion on Ossa as regards flirty hops
and teas, verbally contradicts his good nature in
his paper on “ Two Years in Australian Waters,”
recently read in London. If Admiral Bridge had
growled about the folly of “ subs ’ marrying and
the doubtful wisdom of pensioning the widows of
mere boys it would have sounded en suite ; but
that Admiral Smith, who was so liberal with his
epaulettes whilst “down under,” should endeavor
to come between the Australian girl and her
natural prey, and protect the impressionable sub.
in the green ear, as it were, from her machinations
is indeed a right-about face. However, I piously
hope that all Admiral Smith’s reforms will come
to pass. When once the navy regulations make
wards-in-Ohancery of junior officers, the awful
spectacle of “garrison hacks” pretending to pal-
pitate at the advance of pimply midshipmen will
become a horror of the past. •
Diplomatically enough Admiral Smith puts in a
good word for Australian girls as wives and
mothers (Sir Precedent Scott might be listening),
and between Mr. Smith and Lady Jersey the in-
digenous female has been having rather a good
time of it on paper lately. By the way, Lady
Jersey must have regarded Sydney as her tem-
porary home, to write about it as she does in a
London paper. The sketch of her temporary home
which an ex-Govemor’s wife can draw “ is fortu-
nately bound to be a pleasant one,” says M.E. J.,
and yet it was during her reign that the memorable
barn-dance episode occurred, and that the news-
boys at a Govt. House treat called out to her
amiable spouse—“ Jersey, pass the pickles ! ”
Australian society, though it respects Lady
Jersey, didn’t quite love her : she was far too
downright in her style. Also, her habit of assem-
bling a lot of purse-proud ignoramuses in the red
drawing-room and talking over their heads about
the whichness of the what and then drawing into
her shell in plain disgust when somebody feebly
remarked that it was very ’ot, has not endeared
her memory, and her constant expeditions from
her temporary — very temporary —home left Govt.
House without a hostess for weeks. Gov. Jersey,
charmingly ugly, amiable, and unaffectedly popu-
lar, was the success of the brief Jersey regime.
Ada Crossley sends along a batch of press
criticisms —all “raining rubies and diamonds and
beautiful pearls,” as Svengali says—in praise of
the voice and method of the fair contralto. But
the best line, from a feminine point of view, is
where the scribe tells of the Victorian’s bell-bird’s
nobby appearance in a black gown, beautifully
designed in spangles, and the low bodice outlined
with pink chiffon, which proved very becoming to
her lovely complexion. And Miss Crossley was
standing beside Patti, gorgeous in blue and pink
brocade, when she got that notice. By the way,
had Miss Crossley a “lovely complexion” when
she left us ? I thought it was rather raspy. How-
ever, it is wonderful what England does for the
most sallow skin. It must be some inherent virtue
in the fogs.
A perfect deluge of “Trilby ” music comes from
W. H. Glen and Co. The “Trilby” waltz, by
Bernard Hill, includes all the weary old tunes
resurrected in “Trilby,” and is trifling and turn-
turn from a serious point of view ; nevertheless it
ought to be thankfully received by people who
like to dance to what everybody is whistling.
Much the same may be said of the limp “ Trilby ”
march by Svengali, introducing the air and
words of “Benßolt ” —but the “ Trilby ’Gavotte,
by A. E. Wilson, is a silvery measure sufficiently
pizzicato to be pleasing, and decidedly the best of
the “ Trilby ” bunch. Said bunch is all more or less
dedicated to Edith Crane, whose photo, on the
covers helps things along. The most grateful
features about the “Konowna” mazurka, by
Fannie Ryan, are that it isn’t dedicated to a
Governor and isn’t called the “ Trilby.”
Talking of “ Trilby ” somehow reminds me of a
yarn concerning the N.S.W. medicine-man whose
sudden disappearance has not yet been fully
accounted for. It was at the doctor’s house, one
evening. “What do you think of that?” said
the doctor to a he-friend, showing him the portrait
of a deliciously-refined and divinely-intelligent
face. The other man was entranced. “It is my
ideal of womanhood,” said the medicine-man.
“And she was -?” “A ballet-girl.” A
moving and suggestive tale, but, after all, the
doctor’s friend who tells the story was hardly
justified in sizing up a coryphee on her face alone.
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“ The Brasseys’ full-dress reception in honor of
Y.R.’s birthday proved a woeful take-down for
the loyal mob. 9.30 p.m. was the time named for
arriving in one’s gala garments, and a dance, of
the choicest birthday order, was taken for granted.
However, it soon became evident that the ball
theory had been founded on ignorance. There
was no ball, and no anything else in particular,
beyond a band that suffered from * Ben Bolt ’ on
the brain, and some cheap refreshments discernible
in the distance, but seldom available when a
hungry customer applied for shares. At 11 o’clock
Maggie Stirling mounted to the organ-gallery and
sang ‘God Save the Qi ee i,’ whereupon sassiety
shook the dust of the full-d /ess reception from its
angry skirts and went snorting away. The chief
mourners were a number of debutantes, whose grief
at not having been launched upon the sea of dissi-
pation was unaffectedly sincere. .A girl can’t be
said to ‘ come out ’ at a funeral of that kind.
“At South Melb. Town Hall, the other night,
a cooking-class for working girls was opened
under circumstances which must have shocked
local snobocracy. The Governor’s wife signified
her desire to encourage made dishes among the
humblah classes by strolling into the hall half-an-
hour before the appointed time. The first person
to discover the illustrious stranger cooling her
heels in solitude was the Mayoress, who nearly fell
dead before the apparition. Lady Brassey it
seemed, had got there early in order to smile upon
each individual working female as she came to
class, and having carried out this fell purpose her
Lx. bolted home to dinner. She didn’t wait to
appease her inner pangs with an amateur contri-
bution from the new school of cookery.
“Gov. Brassey and Lady have, more or less
gone on strike. Every tin-pot ceremony in Vic-
toria expected their presence, until at last thev
kicked with one accord. Recently the gloomsome
Gov. was asked to open the new operating-theatre
at a Melbourne hospital, whilst Lady B. was in-
vited to do something in the same direction at
Castlemame, and they put the negative on both
invitations, H.E. saying that vice-regal functions
must stop at a sawbones butchery-room How
ever, ex-Acting-Gov. Madden did not despise the
honor of taking Brassey’s leavings. In fact if an
oyster was to be opened in public, the Chief-
Justice would find it a proud privilege to
part the shells, and make a few appropriate re-
marks on the interesting occasion.
“ Mrs. Freeman Thomas comes up smiling
to the ‘ Political Economy ’ lectures at Melb
University. Previously the seekers after this par-
ticular kind of wisdom were few and scattered
but with the advent of the Gov.’s daughter on the
scene a strange reverence for the law of supply
and demand is in the air. The first row, reserved
for Toorak ladies of ‘ neglected and backward
education,’ begins to be fashionably attended
and the body of the hall is no longer bare. ’
“ The announcement that Mrs. George Laber-
touche intends to give a lecturette on palmistry in
a fashionable drawing-room has caused a mild sen-
sation. Mrs. L.’s return from England was
happily timed. The marriage-market is deplor-
ably slack, and an experienced palmist capable of
stating the intentions of the fair man or of fixing
the date when the dark man will come up to the
scratch, will certainly fill a long-felt want.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
A Sydney Chinaman has been fined £5 for
keeping a five-roomed house as a leprous offal-tip.
In that core of cancer dwelt 14 men and severai
polyandrie females. Does Australia love the
Chow ? It hugs him to its nodular heart.
Q. Ex-Chief Justice Lilley says he didn’t
write that letter to “My dear Ryan.” He wrote
several letters, covering some time, and “My
dear Ryan ” picked out little bits here and there
and hashed them into one coherent epistle which
he sent to the Sydney press as an apparent whole!!
Cabled that “A fearfulcatastrophe occurred
in Russia at a feast to celebrate the Czar’s coro-
nation. Fully 400,000 people attended, and a
mad rush took place, 1100 being killed and 450
injured.” Which roundaboutly recalls the cele-
brated rush at the free banquet given in Mel-
bourne under the auspices of L. L. Smith and Co.
to celebrate the Duke of Edinburgh’s visit to Aus-
tralia. The mob rushed the huge wine-tun and
stove it in. Nobody was killed, but many became
dead-drunk.
When the news of Baron Hirsch’s death
reached the Prince of Wales on his arrival at
Epsom on the Metropolitan day, he at once re-
turned to London by the special which brought
him down. It was immediately whispered that
he “had gone back to try and get hold of his
I O U’s,” of which it is supposed that the Baron
held a good many for considerable value. But
people will say ill-natured things. The pair were
great friends, and it is believed that the deceased
millionaire left H.R.H, a very substantial legacy.
An interesting side-light on the Sydney
Suppress. One day last week, the time of the
Full Court was taken up for a whole morning in
hearing an application, made by Barrister Heydon,
for the issue of a criminal summons against certain
members of a leading liquor firm, charged on
affidavit made by a private individual with
having cheated the excise-revenue. Ineffec-
tual application had been made to a magis-
trate for a summons, and, as it was technically
within the magistrate’s discretion to refuse, a
mandamus could not be applied for; hence the ap-
plication to the Full Court. Though the alleged
facts were set forth on oath in detail by the would-
be prosecutor, and though the daily-journal re-
porters were present in court, not a word about
this important affair, which was the means of post-
poning the Meagher case for a day or two, ap-
peared in any newspaper. Why ?
‘‘ O what a tangled web w e weave when first
we practise to deceive ! ” A tangled web indeed
was that spun by attorney Meagher, now struck
off the roll of N.S.W. solicitors for his tortuous
conduct in connection with the Dean case. In
delivering judgment the court went right out of
its way to explain that certain leading articles in
the Victorian papers were founded on the erroneous
assumption that Judge Windeyer’s celebrated re-
mark to the effect that he was as sure Dean
poisoned his wife as if he (the Judge) had seen
him doing it, was made before, instead of after,
the delivery of the verdict. While thus apolo-
gising for its learned brother, the court ought
really to have explained away His Honor’s unique
and immoral suggestion that the hesitant and
divided jury might recommend Dean to mercy—
i. e. , compound with its doubts (for it had not up
to then been able to agree) by recommending pri-
soner to mercy for a crime which, if he committed
it, was one of the last crimes in the world de-
serving of mercy. That suggestion was made, not
after, but before the conviction, and it constituted
the real root of the Dean-case agitation against
Judge Windeyer. But for that noxious and mde-
fensible suggestion it is altogether unlikely that
there would have been any effectual public move-
ment in the matter.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Odds about 16 to 4 that out-and-out Pro-
tectionist M‘Kinley will be the next U.S. Presi-
dent.
A big row is pending in a Victorian Public
Service secret society. Admission of outsiders the
cause.
“Lands” Minister Carruthers : “I do
not, unfortunately, own a foot of land in N.S.
Wales.”
S.A. Democrats are already organising for
next year’s Leg. Council elections. Chaffey Baker,
arch-fiend of the National Ass, is one of the retiring
candidates.
At Mutooroo (S.A.) there are 75 electors
on the roll, and at last general election not one
vote was recorded. The inhabitants were too
tired. Mutooroo is evidently the champion tired
town of Australia.
The Newcastle (N.S.W.) coal-strike posi-
tion is that the unfortunate miners, who are prac-
tically without funds, are being allowed, as on
former occasions, to stew in their own gravy. The
appeal made to England for funds will, of course,
be resultless.
Roughly speaking, Britain has lent Aus-
tralia, publicly and privately, upwards of
41400,000,000 outstanding at the present time, on
which Kangarooland sweats to pay interest to
the tune of something over 4117,000,000 yearly.
The dear old Mo. country !
The Argus leaders against socialism for-
cibly recall those sermons which refute objections
you never heard of before, and only leave new
objections in your mind. Charles Bright was em-
ployed by the Argus to write down Spiritualism,
but got converted. Is the present writer under-
going that process ? The vividness with which he
states the case of Lassalle, Blanc, and Marx is
alarming.
NEMESIS !
MRS. MURPHY: “ And sure ye lost all yer money in thim thavin'
companies!”
MRS. ROONEY: “ Yes, I did! But God’s good: I have one consola-
tion — my son Mickey is a warder out in Pentridge beyant.”
The N.S.W. Public Works Commission
sittings should have been open from the
public and press. Closed doors only convey that
there is something to conceal. Everything not
indecent should see the light of print. Perhaps
the Works revelations are indecent.
The Yic. Railway Department has just
realised that there is a big leakage somewhere
in the passenger-returns, and that the number
of tickets taken and the number sold show
a great discrepancy. The theory is that the enter-
prising clerk prints tickets on his own account.
One clerk is now charged with haying made a haul
of £3OO, and the weather-eye of the department is
being opened to a lot of unexpected things.
Two members of N.S.W. Public Works
Committee have died within the last six months.
The committee, fully manned, consists of the
fateful number 13. A hint for Governments in
future to nominate only members of the Opposi-
tion to the erstwhile much-coveted positions.
A devoted subject to the Argus :
In the United States I believe that the “ Stars and
Stripes ” is conspicuous in the schools and is saluted by
the scholars. Why should not the Union Jack be dis-
played in our State schools ?
Well, because Australia, like Yankeeland, has a
flag of its own.
Free cabs are explicitly forbidden at
Yic. elections, by Act of Parliament, and yet
there were as many as ever flying about at South
Melb. election. The drivers give their services,
and are content with a douceur —another example
of driving the coach and horses through an Act
of Parliament.
Mauger, the runner-up in the S. Melb.
election, was much-boomed by his conspicuous
connection with the Anti-Sweating League, but
the electors could not stomach his air of smug
Methodism and his tea-meeting principles. He
is a rabid cold-water man, and S. Melb. electors
don’t run to cold water.
N.S.W. Justice Simpson’s initiation of
a scheme —to be followed by a rule of court
enabling those suing in forum pauperis to do
so in reality, is the importation, at a very late
day, into N.S.W. law practice of a custom ages
old in France. There the Court supplies without
charge even paper and stamps to such suitors.
Something has gone wrong with the cheap
loaf which was to be supplied in N.S.W. under
the Foreign-trade regime. Bread has already gone
up once in Sydney, and, at latest advices, it was
seriously thinking of going up some more. And
nothing has come down to any serious extent by
way of compensation, save Brussels carpet and
jewellery.
Cabled that “Mr. Fort, formerly Lord
Loch’s private secretary, asserts that Mr. Rhodes
supported Dr. Jameson’s raid, as he had positive
knowledge that Kruger was arranging an anti-
English alliance. The intention of Dr. Jameson s
raid, says Mr. Fort, was merely to destroy the
Hollander-German cabal.’ Patriotic Rhodes! Yet
it would have suited his private business very
much to be able to jump on Kruger with both feet.
Also Rhodes and Co., until they were bowled out
by documentary and other evidence as liars and
conspirators, insisted that bandit Jameson acted
purely 11 on his own.” The mutual dislike of Eng-
lishman and German is about the only card left
for the Chartered Co. to play, and Mr. Fort is
playing it.
Lands Minister Carru-
thers in a recent speech at
Fort-street School, Sydney:
With that regret there must be
a feeling of satisfaction that the
Port-street Public School Boys’
Union had offered an opportunity
to the old scholars of the school
to pay a tribute of respect to the
memory of one who was the
founder of the Public school sys-
tem throughout the whole of the
Australian colonies. (Hear, hear.)
He said that advisedly, because
the work of the late Sir Henry
Parkes in regard to our system
of education had not been con
fined to New South Wales, for the
Act of which he was the author
had been adopted in every one
of the Australian colonies having
self-governing powers, with the
exception, perhaps, of Western
Australia.
The Bulletin respectfully
states just here that Minister
Carruthers is an igno-
ramus. South Australia
started the State school sys-
tem under a Board of Edu-
cation in 1851 ; Victoria did
the same in 1862; Tasmania
in 1854 ; Queensland in 1860.
Then N.S.W. in 1866 imi-
tated them and set up a
similar system and a similar
Council of Education. In
1872 Victoria created a
Public Instruction Depart-
ment under a responsible
Minister. Queensland creat-
ed a similar department in
1875; South Australia in
1878 ; and N.S.W. again imi-
tated them in 1880. Parkes
wasn’t the creator, but the
imitator; he wasn’t the
founder of the education sys-
tem ; he didn’t draft the
N.S.W. Education Act ; he
didn’t even invent the idea
of imitating the other pro-
vinces. In fact, about the
only thing he ever did invent
was the Kiama Ghost,
Reid and Want are the
only N.S.W. Ministers with
pluck or brains enough to
publicly hold reasonable opin-
ions on the subject of the
liquor-law. They both dare
to believe that you can’t sup-
press the grog-traffic —that if
you closed up every pub. and
stopped the importation of liquor, people
would distill grog in their bedrooms. In
truth, you can no more abolish alcohol than you
can flatten quicksilver under your thumb. The
professional prohibitionists are simply a set of
fools and rogues, or fool-rogues, who live politi-
cally or otherwise by pretending that they can
thumb-squeeze quicksilver.

to its Chief Justices. And yet, with this and
other profligacies in finance under their noses,
people meet you in the street and ask : How is
it costs so much to govern us?

Up to now there has been a go-as-you-
please for seats in the strangers’ gallery of N.S.W.
Assembly : the first persons who arrived got m,
and when the gallery became full no one else was
allowed admission until a seat became vacant.
The Speaker has now ukased that admission is in
future only to be obtained on tickets issued to and
obtained from hon. members. In a word, whereas
the gallery has hitherto been filled with members
enemies, in future it is to be only at the disposal
of members’ friends.

J. B. Tucker, the South Melb. wharf-
laborer, elected to represent the Trades Hall in
Vic. Assembly, is 54 years old, and an English-
man—of course. The average young Australian
is all there in any impersonal skite about his native
iand, but, when anything definite arrives, he in-
variably backs down, refusing to accord the
prophet honor in his own country or to admit
youth and ability as co-existent possibilities.
Tucker is personally a decent fellow, and just
about as fit to be a M.P. as any one of his fellows
who sling cargo or drive donkey-engines. Not a
bit more or less.
The Tozer-Reid discrepancies on the bald
Barrier episode arc much less suggestive of abys-
mal idiocy than is the sheep-witted instinct which
impelled politicians to take sides as parties.
N.S.W. Opposition—not one member, but all of
them—believed every word Tozer said, and jeered
at Thomas, of the Labor camp. N.S.W. Free-
traders— not one, but all of them—believed every
word Reid said, and also jeered at Thomas ; and
N.S.W. Labor men and Q. Labor men and all
Labor men pinned their faith to Thomas and
cared not a fig for Reid or Tozer. The average
man, to become a “ staunch ” member of a politi-
cal party, needs to have the soul blasted out of
him and his brains addled by some huge ancestral
curse or course of heartless personal crime. He
lies, without knowing he lies, for it; he belies,
without knowing he belies, for it; and the worse
in every political sense the man the “ stauncher ”
the party-man is assumed to be.
Hints to Cyclists.
Don’t think you have rented the entire roadway.
Don’t wait until within two feet of a person you
are passing before ringing your bell.
Don’t try to see how close you can run to persons
you are passing without hitting them.
Don’t try to show off because passing a trap filled
with what you assume are envious lookers-on.
Don’t “ scorch ”on the public roads. First-class
riders in good standing have access to tracks
prepared for fast riding.
Don’t bend into a position which represents curva-
ture of the spine, and subjects cycling to
ridicule.
Don’t misunderstand the cause of undue shortness
of breath. The least torpidity or weakness of
the liver is. characterised by shortness of
breath.
Don’t let anyone deceive you. There is only one
remedy known in the world for liver troubles.
That remedy is Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure.
Don’t hesitate to use it promptly if you have
shortness of breath, or notice signs of the
slightest irritation of the urinary organs 01
kidneys, particularly brick-dust deposits in
the water after standing, and, by so doing,
cycling may be followed fearlessly.
Don’t take the vendors’ (of Warner’s “Safe”
Cure) word for it. Mr. W. De Largz D’Alton,
of Warracknabeal, an old Victorian athlete,
says: “I cannot say enough in praise of
Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure. I suffered from uric
acid poisoning. It went to the kidneys—and
this medicine cured me.”

The New York Life Insurance Co. has ap-
pointed as its new resident secretary in Brisbane
Mr. Archibald J. Somerville, who has for many
yoars, in various capacities, been connected with
the Mutual Life Association of Australasia.
Even the most casual observer must have
noted the competition in the boot trade, and
indeed the question for everyone now is, “ Where
should I buy my boots ? Which of the many
finger-posts should I follow ?” The solution of
this as of any question rests on evidence. Sydney
and N.S.W. people know from their senses of
seeing and hearing that the firm of Callaghan and
Son is long-established and of good repute; that
the firm’s clearly-defined course has ever been to
maintain a character for honest quality and ster-
ling value. So the question, “ Where should 1
buy my boots ? ” is resolved into “ How will the
competition affect Callaghan and Son ? ’ The
answer is that the firm strictly adheres to its con-
stant practice of giving the utmost value, because
by so upholding the standard of its goods it pre-
serves its relative position in shoe-selling; and
also because this plan is all the more advantageous
now that a much-canvassed public is liable to be
undecided where to buy. Further reason is that
the principle ensures one customer recommending
another—an invaluable help in these times of keen
competition. So, when calmly analysed, the
question of “ Where should I buy my boots? is
no question at all ; people who like good boots
and do not go to Callaghan’s do themselves a
wrong.*

Personal Items.
A portrait of Parkes, exhibited in a Chicago
hotel, bears under it the name “Stepniak.” This
is fame.
From Governor Hampden’s revised Scrip-
ture : “ Sell all thou hast, and give to the Charity
Organisation Society.”
Recently, in a M.L. church, a man became
the fourth husband of a lady whose three previous
husbands had suicided.
Primate Smith is reported to have sails
set for the harbor of matrimony. Gossip associates
a well-known widow with the venture.

bunch of official letters from America last week
addressed to “Mr. Richard Soule Osborne, Aus-
tralia. ”
Bananaland gibe at Prophet Wragge :
“ When the Lord supervised the weather it was
all right; but, since the Lord put Wragge in
charge, it’s all wrong.”
Messrs. Firebrace’s Mercadool station-
brand is a big, big “ D ” —surely a more than
ordinary coincidence. They put the brand on
bigger than ever just now.
At Cowra (N.S.W.), last week, a man
allegedly aged 107 years, was fined 10s. for an
assault. He had been 80 years in Australia, and
had never been in “ trouble ” before.
“ Putt on the Hempire !” is the yell of the
vulgar in the cricket - field when bowling is
“pasted.” Whenever Gov. Brassey is gravelled
in a speech, he putts on the Hempire.
Throughout the whole of the Mount Lyell
case, Hobart, which has lasted 17 or 18 days, Mr.
Justice Adams opened his lips once, and said five
words. “ The silence of Judge Adams” is omin-
ous.

Characteristic item :
London, April 24.—Mr. Justice Windeyer has not been
heard of for some days, either at the N.S.W. Government
offices or his club. It is understood, however, that he will
return to town for the Colonial Institute dinner to-night.
It recalls two evenings of the Mount Rennie trial.
R. A. Fraser, whose automatic railway-
door controller appears to have “ millions in it,’(is
only 28. He was a clerkling at the Bank of Vic-
toria, Melb., when the idea struck him, and the
controller, on which Gov. Brassey lately bestowed
his —haw ! —warm approval, is the result of 10
years’ steady thinking.
“Seppy” Stephen, M.L.C., solicitor to
the A.M.P. Society, has returned to Sydney after
a sojourn in London, where in some respects he is
a great “ pot.” They do say that the Prince of
Wales once said to the distinguished Septimus :
“ Mr. Stephen, leaving out yourself, whom do you
consider to be the best whist-player in London ? ”
Singularly little notice has been taken in
Australia of the very noteworthy achievement of
Capt. Patrick Weir, of S.A., who has devised a
diagram for finding the true bearing of celestial
bodies so as to correct the compass. Lord Kelvin
(Sir William Thompson) said of the diagram
“ that it is a work of which any high mathema-
tician might be proud. ”
P. S. F. Stephen, who died recently at
Wagga, was a nephew of the late N.S.W. Chief
Justice, and had been 70 years in Australia. He
was educated by Dr. Lang and the old-time
teacher, Cape. In 1851 he was appointed by his
uncle, Gold-commissioner Hardy, captain of the
gold escorts from Beechworth to Melbourne, and
he was at Ballarat during the “ rising ”in ’54. He
died in the modest office of secretary to the Stock
and Pastures Board at Wagga.
John Davies in his heyday dropped one
morning into the office of a Sydney attorney who
had large prints of Gladstone and Disraeli hung
over his desk. John’s eye caught them at once.
“Ha!” he said, pointing to Gladstone, “I see
you’ve got our old friend Montagu ” —a back-
country M.P. of that era —“ in a place of honor.
And, what?” —turning to Dizzy—“yes, it is !
Alderman Caraher, his living image ! ” And John
went uto ecstasies of recognition for full five
minutes.

BEGINNING DE NOVO.
SHE: “I believe you’ve had a kiss for every year since the
year One.”
HE: “ That’s all right—now let’s start B. C.”
Geologist David, of Sydney University,
tactfully distributes the glory of the discovery
that Australian artesian water is derived from
triassic rocks nearly equally between Government
Geologist Jack (Q.), Father Curran (N.S.W.), and
Govt. Geologist Pittman (N.S.W.). A triumph
of professional diplomacy. The credit of first
having noticed the occurrence (small) of artesian
water in triassic rocks is given to Jack; that for
“ large supplies ”to Curran; while “the credit for
having been the first to adduce conclusive evidence
as to the triassic age of much of our artesian water-
bearing strata ”is thrown to Pittman. A bow to
you all, gentlemen.

Two Bagdad Jews have bought the site of
ancient Babylon cheap. This is getting level for
the Captivity. The house of Cohen always gets
level, soon or late, and the house of Cohen is the
only race which can afford to wait 25 centuries
for its vengeance, with a certainty that when the
opportunity comes Cohen will be there to seize it.
Tom Bracken, the M.L. poet laureate,
author of “ Not Understood,” is now known, per
medium of Wellington Post, as the obituary
versifier. No sooner does a man above his fellows
“cross the bar” than Tom lays a slab of rhyme
upon his memory. He has a jingle for all creeds,
all men. In recent months he has poetically
bewailed Blundell, Bishop Moran, Rev. Dr.
Stuart, Bishop Harper, the Brunner miners,
Editor Gillon, Henry Parkes, Pat. Buckley, and
Dr. Pollen —a record, surely—and to Parkes he
gave his best
Lord Lamington, Q’sland’s
new viceroy, visited a Northern R.C.
school. Patting a youngster on the
head, he asked: “Now, little one,
who is Our Gracious Lady ? ” “ The
Blessed Virgin—l pray to her every-
day,” answered the bright scholar.
“ I’ll tell you,” said a N.S.W.
politician the other day, “why I
distrust journalists as a class. I’ll
let the anonymous men go; they
may be better or worse, but the fact
of their anonymity protects them.
Take the editors —those who ‘ re-
present’ the Press at Government
House and on great banqueting oc-
casions. These are not anonymous
—in fact, they print their own
names, and appear to like it, in their
own columns. Now, can you quote
any politician who has changed his
coat as often as the average of
these editors ? Take your friend
Lukin, the other day. What will
he argue now while he runs the
Tory Wellington Post? Yet you
remember him breathing Radicalism
and Republicanism of the reddest
brands. Take Gullett. After over
a dozen years of the high Conserva-
tism of the Argus company he flared
out as a democratic assistant-editor
of the then go-ahead people’s papers,
the Sydney Telegraph and Tri-
bune. This did not work smoothly,
so he passed smilingly over to the
Conservative Herald. Take Ward :
Well, Ward passed from Radical -
Churohism to the Herald, from the
Herald to the Democratic Tele-
graph, and back again to the Con-
servative Courier. And take his
friend, Buzacott. Buzacott was a
roaring lion among the miners at
Clermont one time, denouncing
squatters in every issue, but when
he bought a squatter paper in Rock-
hampton he moderated his tone, and
gradually flowered out as managing
head of the Tory Courier. And so
on. When you newspaper fellows
rate us for turning our coats, will
you, for God’s sake, just look around
you?”
In Sydney G.P.O. is a room set apart
specially as the meeting-place of the “ Post Office
Christian Association.” Once a month the
Christian brethren meet, and Postmaster-General
Cook steps in to help the good work along as
chairman. Some time ago, in an address, he made
the statement that in his opinion there was no such
thing as luck—the best men invariably came out
on top. This from a man whose public life has
been a succession of lucky accidents ! The illness
of the Rev. Smailes, just before the election when
Cook was put up as a makeshift, is a fair sample
of many remembersome items.
At Omeo, Vie., local lawyers had estab-
lished a common-sense practice of officiating in
court without gowns and horsehair bangs, till
Judge Gaunt, the puffy, purple County Court
man, came along and insisted on the usual Guy
Fawkes business. ‘ ‘ These little appearances must
always be respected,” he says. Judge Gaunt, too,
of all the Vic. Judges, looks most ridiculous in
his wig—like something skinned with a little of
the hide and wool left on. Let his Honor look
into a mirror.
Yarn told of “ Joe ” Walford, of Sydney,
who lately died immensely rich. When the Aus-
tralian Gaslight Co. started business, “Joe’’was
one of the first to have gas laid on to his house.
After the first quarter “Joe” did not like the
light, and turned off the gas at the meter. The
co.’s man came round and read the meter, and
“ Joe ” received an account for the second quarter
which showed that he was still using gas at a great
rate. “ Joe ” went down to the co.’s office to ex-
postulate, but he was met with the explanation
that the meter “couldn’t lie.” He accordingly
paid up, and immediately sold off nearly all his
property and bought gas shares, and when he died
he was one of the biggest shareholders. “ Directly
I found what accurate and truthful little affairs the
meters were,” he used to say, “I saw money in
the business.”
Once, when his chronic financial distress
was keener than usual, Parkes applied to one
of the friends who always stuck by him, with a
pitiful tale of his necessities. This gentleman,
who had helped him before, wrote out a good-sized
cheque and gave it to the old man to keep the
wolf from his door. Shortly afterwards, being at
a shop in George-street, Parkes was taken by a
fine oil-painting which he ordered should be sent
home, without enquiring the price. The owner of
the picture, putting a little “on” for the cus-
tomer’s reputation, suggested that he would like
to be paid before delivery, on which Parkes gave
him the cheque, and received a very little change.
A couple of days after, the donor, a well-known
business man, happened to call at the dealer’s,
when he was informed incidentally that a cheque
of his had been passed through and told the nature
of the purchase—his ideas of the necessities of life
receiving a little enlargement.
Apropos of Judge Windeyer’s commenda-
tion of the Californian system, under which coun-
sel are restricted to one hour in which to state a
case or reply, it is interesting to recall that in the
Mount Rennie “ trial ” (involving the lives of
eleven prisoners, nine of whom w'ere sentenced to
death and four hanged) Judge Ventosior occupied
ten hours straightaway in his summing-up—this in
the presence of a jury that was, and must neces-
sarily have been, half-asleep. For it had sat from,
nine o’clock on Friday morning until half-past three
on Saturday morning, and this after being caged up
for four days, night and day. And at nine o’clock
on the same Saturday morning it was brought
back to Court to proceed with one of the most
shameful farces ever perpetrated in the name
of civilisation; at 11.30 on that Saturday night
(i.e., after sitting for thirty-eight and a-half hours
with an intermission of five and a-half hours only !)
it found its death-verdict. Apropos, what is the
N.S.W. Ministry doing about the Mount Rennie
prisoners ? Does it still fear offending that great
social god, the overbearing Mr. Justice Windeyer?
Peart, the diver, recently killed at Fitz-
geralds’Circus in Sydney, was full of madcap tricks,
which generally manifested themselves on his
spring-board. One night in Maoriland he made
this quaint speech: “ Ladies and Gentlemen, —
Some people say that I hold my breath all the way
down into the water. But I don’t; and, to show
you I don’t, I will sing a comic song all the way
down.” He then took a couple of long breaths,
and as he plunged he sang “ Two lovely black ”
plop into the water. Once, in Brisbane, he was
hauled up to the tiny platform and when just getting
ready to dive two of the guys broke, leaving him
suspended in air with his leg twisted round a wire
rope. A rope was sent up, but he refused to come
down to it —“he never came down that way
and men ascended to refix the platform. “Send
me a cigarette,” he called ; and one was sent up
the rope to him. He smoked until the platform
was fixed and strongly guyed. Then, before he
sprang off, he said ‘ ‘ Here goes ! ” and as he de-
scended he exclaimed “Mine’s whisky and”
plop into the water ! He had a most extraordinary
nerve, and knew no fear.

A Victorian-told story Time, 9 a.m.
Place, Sydney railway-station. Man running from
platform for ’bus is stopped by stranger, who places
half-a-crown in his hand remarking, “ I’m a Vic-
torian, and they are so beastly slow over here that
I said I would give this to the first man I saw in a
hurry.” “Thanks,” said the recipient, mounting
the bus, “ I’m a Victorian myself.”
At Emu Plains—one of the oldest N.S.W.
settlements—the rude forefathers of the hamlet
rarely used to drop off before 90 or so, and cen-
tenarians were by no means rare. Rum was their
tipple—so much so, that anyone nowadays who
wants good stiff grog and plenty of it has only
to sink a well witmn half-a-mile of the local ceme-
tery. So says G-. Republican Dibbs.

Cold weather doesn’t send B. Mountcastle
and Sons to sleep ; it livens those famous Sydney
hatters up to show off their choice stock of latest-

and first-quality umbrellas to any customer with

Importance of Dipping Sheep.—The import-
ance of farmers keeping their sheep free from ticks
and other parasites by dipping their flocks regu-
larly after shearing cannot be over-estimated. It
is computed that an animal kept healthy by this
useful economy will give a return of at least is. 6d.
per head more than one suffering from these para-
sites ; a clean sheep keeps in better condition, pro-
duces a heavier fleece, and one which will bring
higher value than a diseased animal. Wool-
buyers are more exacting in their selection of
wools than heretofore, and insist upon careful
preparation. The lesson of the day, taught by
depression and low prices, is that sheep-farmers
and agriculturists must regulate their operations
on a scientific basis, must turn out their products
in first-class style, and must exercise frugality and
care in every step, both of production and distri-
bution. Sheep-farmers require suitable breeds of
sheep for the country they occupy, and should
institute a thorough system of culling, dipping,
avoiding overstocking, and applying intelligence
to the sorting, skirting, and classing of the fleeces.
Under these conditions sheep-farming will pay
handsomely, even at the present low prices of
wool. Odams’ Imperial Dips (fluid and powder)
are recognised as the best. The National Wool
Company, Melbourne, are the agents.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
It will be noticed (says The Bulletin’s London
correspondent) that amongst the fixtures arranged for the
Australian team the name of Middlesex is conspicuously
absent. This, no doubt, is due to the Walker-Webbe influ-
ence. All the years I can remember, the Middlesex club
has been most antagonistic to Australian cricketers—for
what reason it is difficult to say, for no people have been
better treated during their visits down under than some
of the leading lights of the County. Webbe was enter-
tained like a prince when he was out with Lord Harris’s
team, and the hospitality shown to R. D. and I. D. Walker
could not be exceeded, whilst one need only recall the
visits of Stoddart to know that he was right royally en-
tertained and received everywhere. V. E. Walker is
almost rabid in his dislike for Australians, a dislike which
little Bannerman’s brusque way of speaking when he had
a grievance did much to keep alive. I remember, when
there was some question about unfair rolling of the ground
on the Oval, how Alec, spoke his mind, much to Walker’s
disgust, and the writer of this has never been forgiven for
the part he took in the matter on that occasion. How-
ever, the Middlesex people will be the losers, for they
would have a big gate for any fixture against the Austra-
lians, who can well afford to ignore the shoddy swelldom
of the county.
* * * *
Australia has never before had such solid reason
for “blowing” over Our Boys, nevertheless it doesn’t
appear to be blowing at all this time. The match with
Yorkshire, coming after the Australians’ big score against
a decent England Eleven, makes out Trott and Co. to be
rather better than the best that have gone ’Ome before
them. They have their “ unexpected defeats ” to come,
no doubt, but whatever may happen between now and the
first test-match, it will not be 2 to 1 on the other fellows.
Thus far, the Australians haven’t met with any new
slogger or bowler of an appalling character. If there are
no recent additions to the front rank of English cricketers
the beastly colonials will want a lot of shaking off. Mean-
while, of course, the local men, who have had a long
winter to get through, are not quite on level terms with
the visitors, whose break between two cricket seasons was
an invigorating six weeks on the briny.
* * * *
It would be idle to prophesy that the Austra-
lians’ doings up to date augur a beating for All England.
The extinction of Yorkshire by an innings and 8 runs is
valuable only in showing how a fast and a slow bowler
working in conjunction can mix up a real strong side.
Jones’s express volleys from one end, and Giffen’s donkey-
droppers from the other, gained for each bowler nine
wickets altogether. Mold and Briggs’s bowling for Lan-
cashire against Aus. in the second innings affords a similar
instance. Six wickets fell for 68 runs, and but for
Gregory’s 54 and Trumble’s 30 the Aus. second innings
would have been dismal. As it is, 139 runs isn’t over-gay.
* * * »
Much of the strength of the Aus. bowling is in
the weakness of the opposing batsmen, and, good as the
Aus. bowling may be deemed on its present form, the first
six batsmen of All-England—Grace, Stoddart, Abel, Jack-
son, Ranjitsinghi, Ward or Gunn —will take all the sting
out of it on a good wicket. And such a combination of
English bowling as Richardson, Mold, Peel, Attewell or
Briggs, with Grace and Jackson thrown in, will get the
Australians out quite soon enough.
* * * *
That 243 of Grace’s for Gloucestershire against
Surrey is the more noteworthy because no English side
has yet scored 200—190 was the highest—in a completed
innings against the Aus. Eleven.
• # * *
Giffen of all men must applaud Jonah’s success.
Richardson and Mold gave Giffen real Sheol on fiery
wickets last tour. The great George’s method of standing
with a foot of daylight between his legs and wicket
brought forth a sneer from Alcock, sec. Surry C.C. :
“Fast howling has never been to the taste of the Aus-
tralians on English wickets.” Now Giffen’s retort comes :
“Encore Jonah.”
The new batsmen of the Aus. Eleven speak
well for the cold-water scheme. Darling has been right
on the job; Donnan and Hill have proved they went
'Ome to play cricket. Iredale, however, has done nothing
except his 67 against Lancashire.
Names scarcely breathed up to date—Johns,
M'Kibbin, Eady.
So far, the Australians’ tour has been meri-
torious from a cricket standpoint, and a banner-and-
trumpet tour from the point of advt. and biz. Getting an
all-England side out for 39, defeating Yorkshire by an
innings, and Lancashire by 154 runs will draw much gate.
The Aus. bowling has sent every side to the right-about
for less than 200 in any completed innings.
Rock-em-in Jonah was a fair bowler here, but in
England he seems to have found his happy hunting-
ground. The English hearts of oak must quail before
Jonah’s wind-jammers Nothing so persuades a batsman
to quit as a cannon-sbot bowler who takes a rib if he
cannot get a stump. The Almighty took a rib to make a
woman out of a man, and the bowler who takes a rib
makes a woman of a man also.
* * * *
Crack bat Jackson didn’t play for his Yorkshire
county against the Australians. He is said to be a
superior sort of person, who gets offended on slight pro-
vocation, and stops away in consequence. “ Jammy ”
Jackson was his title at school.
* * * *
Frank Walters, who can bowl despite his 15st.,
bald head, and refreshment business, heads the South
Sydney club’s bowling averages with 11 runs per wicket.
Walters was also second to Donnan in the batting averages
for interprovincial matches. Were the Queensland game
included the burly rep. would have been on top. Apropos,
why isn’t the Queensland fixture an interprovincial one ?
Are the Bananalanders merely vagrants ?
* * * *
At time of writing, Clem. Hill had scored
double figures in all his English matches. He and Darling
have six double-figure scores, Giffen five, Trott and Iredale
four, Gregory, Donnan and Trumble three. Scores so
far—Darling 336, Gregory 248, Hill 221, Giffen 201, Trott
183, Donnan 111, Trumble 100.
* * * *
Like a good many Australian singers and others
(says The Bulletin’s London correspondent), Paris 111.
suffers from the injudicious adulation of unthinking
friends ; hence his starting at 2 to 1 in the Great Metro-
politan Stakes at Epsom although he carried the welter-
weight of 9.9, which, even allowing for the “proverbial
ease of the Epsom gradients,” takes carrying. Watts
rode the little horse, and seemed to come too soon in a
fast-run race; and though he looked as if the race was
over at two miles, and the bookies were calling out “ ten
to one on the favorite,” the little chap shut up like a
knife and tailed off at once, much to the disgust of
backers. Of course, after the event everybody discovered
that Mrs. White’s horse cannot stay two miles and a-
quarter, but I saw no marked signs of disappointment
on the faces of his party. It was remarked that his
rider, Watts, has ridden the horse in all the races he has
lost.
* * * »
Caulfield and Melb. Cup entries look well nu-
merically, but comprise the usual crowd of doubtful
starters, cronk-legged ’uns, and the like, including a prad
named Shoddy, which well describes the greater propor-
tion of the draft. Wallace goes for the two miles only,
and after the declaration of weights sporting-talk for the
next few months will consist mostly of Wallace and his
weight, and his dad and all the rest of it. N.S.W. has
unusually large representation in both events, but the
M.L. cracks—Euroclydon, Gipsy Grand, and Fatalist—
don’t appear on the list, while Auraria is already a has-
been.
* * * *
: Redleap’s nomination for the Yic. G.N. Hurdles
and Steeple will give the adjuster something to start his
lists with. Flat-racing Preston is in the Hurdles as well
as the Cups, and Pivot is also given a treble-barrelled
show. Of the two M.L. entries, Mutiny has been here
before, while Tiritea is one of the medium sort, owned by
a Maori.
* * * *
Pony Iduna, entered for Caulfield Cup, should
have a nice chance. Sydney sports haven’t ceased talking
yet about Grand Clogs being taken over for Brisbane Cup,
and since little Paris came to the surface the owner of
every smart miniature thinks he has a Caulfield Cup-
winner, and flatters himself accordingly.
When ill-fortuned Response looked a dead moral
in the straight for Sydney Birthday Cup, everybody
wondered if anything would flash up as usual and beat
her home. The lightly-burdened favorite, neither shirking
nor swerving, came along amid yells and, giving the mare
a bitter finishing pill, just outed her. Then unlucky Dan
O’Brien, for about the twelfth time lately, said “ Well,
I’m blowed ! ”
* * * »
The vicissitudes that have marked the career of
the 500-guinea Captain didn’t deter the patient Kelso
stable from taking money about their well-bred neddy for
Sydney Birthday Cup. A wait of nearly three years to
recoup original outlay and sundries was rather long, and
then the doubtful grit of the alleged fraud had to stand
trial. This was successfully tested in a, pinching tussle
when he paired-off with O’Brien’s star of ill-luck. By no
means first-class, the Captain will likely be well supported
for Caulfield Cup by punters before the winter’s out.
One non-starter for Sydney Royal Stakes caused
the laying fraternity much anxiety. The moke’s crowd
have a cute reputation, and Ikey was frightened. The
stable bit not being forthcoming at a good price, it was
decided to scratch. Even then the books wouldn’t
lengthen out and rope in the public. It savored too
much of the tactics pursued with the last Doncaster
winner. So they let the doubtful chance slide. Punters
who had snapped early tens wept in silence.
* * * »
A half-fuddled Randwick visitor put J>3 on
Thor, at eights, and stuck the ticket in his hat-band. He
was so elated by success that he waved his hitter wildly,
the ticket dropped out—and someone else collected £27
from a sour-looking bookie.
* * * ♦
Sydney Tattersall’s flash Grand National Steeple
(150 sovs. gross value) was a slow affair, and none of the
horses fell. Skipwith’s M.L. prad, Othello, got home
with Grandwing, and won on the post. Maoriland Bally-
hooley annexed the event last year.
* * * *
The first three in Tatt.’s Winter Stakes were
practically unbacked, and Ikey Mo smiled serenely.
Coo-ee was pushed home at too smart a pace for the
crowd ; Royal Rose, a one-time lame ’un, and Wyalong
dead-heating. The latter is by Trident, who, by the way,
has never got anything as good as himself at the stud.
* * * *
The victory of Huret over a big field on Satur-
day won’t be easily forgotten by those who saw it. A
number of the field were palpably stiff, but the time was
good. There seems no reason why this young Cranbrook
shouldn’t stay out distance runs when pitted against the
best of his.year in the spring.
• * * *
Sir William, second in Malvolio’s Melb. Cup,
now appears in “ The Derby Winner,” at Sydney Royal.
A congenial game for a played-out neddy. The turf’s a
stage and jockeyed equines merely players.
* * * ♦
Punters are already discussing Ike Foulsham’s
Chevalier for Caulfield Cup.
* * * *
English steeplechaser Cloister’s remarkable re-
covery is alleged to be the result of large draughts of new
milk !
* * * *
Manifold’s Grand National string now includes
Waterloo, Dungan, Veronica, Apsley, Geometry, Elva,
Pivot, and several others. A good hand.
* * * *
M.L. Premier Seddon and the racing bosses
can’t agree re tote. The racing bosses want to limit its
use to their own particular domains, but Seddon wishes
to make it generally available. Same game was tried on
with Vic. Betting-Suppression Bill. Frank Madden
wanted legalised betting restricted to clubs racing under
V.R.C. rules.
* • * *
Gollan’s Ebor put up his third English win on
April 23. He carried list. 51b. three miles for a £220
prize in the Ludlow county steeplechase.
* * * *
In future, Adelaide street-bettors (when caught)
are to be arrested—not summoned.
* * * *
Hova, now lame, has raced long enough for a
rest. The champion has never been sent off his legs on
the track, however, not requiring it. He belongs to the
class that can be got ready for a Cup in five minutes.
* * » *
If the time is correct, The Merry Boy’s 3min.
ssec. for Adelaide handicap shows him to be no slouch.
Cook’s colt is a possible dangerous Cup neddy. But his
last week’s success won’t compensate owner Sam for the
knock-out of the brilliant Harvester.
» ♦ * *
Half-a-dozen sailors, patrons of the Leger, at
Randwick, backed The Captain for the Cup, and stood by
the bookie the whole time until their choice won and the
cash was handed over. A welsher would have had little
chance with that crowd.
» * * »
The Melb. anti-cyclists, who are holding meet-
ings and conferences all over the smellful city, have drawn
up 18 pp. of new by-laws applying especially to cyclists,
which they want Premier Turner to incorporate in the
Police Offences Statute.
* * * *
Ex-sculler Neil Matterson came out of his shell
and won both the Princess and second-class handicaps at
Melb. Theatrical Carnival. Neil was a rather neat rider
when he pedalled a low gear ; but he jumps on the pedals
of the high-gear machine he now rides in most elephantine
fashion. He put in four half-mile sprints in the heats and
finals of the races he won, which took all the steel out of
the fast finishers ; Middleton (gamest and most ungainly
of riders) was the only one to persevere in the hopeless
pursuit. Mattelson’s win was very popular. He has
hitherto been very unfortunate in Melbourne, but has
always given the public a good run.
* * * *
It is probable that Sydney has seen the last of
Matterson as a resident, he having identified himself with
the Melbourne-manufactured Dux machine. Martin has
cast in his lot with the same firm ; Pither goes over to the
Rudge-Whitworth in June ; Porta has joined the Electra;
and Harris has returned ’Ome. Which seems to leave
the once all-powerful Humber without a local back-mark
representative.
* * ♦ *
Understood that Harris will return to Australia
for next racing season; but this can hardly be the
Leicester man’s intention, as he knew that by racing
against trotter Osterley he forfeited any right to race
under league rules again.
* * * *
G. Morrah, a six-foot novice of much promise
and magnificent physique, was the “ snip ” for the Princess
Theatrical Handicap, and was going well when a rider
fell in front of him. Morrah came down and skidded
along the asphalt on his head, one of his ears being nearly
severed. Porta fell in his heat through his tyre blowing-
out. The Italian insists upon having his tyres hard as
iron, and has suffered many times this season therefrom.
Luckily he was behind, and injured no one but himself.
* » ♦ *
Rumored that the flyer M‘Leod, with something
approaching 100 American races to his credit last season,
will be in Australia in the spring to ride for the crowd he
represents on a 16}41b. Yankee jigger.
* * * #
“Phormi” predicts to The Bulletin that in
two years or less 90 per cent, of Melb. racing-men will be
riding either American or locally-made machines. The
talk of the dressing-room, he says, points emphatically to
this.
Gameness doesn’t pay. M'Lean, by sheer
pluck, won the 25 miles Vic. championship, and was at
once brought back 40yds. in a two-mile handicap, from a
mark off which he could not previously get a place in his
heat.
* * * »
Lou. Sharp, who has kept in championship
form, or near it, for over a dozen years, having got
married, has retired from the track. Greenwood (cham-
pion before Megson, Parsons, and co. could feed them-
selves) is still winning from back-marks. Fitzgerald, who
has rowed, cycled, and athleted generally for about 20
years, also retains his vigor and is still in the first-class
division. It seems, despite the appearances of a few
months ago, as though cyclists who take reasonable care
of themselves will, as a class, retain racing-form much
longer than any other athletic clan. Many Melb. racing-
men are well into the thirties. One beginner, who lost
his novice status lately by winning a handicap in splendid
style, is the father of eight children, and was married in
’B3. Another has his nearlv grown-up son as trainer.
* * * *
Yankee Martin shone again at Parramatta last
Saturday. “Bill” is triumphing now, but how does he
feel in his heart about next season? It is a marvel how
he keeps his form so long. What a living lesson on steady
living he is to all athletes ! Also, what a lesson on the
desirableness of having had the sort of ancestors who fit
you out with a heavy jaw and other physical fittings to
match.
* * * *
The amateur boxing competitions at St. James’s
Hall, Piccadilly (writes an Australian in England to The
Bulletin) were poor indeed, and evidenced the decay
of the noble art here. Three-fourths of the entries looked
as if they couldn’t fight, and didn’t want to, and only two
or three had anything but a “ left.” A smart boy, Jones
—whom Pluto would eat first and play the Dead March
over afterwards —retained the bantamship ; Gunn doubt-
fully kept the feather. A Dutchman, Vanderhout, showed
more than his race’s proverbial courage, and punched his
opponents out, and himself to the light-weight champion-
ship ; whilst Ross won the middle creditably for such a
light ’un. The heavy-weights provided but one bout and
the only sensational “go” of the competition. An Edin-
burgh University 75-incher met Captain Johnstone, the
holder, and was all over him in the first two rounds. If
he had had any kind of a decent “ right” he must have
won then. The captain, a showy, muscular man, had a
very powerful right, and shoved it home three times in
succession in the third round. Then, while his lengthy
opponent stood helplessly dazed, looking like a crow-
blinded lamb, the noble captain hit him a terrible blow
behind the left ear. Down came the slab with his head
between his shoulders on the floor, and he was counted
out.
* * * *
“Torpedo ” Murphy is not the only Australian
pug. who has gone back to hard work. Peter Newton
has for some time been at his trade (bootmaking) in a
Collingwood factory. Peter was one of the very best
until Burke, “the Irish lad,” beat him. As a second he
had no equal. His last appearance in the square circle
was some few years ago, when he downed his old instruc-
tor, Hicken, at the M.A.C.
« » • *
London Referee received 18,000 guesses at the
result of the Grand National Steeplechase. Not one
guesser named the winner Soarer, and only seven of the
amateur prophets fancied him for a “ place.” Now, what
sort of dividend would Soarer have paid to Tote backers ?
* » * *
Asked Cataract’s price straight-out for the Sell
ing Steeple at the first day of the A.R.C. Birthday meet-
ing, a bookie —they call ’em “ totalisator-investment
agents” in Adelaide —replied “12 to 1.” The tote paid a
div. of £22 9s. Then the books wanted 4 to lon about
Hova for the w.f.a. race—the Parkside Stakes. The tote
paid a div. at 5 to 3 on.
* * * •
The University-Wallaroo Rugby game on Satur-
day was a bull-dog battle between old enemies. After a
frantic effort to win on either side, it resulted in a eleven-
point tie. The Wallaroos played the better game, however,
their superb passing nonplussing their opponents. Pass-
ing is the point in Rugby. A selfish player shouldn’t be
given a place in a team. Pirates went under to Went-
worths by 11 to love, skipper Brlly Warbrick being as good
as sixteenth man to the winners. Wentworth’s game, in
fact, appeared to be confined mostly to Warbrick. Rand-
wicks had an afternoon’s exercise with Paddington, win-
ning by 47 to 5.
» • » *
St. Kilda F. C., cocksure of beating Essendon,
looked like doing it till the last quarter. Then, though
Essendon had one goal more, there was naught to choose
between either twenty ; but, with its old-time dash, Essen-
don added six goals while St. Hilda dazedly looked on.
Present form preserved, Essendon should easily be pre-
miers.
* * * *
To-day’s (Saturday) Melb. football, on behalf of
local charities, includes three 40-minute games—Essen-
don v. Collingwood and South v. Port Melb., the winners
in each game playing off for a £lO prize. Innovations in-
clude experiments with boundary-umpires, 10yds. mark
limit, and goal-bars 10ft. from ground.
* „ * * *
In the wood-chopping and sawing matches at
Maclean, N.S.W., the Tasmanians, Macpherson and Moore,
easily defeated their N.S.W. opponents. The Tasmanian
wood-chopping fraternity, by the way, are about to pre-
sent an axe to Gladstone. What would be of even more
interest to the old man would be some of the colossal
wood-chips made to fly at the chopping matches in Rasp-
berry Land.

A tiny spark, which will soon grow a live
red coal too hot and big to be carelessly handled :
With whom lies the patronage under which solici-
tors are employed to prosecute for the Crown or
for the officers of a public department in police-
court cases? Is it seemly to crowd an M.L.A.
solicitor with work of this class ? Consequent and
cognate questions are innumerable.
Premier Turner was the most unassuming
figure at Gov. Brassey’s Birthday Levee. While
Democrat Deakin blazed with gold, and numerous
swaggering “oil’d and curl’d Assyrian bulls smell-
ing of musk and impudence ” clanked their swords
and spurs, and the Bishop flapped his lawn sleeves,
and the R.C. Archbishop waggled his purple
cassock, and fat, hairy pastor Bevan strutted in a
chaplain’s uniform—the Premier, in a plain dress-
suit without even silk revers, allowed himself to be
shoved at the back, and could only be seen when
the Bishop lifted his flour-bag-sleeved arms to
scratch his head. Turner really hates show.

Democrat Deakin, M.P., in a gorgeous
diplomatic uniform, was the spectacle of Gov.
Brassey’s birthday levee. Premier Turner turned
up in the plainest possible attire, but Alfred was
littered with frills and embossed with gold all
over. Seeing that dazzling rig-out, one wonders
more than ever at Deakin’s refusal of a knight-
hood, and sadly concludes that if the “ distinc-
tion ” were offered again he would grab -with both
hands —if not with his feet also, like a Chinese
cabinet-maker. “ That Deakin ? ” said a compara-
tive stranger, who had asked about the potentate
in gold and many other colors —“I thought it
must be Peacock.”

Maoriland’s tribute to the Great Protector
is that in the town of Cromwell nearly the entire
population is Chinese.
W. Creek : “Theworld will smile, but by far
the most important name in a recent week’sMelb.
obituary is that of Peter Gleeson, first pioneer of
Jamieson, Vie., who died there in obscurity, May
2. Such Walt Whitman addresses, in his song of
‘ The Redwood Tree ’ as : ‘ You that, sometimes
known, oftener unknown, really shape and mould
the New World.’ ”
The Australian tramp again. The other
day telegraphic communication across S. Australia
was interrupted by a hard-up, who cut the wires
to obtain assistance from the repairing-party. The
repairing - party duly arrived, provisioned the
hungry outcast, and sent him North rejoicing. A
few weeks later the wires were cut again, between
Daly’s Waters and Powell’s Creek, by a second
tramp, who was found by the repairing-party to
be well-provisioned and comfortably camped, but
who, being footsore, wanted a lift northward. It
was determined to prosecute this time, but the
nearest police-station was a few hundred miles
north on the Katherine —the very goal to which
swaggie was wending. Consequently, the latter
scored again, and the authorities were left blas-
pheming.
Angus and Robertson, the well - known
booksellers of Castlereagh-street, Sydney, have at
much expense undertaken the enterprise of pro-
viding a thoroughly Australian copy-book for use
in the State schools of this badly-writing conti-
nent. Hitherto, the local youngster has had to
form his hand on questionable foreign maxims:
Angus and Robertson’s series provides for him
facts regarding the history and industries of Aus-
tralia, which is a distinct gain. Further, the
success of the series will mean the establishment
of a considerable local industry, all the work in
connection with it being done in Sydney. The
new copy-books have been approved byN.S.W.
Dept, of Public Instruction, also by the managers
of many local banks, insurance co’s, and business
firms ; and valuable prizes are offered annually to
scholars and pupil-teachers using them. Conse-
quently, the enterprise commends itself to every
Australian who wants to see this country stand on
its own merits, and utilise its resources, and em-
ploy local industry and local intellect to the
utmost.*
Port Melbourne is a wormy suburb, with
a flavor of dead fish and stagnant mussels, and it
is a popular place of call with the friendless
suicide and miscellaneous dead, who come up from
the sea and bask on the fore-shore. An attempt
was lately made to deprive the Port of its
morgue, and public-spirited citizens arose in
energetic protest. It was pointed out that the
local morgue was a crazy establishment raised on
props, and that the water running over the corpses
stored above fell upon the innocent children who
played hide-and-seek under the monumental ruin.
It was also declared that the suburb’s unclaimed
dead would be better served and more comfortably
housed in the city morgue; but Port Melbourne
objected with great wailing, and refused to be
comforted. The local idea was that the morgue
was good for business and lent a certain status to
the town, and that a place without a morgue was
a place of no consequence whatever. So Port
Melbourne retains its morgue, and on pleasant
Sunday afternoons the children of the suburb may
still frolic in the juices of the latest cadaver fresh
up from the beach.

“ Orroroo, Adelaide, Aug. 25, 1894. Gentlemen,—l
hereby acknowledge receipt of cheque, iny share in
estate, with thanks. I also thank you for prompt manner
you have conducted the business. —Yours respectfully,
Amy Nutt.”
“ Port Darwin Camp, South Australia, July 7, 1894.—1
purchased your ‘ Register’ and found my uncle’s name as
having funds in Chancery. I thereupon instructed you to
investigate the matter, the result of which was I recovered
my share, and advise all to procure your ‘ Register ’ and
have their claims properly investigated by Dougal and
Co., London. —Yours faithfully, A. Warland.”
“Queen-street, Auckland, 16th June, 1894. —Dear Sirs,—
The ‘ Register’ compiled by you is of very great value In
my own case, my mother was entitled to money lying un-
claimed for 50 years in Chancery. Seeing your book 1
noticed my mother’s name, and was successful in obtain-
ing what should have been paid more than half a century
ago. It is to be regretted your book is not more generally
known.—Yours faithfully, E. T. Dufaur (Solicitor).”
“ Bordertown, Adelaide, October 9, 1894. —Gentlemen,—
I beg to acknowledge cheque for my portion of money,
bequeathed to my mother, with thanks.— Yours, &c.,
Luke Farrow.”
