Selected Testimonials
Nerves Cured.
A Magistrate Cured of Nervousness and
Biliousness.
160 Elizabeth-st., Melb., July 18, 1892.
Dear Sir —I have much pleasure to inform you
that a course of your Alfaline Pills completely
cured me of a severe case of biliousness, wind and
nervousness. I may also mention that several of
my friends to whom I have recommended your
remedies have derived great benefit from them;
therefore, T consider you are deserving of success,
wmoh I do most heartily wish you. —Yours grate-
fully, A. HARRIS, J.P.
Nervousness Cured.
The well-known and popular representative of
the Sydney Bulletin, a gentleman whose testi-
mony carries special weight, writes thus : To Herr
Rassmusskn.—Dear Sir,—l think it my duty to
inform you that a course of your Alfaline Vitality
Pills acted very beneficially on my nervous system,
and, in consequence, I have recommended them
to many friends, who have all been cured by their
use.—Yours, R. A. LUCKHAM.
A Much-valued Testimonial from one of
Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Here Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. E. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”
A Lady’s Wish Fulfilled.
It gives me great pleasure indeed to inform
you that your celebrated Alfaline Universal Pills
cured me, when all other remedies and doctors
failed, of a stubborn case of Debility of 18 years’
standing. My wish is fulfilled at last, and I don’t
know how to thank you enough. Dear Sir, I
shall recommend your Alfaline Remedies to all
sufferers I come across. You are quite at liberty
to publish or make what use you like of this testi-
monial, and anyone writing to me will have full
particulars. I am, yours respectfully, Mrs. E.
SMITH, Port Macquarie, Hastings River, N.S.
Wales.
A Miraculous Cure.

master to the Queensland Military, writes as follows :
Ithaca-st., Normanby Hill, Brisbane,
Sept. 20, 1892.
To Herr Rassmussen.
Dear Sir,—l am jubilant to inform you that
your wonderful Alfaline Pills completely cured
me of a very obstinate internal complaint; and
that, although I am now over 70 years of age,
I feel as strong as any young man, and shall
always be grateful to you.
Yours very truly, N. LARSEN.

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pifls, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness : Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street..
A Lady Cured of General Debility.
Herr Rassmussen. March 15th, 1894.
Sir, —I hereby beg to certify that having been
a sufferer from General Debility of the Nervous
System for over seven years, and having tried
without success several of the leading doctors
both in Sydney and Melbourne, I was advised to
try your Alfaline Remedies. lam thankful I did
so, for after a few courses of the Alfaline Remedies
I am now completely cured, and I shall be most
happy to recommend your remedies to anyone
suffering as I was. Thanking you for your
courtesy and the uniform patience with which
you have treated me, allow me to say that you
can make what use you may think proper of this.
Wishing you every success, allow me to remain,
yours,
CATHERINE HAGERMAN.
As witness: John Hagerman (my husband),
caretaker Tivoli Theatre, Castlereagh - street
Sydney.

Leges : “Purves gets £5OO douceur, and
.£lOO a day, to go to Tasmania, over the Mt.
Lyell case. What for? It recalls Chief Justice
Russell, in England, getting iloOO, just before his
elevation to the Bench, for going down to Leeds
Assizes on one day only. Not, of course, to plate
sin with gold, hut to succour the poor and him
that hath no helper. Why should money have
such weight in the legal balance ? In short, why
should a —mistaken—impression abound, that it is
nine-tenths of Justice?”
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition'; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
Dear Bulletin, —If Mr. Plummer will
read the “ Parkesiana ” paragraph again relating
to the supposed presence of Oxford, who shot at
the Queen, in court during O’Earrell’s trial, he
will see, I think, that I have not made “ several
errors.’' I didn’t commit myself to any direct
assertion as to the truth or falsity of the statement
that Oxford was in court. I put the point tenta-
tively, and the enquiry from Parkes was simply :
Had he heard that such was the case; the reply
being that he had known there wmre rumors to that
effect. For myself, I was prepared, by the know-
ledge of some of the very facts referred to by Mr.
Plummer, to doubt the accuracy of the report. I
forward for your own information the name of
my authority, a gentleman who emphatically
declared that he was convinced the solicitor
in question teas Oxford. Writing hurriedly up-
country, and without the opportunity of revising
proofs, I made one slip ; I should have used the
term “ exile d ” for transported. What was in my
mind was the difficulty felt in official circles as to
what should be done with Oxford. Within two or
three years after his incarceration in 1840 the
then Under-Secretary for the Colonies wrote to
Governor Eardley - Wilmot (Tasmania), asking
“ whether Oxford’s life would be safe ” if he were
sent to the colony. When- the question of
releasing him was again discussed in 1867, Aus-
tralia was once more suggested as the place
of his exile, and to this circumstance and a very
pronounced facial resemblance (judging from por-
traits) between the solicitor alluded to and Oxford
was no doubt attributable the origination of the
rumor. I was perfectly aware, of course, that
transportation had ceased long before 1867, but
that Oxford was released on condition of exile was
certain. As for Captain M‘Lerie’s share in the
arrest, I stated what was popularly and credibly
believed—and the belief, let me say, would be
found particularly well-based, if it were worth
while to go- into details. —The Whiter of
Paekesiana.
Mr. Henry Lloyd, trading as the N.S.W.
Bookstall Co., is opening a large bookstall on the
jetty of the North Shore Ferry Co., and the public
may rely on finding at His new branch, as at all
his other stalls, the latest books, popular maga-
zines, &c. ; also, the very latest editions of all the
newspapers. This new departure will be appre-
ciated by the North Shore ferry travellers, as the
N.S.W. Bookstall Co. has on hand an excellent
stock of literature.*

THE SMELTING COMPANY Of AUSTEAL’A
LIMITED.
Managing Director : JOHN HOWELL (late General
Manager of the Broken Hill Proprietary Co., Limited).
ADDRESS:
Head Office—s 6 Margaret-st., Sydney.
THE Works are now in course of construction at Lake
Illawarra, N.S.W., and a portion will be in operation
by AUGUST 1896, when the Company will be prepared to
purchase and treat, in large or small quantities, all classes
of Gold, Silver, Lead, Copper, and other Ores.
An ASSAY OFFICE and LABORATORY have been
opened at the Head Office in Sydney, and most com-
modious Assay Offices and Laboratories are now in
course of erection at the works, which will be equipped
in the most Modern Style, at both of which establish-
ments samples of ores of all classes will be tested for
the guidance of those not familiar with the value of
same.
Treatment of base refractory ores of low grade a
speciality.

An inquest was held on the body of Mar-
garet Curtis, aged 73, at Melbourne the other day.
For a year the old woman had made persistent
efforts to get into the local Benevolent Asylum
and failed. After her last attempt she walked to
the Yarra, and found a refuge there. “Suicide
whilst of unsound mind.” There is a fine field in
Melbourne for the operations of a few missionaries
from the ‘ ‘ heathen tribes ” that eat their old and
worn-out relatives.

Strike Me!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a typical Yankee drummer, wavy-haired,
dapper, cute ; with the assurance of saint or devil.
The boots he stood in tapered to the sharpest of
points, and those points somehow gave you the
idea of wedges—show them the smallest opening
and straightway the whole man who stood in
them followed.
It was at a cycling carnival. The Governor was
there, seated on a chair ; close by lay the Yank,
with his bicycle prominent. The Governor was
called away for a few minutes by the ground secre-
tary ; the Yank instantly took the Governor’s
chair, and sat with crossed legs. The Governor
returned alone—the Governor, whose task, like
Satan’s, is to be nice to everybody. Seeing the
Yankee agent in his chair, he lifted the bicycle
and examined it—just as a gentle hint—while the
owner eyed him out of one eye-corner !
“ Hullo, Bill ? ” exclaimed the Yank.
The Governor, pretending not to hear the salute,
asked, “ Who is the agent for this bicycle ? ”
“ The agent,” said the youth, “ is a gentleman,
since he laughs at every joke just as if it were a
joke and he had never heard it before.”
The Governor smiled —the tired smile of office.
“ The machine ought to have pace,” he said, in a
confidential, insinuating way.
The Yank knocked the ash of his cigar off on
the Governor’s walking-stick, and, taking a draw,
blew a cloud in the air expressive of disdain.
“ It ought to have pace,” repeated the Governor
rather nervously.
“Say, stranger?” asked the Yank, “air you
pinching my back or trying to give my machine a
black eye? Well, just you look right here ! that
machine is so fast that you’ve only to put one of
your crazy splinters on each of those pedals
and you streak like a greased flash for the horizon.
Start level with a bullet from the best rifle, bullet
gives up after the first hundred yards. Why ?
’Cause that machine’s ’Murkan, stranger, not Eng-
lish. And light ! that machine is so light that
you can pick it up and carry it as easily as the
ideas in your head. And strong! Why the
heaviest man in the States —weighs 25 stone —’s
been ridin’ one of those machines down stairs last
10 years—broke the stairs; never broke that
machine. Can’t pinch my back, stranger ; can’t
give my machine a black eye ! Every man that
ever rides one of those machines holds up hands
and feet in praise of it. You mount it now; when
I say hit her up, you hit her up, and if you don’t
make this country stare, well, you can take me
home to be hung.”
The Governor was here called away, and a pal-
pitating official spluttered to the Yank : “ Good
God ! do you know that that’s the Governor ! ”
It stopped the Yank in the middle of a sneeze.
“That rag a Governor? I took him for a com-
mon tramp ! an ordinary English globe-trotter !
Strike me for a half-awake Australian ! ”
Item from Kempsey (N.S.W.) paper :—
The champion loafer and dirty man has been unearthed
at Kempsey. has been married 21 years, has
10 children, and would sooner die than yakker. He
lay in bed in his clothes for weeks at a time, too bone-idle
to even scratch himself. One of his shirts, which a con-
stable took off the debased hound and produced in court
as exhibit A, was so caked with filth that it looked like a
coat of mail; and when the P.M. angrily ordered the trap
to take the horror away, he took it out in the yard and
stood it up on end, without any support. It was as stiff
as a board.
Paterfamilias Takes Good Advice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ A man bas no right to take his troubles home,” says
Justice Hartley Williams. Nonsense! A man’s home is
his castle, and he may do what he likes there.
“ Exactly ! just so ! I know it, old fellow;
Been there myself and feel as you do.
‘ The world seems painted a dull, bilious yellow ; ’
As for me, I have cured it, and so can you, too.
“If in silence you fret your worries will double ;
You can’t slay your fellows —the law that for-
bids ;
But when you get home you can work off your
trouble
By taking it out of your wife and the kids.”
I’ll take the advice that my friend kindly proffers ;
He’s a shrewd man to follow—in business no
dunce;
I’ll try just as soon as an opening offers.
Hush ! here they are coming—l’ll test it at once.
Their tender touch smoothes out my wrinkles,
unknowing,
As one climbs on my knee and one strokes my
hair;
All my worries are chased by their spirits o’er-
flowing,
Till they sleepily lisp out their “ God-bless-you”
prayer.
Then she sits beside me, and tells me quite gravely
How five-year-old baby has got beyond bounds ;
I look in her face, and I notice how bravely
She’s sparring for time—till the dinner-gong
sounds.
Next morn I could weep at my yesterday’s blind-
ness,
As I look on this planet through less bilious lids;
For I’ve got in my heart a fresh stock of kindness,
And I’ve taken it out of my wife and the kids !
Melb.
R. K. DEE.
Prof. Agassiz and party, who came all the
way from America specially to explore the Barrier
reef, practically failed to do anything, owing to
the weather, and return to the States much dis-
appointed.
Editor Bulletin, —The author of ‘The
Benefit of Clergy ” in a recent issue evi-
dently has little knowledge of shanty-keepers’
daughters. The shanty girls are often less
accomplished than the city barmaid, but they
possess quite as much of the other sort of know-
ledge as the town-bred nymph. And what did
Jane’s babe do to the author, anyhow, that he
should thrust the old rabbiter and the other he-
person on it as sponsors, when there were so many
women about? Again, who ever heard a sober
priest bawl out for whisky in front of a
crowd ? Also, the author shows his ignorance of
the Irish character when he makes Mrs. Dargan
drag in the two Magdalens before introducing the
corpse ? Apparently Mr. Allen has never been to
an Irish wake, or he would know better. With
Paddy, the corpse is always first. Eor example :
Female Mourner: “ Who is that foine-looking man
over beyant?” Neighbor: “Arrah, hould yer
whist, he is only a cousin to the corpse.” Now,
that is about how a live man stands with the Irish
when there is a corpse about. — Biddy. Saltpeter
Creek, Vic.
Some Business Items.
Melb. : “ I regard it as a sign of the decadence
of our city that the newspapers should be so heavily
charged with leading articles on books, instead of on affairs
—indicating that we become more of a Bristol or Edin-
burgh, than a Chicago or Liverpool. The regular leader
here is the kind of thing which ought to be an occasional
special article, to tickle the bookworm. If business is
alive, it is reflected in the newspaper.”
* * * *
The other day a man presented a <£l note of the
Colonial Bank of Australasia in one of the biggest ware-
houses in Sydney, and was allowed 60 seconds to get out
under pain of being arrested for trying to pass the paper
of an imaginary bank. Being flurried at this extraordinary
reception, he got out with his note, and thereby missed a
chance of making, probably, £lOO damages for false im-
prisonment. Australia has so many banks that hardly
one business man in 1000 could tell the names of them all
straight off, and very few people really know whether
many of the notes they meet are those of banks that are
living or dead or that were never born. Most of them
simply take any note that comes along and risk it.
¥ * * *
When some Riverina squatters want grubbing
or ring-barking done they call for tenders. The whites
who send in the lowest tender are asked to call on the
manager, who shows them a starvation tender from Bung
Hi and Co., and explains that of course the lowest tender
must be accepted, but out of patriotism the Aryans can
have first chance at the Mongol’s price. If the white men
say “ Gotohel!” Chinamen are procured from the stations
or elsewhere and put on till the white men cave in.
* * * »
Melbourne merchants who, a short time ago,
were tumbling over one another in their efforts to open
up branch houses in Westralia have cooled down consider-
ably during the last two months ; in fact, in the majority
of cases, the contemplated ventures are finally “ off.”
Their belief in Westralia as the coming province has
greatly chilled.
* * * »
The Bulletin sincerely hopes to see expert
shorthand writers installed in N.S.W. Supreme Court to
take evidence, summings-up and judgments. So far,
practically the only evidence admissible on appeal as to
what a judge said in summing-up or in delivering an oral
judgment is—His Honor’s own word ! Half-a-dozen bar-
risters, including perhaps a couple of clever shorthanders,
may have down in their notes something absolutely at
variance with his Honor’s present version of what he
said, and the newspaper reports may agree with counsel’s
notes, but no matter—his Honor is as absolute as the
Almighty. If he says he said so-and-so there is an end
of the matter— he did say it, and that’s all. It is really
time that these highly-paid autocrats were pinned down
to black-and-white.
• * * *
Melbourne house properties are looking up.
The growing revival of business down South has touched
all classes of trade, and in such suburbs as Albert Park,
Malvern, Hawksburn, Armadale, Windsor, Prahran, and
South Yarra house-agents now find it difficult to meet
the demand. During the past twelve months the number
of unlet tenements in Melbourne suburbs has decreased
fully 10 per cent.
¥ * * ♦
“ Chambers ” is a term which covereth effec-
tually a multitude of costs. The word “interlocutory”
has a Mesopotamian awe about it which checks irreverent
enquiry. Blit while the public chafes under the smart of
costs in general, it is well to point out one matter
responsible for much expense which could be easily pre-
vented by a determined Judge. It is quite the usual
thing any day in chambers for counsel to apply for ad-
journments on account of their being engaged in other
courts. Opposing counsel seldom resist these applications,
in order to ensure similar favors when needed, and because
of a sort of esprit de corps which would reckon it churlish
so to do. Clients are rarely consulted in such matters ;
but, of course, it all means expense. Occasionally appli-
cations are delayed months on this account. Even the
fearsomeness of “ interlocutory ” cannot keep back a mild
protest against such trifling with clients’ interests.

The army of seedy individuals in Melbourne
who seek to make piles by syndicating processes for treat-
ing refractory gold-ores, has been largely increased lately,
There are also some score would-be floaters of patent bolt-
nuts, and a dozen new spark-arrester inventors; whilst
the number of mechanics who throng Collins-street daily
with prospectuses of portable water-condensers, suitable
f° r Westralia,” is simply legion.
Apropos “ suitable to Westralia.’’ A large per-
centage of the younger men leaving for the West still con-
sider it necessary to carry a revolver and a few lb. of
cartridges, a gold-belt, and a miner s pick and shovel.
Waste of money ! Any of these articles can he had, by
the hundred, unused, in Perth for a fraction of their
wholesale value. , * *

The newsboy, the match-vendor, know
the force of the pathetic appeal—“ Last one I’ve
got, sir ! ” and generally sell the whole dozen on
the same plea. But why should a man buy the
last one rather than the first one or the middle
one ? A complete answer to this question is worth
at least a column of psychology.

The Kaiwarra Mystery .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A prisoner in Darlinghurst recently stated that
he committed, in Maoriland in ’B9, a murder for
which another man is now undergoing life sentence.
That man is Louis Chemis, an Italian, convicted
of the murder of Thomas Hawkings, farmer, of
Kaiwarra, 3£ miles from Wellington (M.L.) Post
Office. The crime, known as the “Kaiwarra
mystery,” ranks among the more extraordinary in
the annals of homicide. This is its true story.
* » * *
Thomas Hawkings, aged 50, farmed 500 acres at
Kaiwarra for 18 ; years preceding his murder. His
farm, on a hill top, chiefly rough hill-land,
is approachable by only one high-road from
Wellington. That is bounded on the right
by the waters of Port Nicholson, a railway
running between sea and road. On the left the
road is shut off by steep hills practically inacces-
sible save by infrequent fenced gullies. Up one
of these is the private road to Hawkings’ farm,
the bacon-factory of Dimock Bros, being within
the fence. Twenty-four chains townward to
Dimock’s, a fenced gully leads to Chemis’s house.
From Dimock’s to Hawking’s house chains)
the rugged, ever ascending and curving road,
which soon shuts Dimock’s from sight and sound,
is encompassed on three sides by hills. At rear
are the wide waters of the port, framed by V-
shaped hills, affording glimpses of the snow-clad
lofty Kaikouras in the far distance the whole
scene dreary and wild in the extreme. Local
society in 1889 was largely butcher and slaughter-
men, by whom Hawkings, inclined to territorial
airs, was unloved. He waged war with strange
tramps and on visitors from Wellington, who tres-
passed on his land, shooting, and he was both
aggressive and timid.
* * * *
On Friday morning, 31st May, 1889, he left
home (his custom on Fridays) for Wellington,
driving a cart and carrying <£6 or £7, which he
banked in town, also a pocket-book, said by his
wife to have contained “ native papers and odds-
and-ends, ” and which was never found after his
death. He transacted business, and left for
home with a few shillings only. About 5.30
p.m. (darkness gathering) he called at Dimock’s
for bread, stayed a minute, and then
went up the steep private road to—death.
Twenty-two chains from Dimock’s, on the left and
on a bank three feet higher than the road, is a
gorse bush. Here the murderer or murderers
crouched. Hawkins, on the near side of his horse,
must have passed this bush a yard, and either been
challenged or otherwise alarmed. He turned
three-quarters left-about face. A bullet struck
him under right top button of his sac coat, bruised
him only, and passed out of left lower pocket of
coat, carrying with it a penknife, subsequently
found opposite the gorse bush. Then, doubtless,
Hawkings ran down hill, and his pursuer, at close
range, fired (with No. 4 shot) and hit Hawkings
in the back below the right centre of the coat-
collar, and then, with a knife, hacked Hawkings
(who, exclusive of several incised wounds in the
arms, had 24 wounds on the body) until he fell.
He only ran 44 feet. As Hawkings lay prone, the
murderer apparently lifted his left arm and plunged
a knife into his heart. The attack was delivered
with maniacal ferocity. Meanwhile the horse
dragged the cart 31 chains up the ascent (passing
scathless some terribly steep pinches), got through
a gate generally kept closed, and brought up—the
near wheel in the palings—a few chains from the
farm.
* * * *
Though Hawkings seldom arrived home after 6
p.m., no anxiety was felt until 7.50, when Mrs.
Hawkings told Charles Bowles, her cousin, a farm
laborer, to go down-hill and look for Hawkings.
Bowles went with a lantern, found the horse and
cart, gave them to a farm-boy (Harry Norman)
and proceeded down-hill. Two chains below the
gorse-bush, 29 chains from the farm, he found
Hawkings—dead. He sent a passing youth to
alarm the Dimocks, who, with others, came to the
body. A telephonic message brought Dr. Cahill
from Wellington, who, after superficial examina-
tion, instructed the police to take the body to
Wellington morgue. About 11 p.m. Cahill' in-
formed the sergeant-major of police that there
had been foul play; no further action was taken
until 6 a.m. next day, when two constables went
to Kaiwarra. The Inspector and the Chief De-
tective were not on the scene of the murder until
20 hours after it occurred. On Monday, J une 3rd,
Inspector Thomson (since dead) called the widow
at the inquest to testify that deceased and Chemis
had a law-suit, and that the former told her
Chemis had threatened him. That, probably,
settled the doom of the foreigner Chemis. On
Tuesday, June 4th, a hysterical Wellington even-
ing paper (since dead) shrieked for the arrest of
Chemis. Thus encouraged, the police arrested
him next day.
* * * *
On the previous Saturday the police, informed
by Mrs. Hawkings of Chemis’s alleged threats,
formed the inevitable police theory, and, as in-
evitably, proceeded to fit Chemis into it. In-
spector Thomson picked up bits of newspapers near
the scene of the murder. Other police did like-
wise. Two detectives searched the suspect’s bed-
room while the Inspector remained without. They
found a double-barrelled gun, shot-pouch, revolver
loaded in three chambers, a small knife, a stiletto,
four bullets and pieces of newspaper, but no traces
of blood on Chemis, his garments or premises.
Chemis, when arrested, was perfectly composed,
and remained so, without fear or bravado, until
sentenced. He was taken before three J’sP. (two
of whom died before the trial) and remanded.
The coroner’s jury returned a verdict of murder
against “ some person unknown, ” and the magis-
trates committed Chemis for trial.
* * * *
His trial, before the Chief Justice, lasted from
Bth to 15th July; he was defended by Charles
Bunny, who suffered from typhoid throughout the
trial, delivered his speech for the defence on 13th
July, and died on 17th idem, and never knew the
verdict. The Crown Prosecutor’s case may be
thus summarised: There was enmity between
Chemis and deceased and a lawsuit over a lease.
The former had threatened the latter. From
Chemis’s house, over the hills, to the place of
murder was only 44 chains. Chemis must have
seen Hawkings pass in the morning, and have
known, when he (Chemis) knocked off work on the
high-road, Hawkings had not returned. Two shots
had been heard from direction of Hawkings’s
house at 5.30 p.m. There was time for Chemis to
have committed the murder, and it was suspicious
he had not called his children (aged 6 and 9) to
account for his whereabouts at 5.30 on the evening
of the murder. Chemis possessed a stiletto which
fitted (?) the wounds, also a double-barrelled gun.
But the shot which killed Hawkings had
been wadded with newspaper. Dr. Cahill
had found newspaper in the awful shot-
wound. The inspector and other police found
newspaper at the scene of the murder, also news-
paper in Chemis’s bed-room. These various scraps
had been pieced by a “ gentleman unconnected
with the police”— i.c. , a clerk in the police depart-
ment ! They belonged to the same issue of Even-
ing Post, and to same shipping-column thereof.
A piece found at place of murder fitted marvel-
lously into the piece said to have been extracted
by Dr. Cahill from the shot-wound, and these
pieces precisely fitted a piece of newspaper found in
prisoner’s house. On this paper evidence, and on
it alone, Chemis was sentenced to death. If that
fails —the rest goes by the board.
V ¥ * •
As a matter of fact, Chemis had nothing to fear
from the lawsuit with Hawkings. There was
much discrepancy of evidence touching the time
Chemis knocked off work on May 31st, and no
clear proof he could have got to the scene of
murder, across terribly difficult hills, by 5.30 p.m.
He would have been mad to call his young chil-
dren, who might not have seen their father that
evening, but he reasonably, as did his wife,
accounted for his time. In the opinion of some
experts, the stiletto, which bore no signs of recent
usage, could not have inflicted the wounds.
There was contradictory evidence as to whether
one or both barrels of the gun had been recently
fired. As regards the paper evidence, it failed,
not because it was not good enough, but because
it was far too good. When the inspector started
picking up newspaper, he had, he said, no theory,
and knew nothing about any gunshot wound on
deceased. He had put the paper found on the
ground in an envelope, and in his pocket. Some
of the paper found at Chemis’s house he put in an
envelope, and in another pocket, and marked the
lot in the evening, at home, and placed them back
in his coat, “in the pockets I had taken them
from,” and left the coat hanging up. On Sunday
morning he took out the papers from one envelope
for examination, but soon returned them. He
took the papers on the sth to the police-clerk,
putting “ each in its turn into a clean envelope.”
The fatal objections to the inspector’s evidence are
—(1) he may have honestly mixed the papers, and
(2) others had access to his pockets. Dr. Cahill,
probing the shot-wound in Hawkings, extracted
“amass of flesh,” and put it in a piece of news-
paper, “ the name and date of which I do not know.”
He carried this flesh home on June Ist, produced it
at the inquest (J une 3), took it home again ‘ ‘ -with-
out changing the wrapping, ” and dissected it 6th
June. When, on the latter date, he opened the
parcel, the whole of the newspaper wrapping did
not come away. The “ mass,” when taken from
the body, consisted of particles of clothing, flesh,
blood-clots, paper, and shot, the whole saturated
with blood. The mass gravitated to and wetted
the newspaper-wrapping—the whole of the wrap-
ping, the doctor stated, came away, except a
piece about the size of a shilling or half-crown, of
unknown name and date, which remained on the
mass. Here, it is evident, a mistake was possible.
* * * *
But there is a far simpler and more fatal objec-
tion to the paper evidence. Chemis, it was
proved, was in the habit of taking his lunch,
wrapped in newspaper, and eating it in the
locality of the scene of the murder —part of a
paper unconnected with the crime was found by
the police near the place of murder, and another
part, fitting the first, in Chemis’s house. Chemis
may, therefore, have taken his lunch in a piece of
the identical newspaper which sentenced him,
leaving a torn portion thereof at home, may have
thrown away the piece enclosing his lunch, and
the murderer may have picked it up and used it
as a gun-wad.
* * * *
The following additional facts tell in favor of
Chemis: He bore an excellent character for so-
briety and industry. He had saved life ;he was
a good husband and father—his wife, an Irish-
woman, fought for him long after his sentence
with surpassing courage. At about 5 o’clock on
the evening of the murder a man (never identified)
carrying a gun, was seen near Hawkings’s land’
After the trial, when Mrs. Chemis brought ari
unsuccessful charge of perjury against the police
a man made an affidavit that he heard, on the
night of the murder and near the scene thereof a
rustling as of someone moving in the scrub ; and
another swore at the trial of Chemis that he saw a
man, a stranger, running up the road near the
place of the murder very early on the following
morning (June Ist). Three youths made affidavit
that on May 24th a strange man, under the influ-
ence of liquor, foaming with rage, in possession of
a gun and acquainted with the locality, had a
violent quarrel with Hawkings. This man was
further described as sft. 9in. high, stout build a
foreigner and wearing a heavy, sandy moustache.
Long after the trial the present writer noticed that
a portion of the sleeve of the coat Hawkings was
killed in was stuck together with congealed blood.
It was opened and examined with a microscope!
In the part opened, stuck to the sleeve by blood!
were found three hairs—apparently from a sandy
moustache !
* * * *
Returning now to the prisoner in Darlinghurst,
who would have been 15 years of age when Hawk-
ings was killed. It was common talk in Welling-
ton after the murder that a boy Hawkings had
once employed had sworn on leaving he would pay
Hawkings out, when he grew big enough, for
some real or imaginary injury Hawkings had
inflicted upon him. Thousands in Maoriland
believe Chemis innocent. It is to be hoped these
lines may be the means of inducing the friends of
Chemis to have the confession of the Darlinghurst
confinee thoroughly sifted. Two may have assisted
at the murder. It is too much to ask the Maori-
land police to essay to prove they bungled over
the Kaiwarra Mystery.
J. EVISON.
